Division and Classification
beginner
10 min
The Three Types of Indication: Intellectual, Natural, and Conventional
Author defines three types of indication with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intellectual, natural, and conventional indication and provide an example of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Indication is either rational, empirical, or conventional.

The author of the text classifies all indication into three fundamental types. Understanding these types is essential because they determine how we derive knowledge from signs – whether in theology, jurisprudence, or daily reasoning. This lesson will walk you through the defining features and examples of each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types from the text
Locate in the Arabic where the author enumerates: عقلية, طبيعية, وضعية. Each is introduced with 'إما أن تكون'.
2
Analyze the basis of each type
For intellectual: rational necessity; natural: empirical regularity (God's system); conventional: human agreement. Note the difference in necessity.
Natural indication can fail; intellectual cannot.
3
Extract examples for each type
From the text: intellectual: effect-cause; natural: fever-illness; conventional: traffic signals. Write them down with their type.
4
Apply the classification to a new Islamic example
Consider the indication of the Quran's eloquence (i'jāz) for its divine origin. Is it intellectual, natural, or conventional? Argue based on the definitions.
This is a classical kalām argument often considered intellectual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدّلالات وأقسَامُها
دلالة شيء على شيء آخر لا بد أن تكون واحدة من الدلالات الثلاث التالية :
أولاً : فهي إما أن تكون دلالة عقلية بحتة، كدلالة الأثر على المؤثر، وكدلالة الحركة بالإرادة على وجود الحياة، وكدلالة حركة اليد على حركة الخاتم الموجود في إصبع من أصابعها، وكدلالة مسير السفينة في البحر على تحرك ركابها وفق حركتها، وهكذا كل ملزوم إذ يدلُّ على لازمه العقلي البحت .
ثانياً : وإما أن تكون دلالة طبيعية، وهي الدلالة التي ليس بين الملزوم واللازم فيها ارتباط عقلي، إلا أن النظام الذي وضعه الله في الطبيعة قد أوجد هذا الترابط، فإذا سألنا العقل المجرد عن ملاحظة النظام الموجود في الطبيعة لم يجد تعليلاً عقلياً له؛ غير أن الاختبار المتكرر للأحداث الطبيعية قد نبه على وجود هذا الترابط في الواقع، ولكن ليس لدى العقل المجرد مانع من انفكاكه لو ثبت ذلك في الواقع ولو نادراً .
مثال ذلك : دلالة ارتفاع درجة حرارة جسم الإنسان على حالةٍ من حالات المرض، ودلالة التقيؤ والإسهال الشديدين على الإصابة بمرض الهيضة (الكوليرا)، ودلالة حمرة الوجه على حالة الخجل في النفس، ودلالة صفرة الوجه على حالة الوجل في النفس، ودلالة الكلام على حياة الإنسان الذي يصدر عنه هذا الكلام، ودلالة كثرة الأمطار على السنة المخصبة، ودلالة شحها على السنة المجدبة؛ فهذه وأشباهها دلالات طبيعية، لا يمنع العقل المجرد عن ملاحظة الواقع من تخلفها وعدم صدقها .
ثالثاً : وإما أن تكون دلالة وضعية، وهي دلالة شيء ما تواضع الناس في اصطلاحهم على أن يكون دالاً على معنى معين، وقد يكون هذا الشيء مَعْلماً من المعالم، أو رسماً من الرسوم، أو لفظاً من الألفاظ .
مثال ذلك : دلالات إشارات المرور، ودلالات الرسوم التي توضع على لوحات لإرشاد سائقي السيارات، ودلالة صورة السهم «←» على تحديد الاتجاه، ودلالة رنين الجرس في المدرسة على بداية الدرس أو نهايته، فهذه وما أشبهها دلالات وضعية غير لفظية، أما الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية فهي دلالة الألفاظ على المعاني بوساطة الوضع اللغوي، سواء كانت دلالة اللفظ على المعنى واردة على سبيل الحقيقة أو على سبيل المجاز، كدلالة خفض الجناح على معنى التواضع، وهذه الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية هي المقصودة في هذا الفن وأقسامها ثلاثة :
أقسام الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية :
تنقسم الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية إلى ثلاثة أقسام كما ذكرنا :
وذلك لأن الكلام إما أن يُساق ليدل على تمام معناه، وإما أن يُساق ليدل على بعض معناه، وإما أن يُساق ليدل على معنى آخر خارج عن معناه، إلا أنه لازم له عقلاً أو عرفاً، فهذه هي الوجوه التي تشتمل على الأقسام الثلاثة للدلالة الوضعية اللفظية، ولكل قسم منها اسم اصطلاحي :
أ ـ فدلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي (مطابقة) .
ب ـ ودلالة اللفظ على بعض معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي (تضمن) .
جـ ـ ودلالة اللفظ على معنى آخر خارج عن معناه لازم له عقلاً أو عرفاً (التزام) .
Indications and their divisions. The indication of one thing for another must be one of the following three types: First: it is either a purely intellectual indication, like the indication of the effect for the cause, and like the indication of voluntary motion for the existence of life, and like the indication of the movement of the hand for the movement of the ring on its finger, and like the indication of the movement of a ship in the sea for the movement of its passengers according to its motion, and so on every necessary thing (malzum) indicates its purely intellectual necessary consequence (lazim). Second: or it is a natural indication, which is the indication where there is no intellectual connection between the malzum and the lazim, except that the system which God has placed in nature has created this connection. If we ask the abstract intellect to observe the system existing in nature, it finds no intellectual explanation for it; however, repeated testing of natural events has alerted to the existence of this connection in reality, but the abstract intellect has no objection to its disconnection if that were to occur in reality, even if rarely. Example of that: the indication of a rise in temperature of the human body for a state of illness, and the indication of severe vomiting and diarrhea for infection with the disease of cholera, and the indication of redness of the face for the state of shyness in the soul, and the indication of paleness of the face for the state of fear in the soul, and the indication of speech for the life of the person from whom this speech emanates, and the indication of abundant rain for a fertile year, and the indication of its scarcity for a barren year. These and their like are natural indications; the abstract intellect observing reality does not prevent their failure and lack of truth. Third: or it is a conventional indication, which is the indication of something that people have conventionally agreed to be indicative of a specific meaning, and this thing may be a landmark, a drawing, or a word. Example of that: the indications of traffic signals, and the indications of drawings placed on signs to guide car drivers, and the indication of the arrow image «←» for determining direction, and the indication of the ringing of a bell in school for the beginning or end of a lesson. These and their like are non-verbal conventional indications. As for verbal conventional indication, it is the indication of words for meanings by means of linguistic convention, whether the indication of the word for the meaning occurs in the way of literal truth or metaphor, like the indication of 'lowering the wing' for the meaning of humility. This verbal conventional indication is what is intended in this art, and its divisions are three: The divisions of verbal conventional indication: The verbal conventional indication is divided into three divisions as we mentioned. That is because speech is either presented to indicate its complete meaning, or to indicate part of its meaning, or to indicate another meaning external to its meaning but necessary for it intellectually or conventionally. These are the aspects that include the three divisions of verbal conventional indication, and each division has a technical name: A) The indication of a word for its complete literal or metaphorical meaning is (correspondence). B) The indication of a word for part of its literal or metaphorical meaning is (inclusion). C) The indication of a word for another meaning external to its meaning but necessary for it intellectually or conventionally is (implication).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of indication (دلالة). The author begins by stating that any indication of one thing for another must be one of three types. First, intellectual indication (دلالة عقلية) is a purely rational connection where the mind necessarily infers the lazim (necessary consequence) from the malzum (that which necessitates). Examples include inferring the existence of a cause from its effect, or inferring life from voluntary motion. Second, natural indication (دلالة طبيعية) involves a connection established by God in nature, not by rational necessity. The mind does not see a necessary link, but repeated observation confirms the correlation. Examples include fever indicating illness, or paleness indicating fear; such indications can fail occasionally. Third, conventional indication (دلالة وضعية) is based on human agreement. It is divided into non-verbal (e.g., traffic signals) and verbal. The verbal conventional indication is the focus of logic. The author then subdivides verbal conventional indication into three: مطابقة (correspondence), where the word indicates its full meaning (e.g., the word 'house' indicating the entire house); تضمن (inclusion), where the word indicates part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' indicating the roof); and التزام (implication), where the word indicates something external to its meaning but necessarily associated (e.g., 'house' indicating the builder). This classification is fundamental to understanding how words convey meanings in Arabic logic and rhetoric.

Key Terms
دلالة
دلالة عقلية
دلالة طبيعية
دلالة وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of indication is exemplified by the correlation between redness of the face and shyness?
A) Intellectual indication
B) Natural indication
C) Conventional verbal indication
D) Conventional non-verbal indication

Key Takeaway
Indication is either intellectual (rational necessity), natural (empirical regularity), or conventional (human agreement).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the Quranic verse 'وَمِنْ آيَاتِهِ خَلْقُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ' (And of His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth), classify the indication of the creation for the existence of the Creator as intellectual, natural, or conventional. Explain why.
?
Identify the type of indication in the following: the ringing of the mosque minaret indicates the time for prayer.

Division and Classification
intermediate
10 min
The Three Modes of Verbal Conventional Indication: Correspondence, Inclusion, and Implication
Author subdivides verbal conventional indication into three modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modes of verbal conventional indication (muṭābaqa, taḍammun, iltizām) and apply them to Arabic terms from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A word indicates its full meaning, a part of it, or something necessary to it.

Having established that verbal conventional indication is the focus of this art, the author now subdivides it into three modes based on the relationship between the word and the meaning. This classification is crucial for precise interpretation of legal and theological texts. This lesson will help you master these three modes.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three modes in the Arabic text
Find where the author says 'أقسام الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية' and then lists: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام. Note the order.
2
Understand the basis for each mode
Correspondence (muṭābaqa): word indicates its complete meaning. Inclusion (taḍammun): word indicates a part of its meaning. Implication (iltizām): word indicates something external but necessary to its meaning.
Iltizām can be intellectual (عقلي) or conventional (عرفي).
3
Analyze the examples given
The text gives 'house' as an example. For 'house': muṭābaqa = entire building, taḍammun = roof, iltizām = builder. Write similar examples for an Islamic term like 'masjid'.
4
Apply to a legal term from Usul al-Fiqh
Take the word 'ṣalāh'. Its muṭābaqa is the entire ritual prayer. Its taḍammun includes specific acts like rukū'. Its iltizām includes the prohibition of indecency (as mentioned in Quran 29:45).
This shows how jurists use these modes to derive rulings.

ثالثاً : وإما أن تكون دلالة وضعية، وهي دلالة شيء ما تواضع الناس في اصطلاحهم على أن يكون دالاً على معنى معين، وقد يكون هذا الشيء مَعْلماً من المعالم، أو رسماً من الرسوم، أو لفظاً من الألفاظ .

تنقسم الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية إلى ثلاثة أقسام كما ذكرنا :

جـ ـ ودلالة اللفظ على معنى آخر خارج عن معناه لازم له عقلاً أو عرفاً (التزام) .
Indications and their divisions. The indication of one thing for another must be one of the following three types: First: it is either a purely intellectual indication, like the indication of the effect for the cause, and like the indication of voluntary motion for the existence of life, and like the indication of the movement of the hand for the movement of the ring on its finger, and like the indication of the movement of a ship in the sea for the movement of its passengers according to its motion, and so on every necessary thing (malzum) indicates its purely intellectual necessary consequence (lazim). Second: or it is a natural indication, which is the indication where there is no intellectual connection between the malzum and the lazim, except that the system which God has placed in nature has created this connection. If we ask the abstract intellect to observe the system existing in nature, it finds no intellectual explanation for it; however, repeated testing of natural events has alerted to the existence of this connection in reality, but the abstract intellect has no objection to its disconnection if that were to occur in reality, even if rarely. Example of that: the indication of a rise in temperature of the human body for a state of illness, and the indication of severe vomiting and diarrhea for infection with the disease of cholera, and the indication of redness of the face for the state of shyness in the soul, and the indication of paleness of the face for the state of fear in the soul, and the indication of speech for the life of the person from whom this speech emanates, and the indication of abundant rain for a fertile year, and the indication of its scarcity for a barren year. These and their like are natural indications; the abstract intellect observing reality does not prevent their failure and lack of truth. Third: or it is a conventional indication, which is the indication of something that people have conventionally agreed to be indicative of a specific meaning, and this thing may be a landmark, a drawing, or a word. Example of that: the indications of traffic signals, and the indications of drawings placed on signs to guide car drivers, and the indication of the arrow image «←» for determining direction, and the indication of the ringing of a bell in school for the beginning or end of a lesson. These and their like are non-verbal conventional indications. As for verbal conventional indication, it is the indication of words for meanings by means of linguistic convention, whether the indication of the word for the meaning occurs in the way of literal truth or metaphor, like the indication of 'lowering the wing' for the meaning of humility. This verbal conventional indication is what is intended in this art, and its divisions are three: The divisions of verbal conventional indication: The verbal conventional indication is divided into three divisions as we mentioned. That is because speech is either presented to indicate its complete meaning, or to indicate part of its meaning, or to indicate another meaning external to its meaning but necessary for it intellectually or conventionally. These are the aspects that include the three divisions of verbal conventional indication, and each division has a technical name: A) The indication of a word for its complete literal or metaphorical meaning is (correspondence). B) The indication of a word for part of its literal or metaphorical meaning is (inclusion). C) The indication of a word for another meaning external to its meaning but necessary for it intellectually or conventionally is (implication).

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of indication (دلالة). The author begins by stating that any indication of one thing for another must be one of three types. First, intellectual indication (دلالة عقلية) is a purely rational connection where the mind necessarily infers the lazim (necessary consequence) from the malzum (that which necessitates). Examples include inferring the existence of a cause from its effect, or inferring life from voluntary motion. Second, natural indication (دلالة طبيعية) involves a connection established by God in nature, not by rational necessity. The mind does not see a necessary link, but repeated observation confirms the correlation. Examples include fever indicating illness, or paleness indicating fear; such indications can fail occasionally. Third, conventional indication (دلالة وضعية) is based on human agreement. It is divided into non-verbal (e.g., traffic signals) and verbal. The verbal conventional indication is the focus of logic. The author then subdivides verbal conventional indication into three: مطابقة (correspondence), where the word indicates its full meaning (e.g., the word 'house' indicating the entire house); تضمن (inclusion), where the word indicates part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' indicating the roof); and التزام (implication), where the word indicates something external to its meaning but necessarily associated (e.g., 'house' indicating the builder). This classification is fundamental to understanding how words convey meanings in Arabic logic and rhetoric.

Division and Classification
beginner

The Tree of Indication: A Memorisation Summary
Hierarchical diagram classifying indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the hierarchical tree of indication and correctly classify any example of indication into its proper type.

Core Idea
All indication is rational, conventional, or natural; conventional verbal indication has three types.

This lesson presents the classification tree of indication (dalāla) as a summary for memorization. By mastering this tree, you will be able to locate any indication under its proper heading and understand the relationships between types. This is essential for mantiq and usul al-fiqh, where the type of indication determines the strength of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary types
Look at the top level of the tree. Indication is divided into three primary types: pure rational (ʿaqliyya), conventional (waḍʿiyya), and natural (ṭabīʿiyya). Memorise their order.
Rational indication is based on reason alone, conventional on human agreement, natural on inherent connection.
2
Notice that only conventional indication branches
Conventional indication is further divided into verbal (lafẓiyya) and non-verbal (ghayr lafẓiyya). The other two types do not have sub-divisions in this tree.
Non-verbal includes gestures, signals, etc.
3
Memorise the three verbal sub-types
Verbal conventional indication splits into conformity (muṭābaqiyya), inclusion (taḍamuniyya), and concomitance (iltizāmiyya). These describe the relation between word and meaning.
Conformity: whole meaning; inclusion: part; concomitance: external necessary connection.
4
Note the further division of concomitance
Concomitance itself divides into rational (ʿaqliyya) and customary (ʿurfiyya). This is the lowest level of the tree.
Rational: necessary by reason; customary: by common convention.
5
Test yourself with examples
Take an example like 'smoke indicates fire' and trace it through the tree. It is pure rational. 'The word 'house' indicates a door'? Inclusion. Practice classifying various indications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصة تقسيم الدلالات :
الدلالة
(١) (٢) (٣)
عقلية بحتة وضعية طبيعية
↓
لفظية غير لفظية
↓
مطابقية تضمنية التزامية
↓
عقلية عرفية
Summary of division of indications:
Indication
(1) (2) (3)
Pure rational Conventional Natural
↓
Verbal Non-verbal
↓
Concomitant Inclusive Concomitant
(actually: مطابقية = conformity, تضمنية = inclusion, التزامية = concomitant)
↓
Rational Customary
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a visual summary tree (shajara) of the divisions of indication (dalāla). The author organizes the types hierarchically:

First level: Indication is divided into three primary types:
1. Pure rational indication (ʿaqliyya baḥta): based on reason alone (e.g., smoke indicates fire).
2. Conventional indication (waḍʿiyya): based on human convention (e.g., words for meanings).
3. Natural indication (ṭabīʿiyya): based on natural connection (e.g., a cry indicates pain).

Second level: Under conventional indication, it branches into:
- Verbal (lafẓiyya): using words.
- Non-verbal (ghayr lafẓiyya): using gestures, signs, etc.

Third level: Verbal conventional indication is further divided into three types based on the relation between word and meaning:
- Conformity (muṭābaqiyya): the word indicates the whole meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates the entire building).
- Inclusion (taḍamuniyya): the word indicates part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates a room).
- Concomitance (iltizāmiyya): the word indicates something external but necessarily connected (e.g., 'roof' implies a wall).

Fourth level: Concomitant indication is then subdivided into:
- Rational (ʿaqliyya): necessary connection by reason (e.g., four implies evenness).
- Customary (ʿurfiyya): connection by custom (e.g., ashes imply generosity as earlier example).

The diagram is meant to be memorized as a quick reference to the classification system of indication.

Key Terms
دلالة عقلية
دلالة وضعية
دلالة مطابقية
دلالة تضمنية
دلالة التزامية

Distinguishing Whole and Universal, Part and Particular
Author clarifies terminological distinction between whole/part and universal/particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the whole (kull) and the universal (kulli) and between a part (juz') and a particular (juz'i) when analyzing logical and ontological relationships.

Core Idea
A universal is predicated of its particulars; a whole is not predicated of its parts.

In logic and ontology, terms like 'whole' and 'universal' are sometimes confused. This lesson clarifies the crucial difference: a universal concept applies to each of its individuals, while a whole is composed of parts but its name does not apply to any part alone. Understanding this distinction prevents category errors in definitions and classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term: Whole or Universal?
Determine whether the term in question is a concept that can be predicated of many individuals (universal) or a composite entity made of parts (whole). For example, 'Human' is a universal; 'House' is a whole.
Check if the term's name applies to each individual instance. If it does, it is likely a universal.
2
Check predication across instances
For a universal, the name applies to each particular (e.g., Khalid is called 'human'). For a whole, the name does not apply to any part alone (e.g., a wall is not called 'house').
Test with examples: Can you say 'this is a human' for each individual? Yes. Can you say 'this is a house' for a wall? No.
3
Examine the relationship: Parts vs. Particulars
A universal's particulars are individuals that fall under it and share its name. A whole's parts are components that together form the whole but do not individually carry the whole's name.
The whole is the sum of its parts, but the universal is not a sum of its particulars—it is their common predicate.
4
Apply with Islamic examples
Practice identifying whether a given term is a whole or a universal using examples from Islamic sciences: e.g., 'Salah' as a universal (applies to Fajr, Dhuhr, etc.) vs. 'Salah' as a whole (composed of qiyam, ruku', sujud). This distinction is important in fiqh definitions.
A universal can also be a whole conceptually, but the relationship with its instances differs from a whole's relationship with its parts.

A note on the difference between whole (kull) and universal (kulli) and between part (juz') and particular (juz'i): It is good for us to point out that there is a terminological difference between whole and universal and between part and particular. The universal, as we have seen, is a concept that applies to individuals, and every individual of these individuals is a particular for this universal, and every particular is called by the name of the universal. For example, Khalid is a particular, and he is called by the name 'Human' which is a universal for him, and Sa'id, Adnan, and the rest of the individuals are similarly. As for the whole, it has parts, not particulars. These parts assembled in their composite form are called the whole, and it is not correct to apply the name of the whole to a single part of its parts alone. An example: 'House' is a whole by virtue of its concept including its parts: walls, roof, and door. It is known that the name 'house' is not applied to one of these parts like the wall alone or the roof alone. So if...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification distinguishing between two pairs: kull (whole) vs. kulli (universal), and juz' (part) vs. juz'i (particular). The author warns that these terms are often confused. The universal (kulli) is a concept that applies to many individuals (afrad); each individual is a particular (juz'i) of that universal. For example, the universal 'Human' applies to individuals like Khalid, Sa'id, Adnan — each is a particular. Importantly, the particular is called by the name of the universal (i.e., we call Khalid a human). In contrast, a whole (kull) is composed of parts (ajza'), not individuals. The parts come together to form the whole, but the name of the whole is not applied to any single part alone. For instance, a house is a whole consisting of walls, roof, and door; we do not call the wall alone a 'house'. The passage emphasizes that while a universal and its particulars share a name (the universal's name applies to each particular), a whole and its parts do not share the name in that way. This distinction is crucial in logic and ontology.

Particular and Universal: The Foundational Division
Author defines particular and universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether any given concept is particular or universal based on the criterion of prevention of sharing.

Core Idea
A concept's universality depends on logical possibility, not actual multiplicity.

The first division in logic of concepts is the split between particular and universal. This passage defines these terms precisely: 'particular' means the concept itself bars multiple individuals; 'universal' means it does not. Understand this distinction well, because everything else in logic—definitions, syllogisms, arguments—rests on it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
In the Arabic, find the two sentences defining 'جزئي' and 'كلي'. Read them aloud.
2
Analyze the criterion
The key phrase is 'يمنع من وقوع الشركة فيه' (prevents sharing). Notice that this is a property of the concept itself, not an external accident.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
The author gives examples later: think of 'شريك الباري' (partner of Allah) which cannot be shared, vs 'الإنسان' (human) which can. Check if your own examples fit.
4
Check comprehension
Is 'الشمس' (sun) particular or universal? According to the author, it is a universal because the concept 'sun' does not prevent sharing, even though only one individual exists. This illustrates that the definition is about logical possibility, not actual existence.

جزئي كلي
وهو مفرد يمنع تصور مفهومه وهو مفرد لا يمنع تصور مفهومه
من وقوع الشركة فيه من وقوع الشركة فيه وهو

يستحيل وجود يمكن وجود يمكن وجود يستحيل في العقل يمكن وجود يمكن وجود

شريك الباري لم يوجد أي فرد لم يوجد منها فرد واحد له نحو: متناهية ولكن متناهية وقد

ولفظ (العدد)
Simple conceptual terms are divided into (1) particular and (2) universal. The particular is a term whose concept prevents sharing in it. The universal is a term whose concept does not prevent sharing in it. It has six divisions: (1) impossible of existence of any individual thereof, such as partner of Allah exalted; (2) possible of existence but no individual has existed in reality, such as 'anqa'; (3) possible of existence but only one individual has existed in reality, such as the sun; (4) impossible in the mind but of existence more than one, such as necessary existence; (5) possible of existence with non-finite individuals but none has existed except a finite number, such as humans and stars; (6) possible of existence with non-finite individuals and they have existed in reality, such as the number (and the word 'number').
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage. It divides simple conceptual terms (مفردات تصورية) into two main categories: particular (جزئي) and universal (كلي). A particular is defined as a term whose very concept prevents multiple individuals from sharing it; that is, it can only refer to one thing, like 'Allah' (unique). A universal is defined as a term whose concept allows multiple individuals to share it, like 'human'. The author then lists six types of universal based on the mode of existence of its individuals in reality. Type 1: impossible existence — no individual can exist, e.g., a partner of Allah. Type 2: possible existence but none has existed in reality, e.g., the mythical 'anqa. Type 3: possible existence but only one individual has existed, e.g., the sun. Type 4: impossible in the mind (i.e., mentally inconceivable?) but has more than one individual? This is confusing; the text says 'impossible in the mind' but gives example 'necessary existence' which is Allah. That seems contradictory. Perhaps it means 'impossible to be absent from the mind'? The OCR may have an error. Type 5: possible existence with infinite individuals but only a finite number have existed, e.g., humans and stars. Type 6: possible existence with infinite individuals and they have existed in reality, e.g., numbers. This classification is used to understand how universals relate to their instances.

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'i): A concept that cannot be shared by multiple individuals; its meaning prevents partnership in it. [Note: No significant shift; standard in logic.]
كلي (kulli): A concept that can be shared by multiple individuals; its meaning does not prevent partnership. [Note: No significant shift; standard in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the sun is not a concept but a physical object.
B) Because the concept 'sun' does not prevent sharing, even if only one individual exists.
C) Because the sun is a particular like Allah.
D) Because universals must have multiple individuals.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between particular and universal is based on whether the concept prevents sharing, not on actual existence.

The Six Types of Universal According to Existence
Author enumerates six types of universal based on existential status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal into one of six types based on the existential status of its individuals.

Core Idea
Universals are not all equal; their relationship to existence gives them different logical properties.

Once you know a concept is universal, the next question is: how do its individuals relate to existence? This passage provides a complete taxonomy of six types, ranging from impossible like a partner of Allah to fully realized like numbers. This classification helps in assessing the ontological commitments of your terms.

Guided Path
1
Review the six types
List them in order: (1) impossible existence, (2) possible but none actual, (3) possible but only one actual, (4) impossible in mind? (note confusion), (5) possible with infinite but finite actual, (6) possible with infinite and infinite actual.
2
Understand the basis
The classification is built on two axes: logical possibility of existence and actual occurrence.
3
Memorize the examples
Each type has a classic example from Islamic thought: partner of Allah, 'anqa, sun, necessary existence, humans/stars, number.
4
Analyze the confusing type 4
The text says 'impossible in the mind but of existence more than one' with example 'necessary existence' (Allah). This is likely an error or special interpretation. Discuss that this might mean 'impossible to be absent from the mind' (i.e., necessary concept). For now, note it as problematic.
5
Apply the classification
Given a new universal, determine its type by checking logical possibility and actual existence.

Distinguishing Kull (Whole) from Kulli (Universal)
Author explains the difference between kull/kulli and juz'/juz'i with examples and implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a whole (kull) and a universal (kulli) in Islamic logical analysis, and apply judgments correctly to each.

Core Idea
A universal applies to each particular individually; a whole applies only when all parts are assembled.

In logic, it's easy to confuse 'whole' and 'universal' because both involve multiple things. However, they are fundamentally different: a universal is a concept that applies to each of its many particulars, while a whole is a composite whose parts together form it. This passage from the textbook clarifies the distinction with concrete examples, essential for precise thinking in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of terms
Recognize that the passage distinguishes between kull/kulli and juz'/juz'i. A whole (kull) is composed of parts (ajza'), while a universal (kulli) has particulars (juz'iyyat).
Note the Arabic terms: الكل (al-kull), الكلي (al-kulli), الجزء (al-juz'), الجزئي (al-juz'i).
2
Understand the composition of a whole
A whole's name applies only when all its parts are assembled together. For example, 'house' is a whole composed of walls, roof, and door; you cannot call a wall alone a house.
3
Understand the application of a universal
A universal applies to each of its particulars individually. For example, the universal 'human' applies to Khalid, Sa'id, and every other human. Each particular is called by the universal name.
4
Analyze the example of 'house'
Notice that the word 'house' functions both as a whole (considering its internal parts) and as a universal (applying to each individual house). This dual role shows how the same term can be viewed from different logical perspectives.
The author emphasizes that concepts differ according to different considerations.
5
Apply judgments correctly
A judgment on a universal (e.g., 'Humans are rational') is true for every particular individually. A judgment on a whole (e.g., 'Build a house') requires all parts to be present. Confusing the two leads to logical errors.
The builder example illustrates this: building only a wall does not satisfy the request for a house.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة حول الفرق بين الكل والكلي وبين الجزء والجزئي :
يحسن بنا أن ننبه على أنه يوجد فرق في الاصطلاح بين الكل والكلي
وبين الجزء والجزئي، فالكلي كما رأينا مفهوم ينطبق على أفراد، وكل فرد من
هذه الأفراد هو جزئي لهذا الكلي، وكل جزئي يطلق عليه اسم الكلي، فخالد
مثلاً جزئي، ويطلق عليه اسم (إنسان) الذي هو كلي له، وسعيد وعدنان
وسائر الأفراد كذلك. أما الكل فتحته أجزاء له لا جزئيات، وهذه الأجزاء
مجتمعة في هيئتها التركيبية يطلق عليها اسم الكل، ولا يصح إطلاق اسم
الكل على جزء من أجزائها وحده، مثال ذلك : (بيت) فهو كل باعتبار اشتمال
مفهومه على أجزاء له، هي الجدران والسقف والباب، ومعلوم أنه لا يطلق
اسم البيت على جزء من هذه الأجزاء كالجدار وحده أو السقف وحده، فإذا
اجتمعت الأجزاء في هيئتها التركيبية الخاصة سميت حينئذٍ بيتاً، وباستطاعتنا
أن نقول: إن (بيتاً) هو كلي، ولكن باعتبار أنه يطلق على كل فرد من أفراد
البيوت، وهذه الأفراد هي جزئيات له لا أجزاء، وقد علمنا أن اسم الكلي
يطلق على كل جزئي من جزئياته.
فإذا أصدرنا حكماً على الكلي تناول هذا الحكم كل جزئي من جزئياته
منفرداً، فإذا قلنا: الإنسان حيوان ناطق، صدق هذا الكلام بزيد وحده،
وخالد وحده وبكل جزئي آخر من جزئيات الإنسان، أي بكل فرد من أفراده،
وإذا أصدرنا حكماً على الكل لم يتناول الحكم كل جزء من الأجزاء وحده،
بل لا بد من اجتماع الأجزاء كلها حتى يتناولها الحكم، فإذا قلت لبناء: ابن
لي بيتاً، فإنه لا يفعل المطلوب إذا هو بنى جداراً أو رفع سقفاً لأن ذلك جزء
من العمل وليس كل العمل المطلوب، لكنه إذا بنى بيتاً كاملاً على أية صفة
فإنه حينئذ يكون قد حقق الطلب، لأنه يصدق اسم بيت باعتباره كلياً على أي
جزئي من جزئياته، ولا يصدق باعتباره كُلّاً على بعض أجزائه دون البعض الآخر.
بهذا البيان نلاحظ الفرق بين الكل والكلي، وبين الجزء والجزئي،
ونلاحظ أيضاً أن اللفظ الواحد قد يكون كُلّاً لأجزاء داخلة في مفهومه، وقد
يكون كلياً أيضاً باعتبار صحة إطلاقه على أي جزئي من جزئياتٍ متعدّدة،
وربما يكون جزئياً باعتبار كونه فرداً من أفراد كلي فوقه، وهو مع ذلك كُلّ
لأجزاء داخلة في تركيبه، فالمفاهيم تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات.
والخلاصة: أن الكلي تحته جزئيات وأن الكل تحته أجزاء، والحكم
على الكلي يصدق بأي جزئي من جزئياته، أما الحكم على الكل فلا يصدق
بجزء من أجزائه، بل لا بد من اجتماعها.
A note on the difference between the whole and the universal and between the part and the particular: It is fitting for us to point out that there is a terminological difference between the whole and the universal, and between the part and the particular. The universal, as we have seen, is a concept that applies to individuals, and every individual of these individuals is a particular for this universal, and every particular is called by the name of the universal. For example, Khalid is a particular, and he is called by the name 'human' which is a universal for him; likewise Sa'id, 'Adnan, and all other individuals. As for the whole, it has parts under it, not particulars. These parts, when combined in their compositional form, are called by the name of the whole, and it is not correct to apply the name of the whole to one of its parts alone. Example: 'house' – it is a whole by virtue of its concept comprising parts: walls, roof, and door. It is known that the name 'house' is not applied to a part of these parts, like a wall alone or a roof alone. When the parts are combined in their specific compositional form, then it is called a house. And we can say that 'house' is a universal, but by virtue of it applying to every individual of houses, and these individuals are particulars for it, not parts. And we have learned that the name of the universal applies to every particular of its particulars. So if we issue a judgment on the universal, this judgment covers every particular of its particulars individually. For if we say: 'Humans are rational animals,' this statement is true for Zayd alone, for Khalid alone, and for every other particular of the particulars of human, i.e., every individual of its individuals. And if we issue a judgment on the whole, the judgment does not cover every part of the parts alone; rather, all the parts must be assembled for the judgment to cover them. For if you say to a builder: 'Build me a house,' he does not fulfill the requirement if he builds a wall or raises a roof, because that is part of the work and not the whole required work. But if he builds a complete house in any form, then he has fulfilled the request, because the name 'house' as a universal applies to any particular of its particulars, while it does not apply as a whole to some of its parts without others. By this explanation we observe the difference between the whole and the universal, and between the part and the particular. We also observe that one word may be a whole for parts included in its concept, and may also be a universal by virtue of its valid application to any particular of multiple particulars; and it may be a particular by virtue of being an individual of a universal above it, while at the same time being a whole for parts within its composition. So concepts differ according to different considerations. In summary: the universal has particulars under it, and the whole has parts under it. The judgment on the universal is true for any particular of its particulars, whereas the judgment on the whole is not true for a part of its parts; rather, they must all be assembled.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note that clarifies the critical distinction between the terms 'kull' (whole) and 'kulli' (universal), and similarly between 'juz'' (part) and 'juz'i' (particular). The author begins by reminding us that a universal (kulli) is a concept that applies to many individuals, and each individual is a particular (juz'i) of that universal. For example, Khalid is a particular under the universal 'human'. The name of the universal can be applied to any of its particulars: Khalid is called 'human', Sa'id is called 'human'. In contrast, a whole (kull) has parts (ajza') under it, not particulars. The parts together form the whole. The author gives the example of 'house': it is a whole composed of parts like walls, roof, and door. You cannot call a single wall a 'house'. However, the word 'house' can also be considered a universal if we consider it applying to each individual house (e.g., this house, that house). So one word can function both as a whole (considering its internal composition) and as a universal (considering its application to many instances). The author then discusses the implications of judgments. A judgment on a universal applies to every particular individually: e.g., 'Humans are rational animals' is true for Zayd alone and for Khalid alone. A judgment on a whole does not apply to a single part but only to the combination of all parts: if you ask for a house, building only a wall does not fulfill the request. The summary reiterates: universal has particulars, whole has parts; a judgment on the universal holds for any particular, but a judgment on the whole requires all parts. This distinction is crucial in logic to avoid equivocation.

Key Terms
الكل
الكلي
الجزء
الجزئي

Division and Classification

Distinction between the Whole and the Universal
Author distinguishes whole/part from universal/particular with building example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'whole' and 'universal' and determine how a judgment applies to each.

Core Idea
A whole requires all parts together; a universal applies to each particular individually.

In Islamic logic, confusing the whole (al-kull) with the universal (al-kulli) leads to errors in reasoning about definitions and rulings. This passage clarifies the distinction using a concrete example and provides precise definitions. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying judgments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of concepts.
The passage distinguishes between whole/part (kull/juz') and universal/particular (kulli/juz'i). Note that these are different relationships: a whole is composed of parts, while a universal applies to many particulars.
A term can be both a whole and a universal in different respects.
2
Examine the building example.
The author uses 'house' as an example: as a whole, it requires all parts (walls, roof) to be present; as a universal, it applies to any individual house. See how the command 'build a house' fails if only a wall is built.
The same word can function as whole or universal depending on the context.
3
Contrast how judgments apply.
A judgment on a universal (e.g., 'man is rational') is true for each particular individually (Zayd, Khalid). A judgment on a whole (e.g., 'build a house') requires all parts to be present for the judgment to be true.
This is the central logical distinction.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example.
Consider the ruling 'Prayer is obligatory'. This is a universal judgment: it applies to each individual prayer (fajr, dhuhr, etc.) separately. In contrast, the statement 'The prayer consists of reciting al-Fatiha and bowing' describes the whole prayer, requiring all parts for the act to be complete.
Understanding this helps avoid confusion in fiqh rulings.

اجتمعت الأجزاء في هيئتها التركيبية الخاصة سميت حينئذٍ بيتاً، وباستطاعتنا أن نقول: إن (بيتاً) هو كلي، ولكن باعتبار أنه يطلق على كل فرد من أفراد البيوت، وهذه الأفراد هي جزئيات له لا أجزاء، وقد علمنا أن اسم الكلي يطلق على كل جزئي من جزئياته. فإذا أصدرنا حكماً على الكلي تناول هذا الحكم كل جزئي من جزئياته منفرداً، فإذا قلنا: الإنسان حيوان ناطق، صدق هذا الكلام بزيد وحده، وخالد وحده وبكل جزئي آخر من جزئيات الإنسان، أي بكل فرد من أفراده، وإذا أصدرنا حكماً على الكل لم يتناول الحكم كل جزء من الأجزاء وحده، بل لا بد من اجتماع الأجزاء كلها حتى يتناولها الحكم، فإذا قلت لبناء: ابن لي بيتاً، فإنه لا يفعل المطلوب إذا هو بنى جداراً أو رفع سقفاً لأن ذلك جزء من العمل وليس كل العمل المطلوب، لكنه إذا بنى بيتاً كاملاً على أية صفة فإنه حينئذ يكون قد حقق الطلب، لأنه يصدق اسم بيت باعتباره كلياً على أي جزئي من جزئياته، ولا يصدق باعتباره كُلّاً على بعض أجزائه دون البعض الآخر. بهذا البيان نلاحظ الفرق بين الكل والكلي، وبين الجزء والجزئي، ونلاحظ أيضاً أن اللفظ الواحد قد يكون كُلّاً لأجزاء داخلة في مفهومه، وقد يكون كلياً أيضاً باعتبار صحة إطلاقه على أي جزئي من جزئياتٍ متعدّدة، وربما يكون جزئياً باعتبار كونه فرداً من أفراد كلي فوقه، وهو مع ذلك كُلّ لأجزاء داخلة في تركيبه، فالمفاهيم تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات. والخلاصة: أن الكلي تحته جزئيات وأن الكل تحته أجزاء، والحكم على الكلي يصدق بأي جزئي من جزئياته، أما الحكم على الكل فلا يصدق بجزء من أجزائه، بل لا بد من اجتماعها. خلاصة تعريفات: ١ - الكل: ما تركب من جزأين فأكثر. ٢ - الكلي: ما لا يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. ٣ - الجزء: ما تركب منه ومن غيره كل. ٤ - الجزئي: ما كان معناه لا يقبل في الذهن الاشتراك.
When the parts are combined in their specific structural form, it is then called a house. And we can say that 'house' is a universal, but in the sense that it applies to every individual among houses, and these individuals are particulars for it, not parts. And we know that the name 'universal' applies to every particular of its particulars. So if we issue a judgment on the universal, that judgment covers each particular of its particulars individually. Thus if we say: 'Man is a rational animal', this statement is true of Zayd alone, and of Khalid alone, and of every other particular of man, i.e., every individual of its individuals. And if we issue a judgment on the whole, the judgment does not cover each part individually; rather, all the parts must be combined for the judgment to cover them. So if you say to a builder: 'Build me a house', he does not do what is required if he builds a wall or raises a roof, because that is part of the work, not all the required work. But if he builds a complete house in any form, then he has fulfilled the request, because the name 'house' as a universal applies to any particular of its particulars, but as a whole it does not apply to some of its parts without others. By this explanation we observe the difference between the whole and the universal, and between the part and the particular. We also observe that a single term may be a whole for parts included in its concept, and may also be a universal in the sense that it can be applied to any particular of multiple particulars, and perhaps it may be a particular in the sense that it is an individual of a universal above it, while being at the same time a whole for parts included in its composition. So concepts differ according to different considerations. The summary: the universal has particulars under it, and the whole has parts under it. The judgment on the universal is true of any particular of its particulars, whereas the judgment on the whole is not true of a part of its parts; rather, they must be combined. Summary of definitions: 1. The whole: that which is composed of two or more parts. 2. The universal: that whose mere conception does not prevent sharing in it. 3. The part: that from which, together with something else, a whole is composed. 4. The particular: that whose meaning does not admit of sharing in the mind.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the concepts of 'whole' (al-kull) and 'universal' (al-kulli), and 'part' (al-juz') and 'particular' (al-juz'i). It begins with an example of a house: when parts combine in a specific structure, the whole is called a house. The word 'house' can be considered a universal because it applies to every individual house (particulars), not to its parts. The key distinction is in how a judgment applies: a judgment on a universal applies to each particular individually (e.g., 'Man is a rational animal' is true of Zayd and Khalid separately), while a judgment on a whole does not apply to any single part alone—all parts must be present (e.g., commanding to build a house requires the complete structure, not just a wall). The author then generalizes: a single term can be a whole in one respect (having parts) and a universal in another (applicable to many particulars), and even a particular relative to a higher universal. The summary definitions are given: whole = composed of two or more parts; universal = concept that does not prevent multiple individuals sharing it; part = that which with another forms a whole; particular = meaning that does not admit of mental sharing.

Key Terms
الكل (al-kull): That which is composed of two or more parts; a judgment on the whole requires all parts together. [Note: In logic, 'kull' often overlaps with 'kulli'; this passage explicitly distinguishes them.]
الكلي (al-kulli): That whose mere concept does not prevent sharing (i.e., multiple individuals can instantiate it); a judgment applies to each particular separately.
الجزء (al-juz'): That from which, together with something else, a whole is composed. [Note: Part is distinguished from particular; 'juz'' refers to components, 'juz'i' refers to individuals under a universal.]
الجزئي (al-juz'i): That whose meaning does not admit of sharing in the mind (i.e., a unique individual).

Division and Classification
beginner
10 min
Three Relations of Concepts: Tabayun, Tasawi, Umum Mutlaq
Author defines the first three of four relations between concepts with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three relations between universal concepts—tabayun, tasawi, and umum mutlaq—by analyzing their intension and extension, and correctly classify pairs of Islamic terms under these relations.

Core Idea
Concepts relate by extension: totally separate, perfectly overlapping, or one containing the other.

In logic, concepts do not exist in isolation; they relate to each other based on their meanings (intension) and the individuals they refer to (extension). This lesson introduces the first three of four fundamental relations: tabayun (disparity), tasawi (equality), and umum mutlaq (absolute general and specific). By understanding these, you will be able to precisely characterize how terms like 'Muslim' and 'believer' or 'animal' and 'human' are connected—a skill essential for definition, classification, and argument analysis in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two dimensions: concept (intension) vs. extension
Every universal concept has a meaning (mafhūm) – what is understood by the term – and an extension (maṣdāq) – the set of individuals it applies to. To determine the relation between two concepts, you must consider both dimensions separately.
For example, 'human' means a rational animal (intension) and refers to all humans (extension).
2
Check for tabayun: zero overlap in extension and difference in intension
If two concepts have completely different intensions and no shared individuals in extension, they are in tabayun (disparity). Look for concepts that are mutually exclusive, like 'human' and 'horse'.
The passage gives the example: 'human, horse, eagle, snake' – all are disjoint.
3
Check for tasawi: different intensions but identical extension
If two concepts differ in meaning but apply to exactly the same set of individuals (without increase or decrease), they are in tasawi (equality). Verify that every individual of one is an individual of the other and vice versa.
Example: 'Muslim' (outward submission) and 'believer' (inward conviction) are co-extensive in reality, though different in concept.
4
Check for umum mutlaq: one extension contains the other
If one concept applies to all individuals of the other and more (i.e., its extension is a proper superset), the relation is umum mutlaq (absolute general and specific). Identify which is broader (a'amm) and which is narrower (akhass).
Use the circle analogy: the larger circle (e.g., 'animal') contains the smaller circle (e.g., 'human').
5
Apply the rule about opposites
For tasawi, the opposites (negations) also have tasawi. For umum mutlaq, the opposites also have umum mutlaq, but the general becomes specific and vice versa. This is useful when dealing with negative propositions.
Example: Not-believer and not-Muslim are equal; not-animal (e.g., non-living) is more specific than not-human.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون هذا التخالف في المفهوم وفي الماصدق في أكثر من معنيين، مثل: (إنسان وفرس ونسر وثعبان) فهذه متباينة، والنسبة بينها التباين.
النسبة الثانية: (التساوي) وهي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، إلا أنهما متحدان في الماصدق، فكل منهما ينطبق على جميع ما ينطبق عليه الآخر من أفراد.
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل كلمتي : (ضاحك بالقوة) و (ناطق بالقوة) فمعنى كل منهما مخالف لمعنى قرينه، إلا أن الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها كل منهما هي الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الآخر، دون زيادة ولا نقصان.
ب - ومثل كلمتي : (مسلم) و (مؤمن) فمفهوم (مسلم) هو المستسلم في الظاهر لما جاء به الرسول صلوات الله عليه، ومفهوم (مؤمن) هو المصدق في قلبه بالحق الذي جاء به الرسول ﷺ، فهما إذن مختلفان في المفهوم، إلا أن كل من هو في الحقيقة مؤمن هو وحده الذي يصح أن يطلق عليه في الحقيقة أنه مسلم، وكل من هو في الحقيقة مسلم هو وحده الذي يصح أن يطلق عليه أنه مؤمن، فهما إذن بهذا الاعتبار متحدان في الماصدق، وإن اختلفا في المفهوم، فالنسبة بينهما هي (التساوي)، وذلك لأن النسبة الملاحظة بين ماصدق هذا وماصدق هذا هي التساوي، أي: تطابق الأفراد تطابقاً تاماً.
ونلاحظ في هذا القسم أن النسبة بين نقيضي المتساويين هي التساوي أيضاً، فكل من ليس هو بمسلم في الحقيقة هو ليس بمؤمن.
النسبة الثالثة : (العموم والخصوص المطلق) وهي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، وذلك من جهة أن أحدهما ينطبق على كل ما ينطبق عليه الآخر من أفراد دون العكس، أي: والثاني ينطبق فقط على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها قرينه، ومثلهما كمثل دائرتين دائرة كبرى وفي ضمنها دائرة صغرى.
فأحدهما أعم مطلقاً من قرينه والآخر أخص مطلقاً ولذلك سميت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق.
الأمثلة :
أ ـ مثل كلمتي : (حيوان) و (إنسان) فالأول منهما معنى كلي ينطبق على كل أفراد الثاني منهما، فكل إنسان يقال له حيوان، ولكنه ينطبق أيضاً على أفراد لا ينطبق عليها إنسان، كالفرس والغزال والطير وهكذا.
والسبب في هذا أن دائرة الإنسان هي دائرة صغرى تقع ضمن دائرة الحيوان الكبرى، إذن فدائرة (حيوان) أعم مطلقاً من دائرة (إنسان) ودائرة (إنسان) أخص مطلقاً من دائرة (حيوان) فالنسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق .
ب ـ ومثل كلمتي : (ماشي) و (غزال)، فالأول منهما وهو (ماشي) أعم مطلقاً، لأنه ينطبق على كل أفراد الثاني منهما، وينطبق أيضاً على أفراد أخرى لا ينطبق عليها الثاني، أما (غزال) فينطبق فقط على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها (ماشي)، إذن فدائرة ماشي أعم مطلقاً من دائرة غزال ودائرة غزال أخص مطلقاً، فالنسبة بينهما هي العموم والخصوص المطلق.
ونلاحظ في هذا القسم أن النسبة بين نقيضي ما بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، هي أيضاً العموم والخصوص المطلق، إلا أن ما كان أعم مطلقاً يصير أخص مطلقاً، وما كان أخص مطلقاً يصير أعم مطلقاً.
This difference may be in concept and in extension in more than two meanings, such as: (human, horse, eagle, snake) — these are disparate, and the relation between them is disparity (tabayun).
The second relation: (equality) which is the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, except that they are united in extension, so each applies to all the individuals to which the other applies.
Examples:
A - Like the two terms: (potentially laughing) and (potentially speaking) — the meaning of each is different from the meaning of its counterpart, except that the individuals each applies to are the individuals to which the other applies, without increase or decrease.
B - And like the two terms: (Muslim) and (believer) — the concept of Muslim is the one who submits outwardly to what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) brought, and the concept of believer is the one who believes in his heart in the truth brought by the Messenger (ﷺ). So they are different in concept, except that everyone who is in reality a believer is alone the one to whom it is correct to apply 'Muslim' in reality, and everyone who is in reality a Muslim is alone the one to whom it is correct to apply 'believer'. So they are therefore, from this perspective, united in extension, even if they differ in concept. So the relation between them is equality, because the relation observed between the extension of this and the extension of that is equality, i.e., complete correspondence of individuals.
We note in this section that the relation between the opposites of two equal concepts is also equality, for everyone who is not in reality a Muslim is not a believer.
The third relation: (absolute general and specific) which is the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, in such a way that one applies to all the individuals to which the other applies without the reverse, i.e., the second applies only to some individuals to which its counterpart applies. Their likeness is like two circles: a large circle and within it a small circle.
 So one of them is absolutely more general than its counterpart and the other is absolutely more specific, and therefore the relation between them is called absolute general and specific.
Examples:
A - Like the two terms: (animal) and (human) — the first is a universal meaning that applies to all individuals of the second, so every human is called an animal, but it also applies to individuals to which human does not apply, such as horse, gazelle, bird, and so on.
The reason for this is that the circle of human is a small circle lying within the larger circle of animal. Therefore, the circle of animal is absolutely more general than the circle of human, and the circle of human is absolutely more specific than the circle of animal. So the relation between them is absolute general and specific.
B - And like the two terms: (walker) and (gazelle) — the first, which is walker, is absolutely more general, because it applies to all individuals of the second, and also applies to other individuals to which the second does not apply. As for gazelle, it applies only to some individuals to which walker applies. Therefore, the circle of walker is absolutely more general than the circle of gazelle, and the circle of gazelle is absolutely more specific. So the relation between them is absolute general and specific.
We note in this section that the relation between the opposites of what are in absolute general and specific is also absolute general and specific, except that what was absolutely more general becomes absolutely more specific, and what was absolutely more specific becomes absolutely more general.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines three of the four fundamental relations between universal concepts, based on their extension and intension. The author begins by stating that concepts can differ in both meaning (intension) and reference (extension) across more than two terms, giving the example of 'human, horse, eagle, snake' — these are mutually exclusive (mutabayina) with no overlapping individuals, and the relation is called tabayun (disparity).

The second relation is tasawi (equality). Here, two concepts differ in meaning but share exactly the same set of individuals. The author provides two examples: (a) 'potentially laughing' and 'potentially speaking' — both apply to the same individuals (humans) but have different definitions; (b) 'Muslim' and 'believer' — the author carefully distinguishes the concepts (outward submission vs. inward conviction) but notes that in reality they are co-extensive: every true believer is a true Muslim and vice versa. Importantly, the author adds a note about the opposites: the relation between the negations of two co-extensive terms (e.g., non-Muslim and non-believer) is also one of equality.

The third relation is al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq (absolute general and specific). Here, one concept is broader in extension than the other: every individual of the specific falls under the general, but not vice versa. The author uses the analogy of concentric circles (a large circle containing a smaller one). The first example is 'animal' and 'human': all humans are animals, but animals include non-humans. The second example is 'walker' (mashi) and 'gazelle': all gazelles are walkers, but not all walkers are gazelles. The author also notes the rule for opposites: if you negate both terms, the relation remains absolute general and specific, but the roles reverse — the negation of the general becomes more specific, and the negation of the specific becomes more general. The passage is clear and systematic, typical of a sharth that expands on a matn definition.

Key Takeaway
The three relations—tabayun, tasawi, and umum mutlaq—provide a precise vocabulary for describing how universal concepts relate in both meaning and reference, essential for clear definition and argumentation in the Islamic sciences.

The Ninth Relation: Al-Takhaluf (Compatibility)
Defines al-takhaluf as compatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of al-takhaluf (compatibility) between two meanings and distinguish it from other relations like tadad and contradiction based on coexistence and removal possibilities.

Core Idea
Al-Takhaluf is compatibility: two meanings can both be present and both be absent.

This passage introduces the ninth relation in the classification of relations between meanings. Understanding al-takhaluf is essential for analysing how concepts can be combined or separated in definitions and logical propositions. The examples of tallness/whiteness and scholar/eloquent illustrate the key feature: they can coexist and both be absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings being compared.
Locate the two terms whose relation is described. In the passage, the pairs are tallness/whiteness and scholar/eloquent.
2
Check if they can coexist in the same subject at the same time.
Determine whether both meanings can be true of a single subject in a single time. If yes, proceed to the next check.
For tallness and whiteness, a tall white person shows coexistence.
3
Check if they can both be absent from the same subject at the same time.
Determine whether it is possible for the subject to lack both meanings simultaneously. If yes, the relation is takhaluf.
A short black body shows both tallness and whiteness absent.
4
Verify the conclusion.
If both checks are positive, the relation is al-takhaluf. If only one is possible, it's another relation (tadad or contradiction).
For example, blackness and whiteness cannot coexist, so they are tadad, not takhaluf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة التاسعة : (التخالف) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان، أي: يمكن اجتماعهما معاً في شيء واحد في زمان واحد، ويمكن ارتفاعهما معاً عن شيء واحد في زمان واحد، ويقصد بارتفاعهما انتفاؤهما وعدم وجودهما.
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل : (الطول والبياض) فهما متخالفان، والنسبة بينهما هي التخالف، لأنهما قد يجتمعان معاً في شيء واحد في زمان واحد، كشخص طويل أبيض، وقد يرتفعان معاً مع اتحاد المكان والزمان، وذلك في نحو جسم قصير أسود.
ب ـ ومثل: (عالم وفصيح) فهما متخالفان، والنسبة بينهما هي التخالف، لأنهما قد يجتمعان معاً في نحو شخص عالم وفصيح، وقد يرتفعان معاً، وذلك عن مثل شخص جاهل غير فصيح.
The ninth relation: al-Takhaluf (compatibility). It is a relation between one meaning and another meaning from the aspect of the possibility of their coexistence and the possibility of their both being removed, with unity of place and time. That is, they can coexist together in one thing at one time, and they can both be removed from one thing at one time. By 'removal' is meant their negation and non-existence. Examples: a. Like tallness and whiteness; they are compatible (mutakhalifan), and the relation between them is al-Takhaluf, because they can coexist together in one thing at one time, such as a tall white person, and they can both be removed with unity of place and time, as in a short black body. b. Like scholar and eloquent; they are compatible, and the relation between them is al-Takhaluf, because they can coexist together in a person both scholarly and eloquent, and they can both be removed, as in an ignorant non-eloquent person.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the ninth relation, al-Takhaluf. The author states that it is the relation between two meanings that are neither mutually exclusive nor necessarily linked. They can both be present in the same subject at the same time, and they can both be absent from the same subject at the same time. Two examples are given: (1) tallness and whiteness—a person can be tall and white, or not tall and not white; (2) being a scholar and being eloquent—a person can be both or neither. This relation is distinct from al-Tadad (contrariety) where they cannot coexist, and from contradiction where both cannot be absent.

Al-Takhaluf is the relation of compatibility where two meanings can both be present and both be absent.

Contrariety (al-Tadad): When Two Meanings Cannot Coexist but Can Both Be Absent
Defines al-Tadad as contrariety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contrariety (al-Tadad) in a pair of meanings and distinguish it from logical contradiction (al-Tanaqud) using the criteria of coexistence and absence.

Core Idea
Contrariety allows both meanings to be false; contradiction forces one true.

This lesson defines the tenth relation between meanings: al-Tadad (contrariety). Unlike contradiction, contraries cannot both be true in the same subject at the same time and place, but they can both be absent. You will learn the precise conditions and see how this relation operates in examples like whiteness/blackness and masculinity/femininity. Understanding contrariety is essential for correctly analyzing rulings in fiqh and propositions in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of al-Tadad
Read the Arabic definition: 'نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً'. Identify the two key conditions: (1) impossibility of coexistence (ijtima'), (2) possibility of both being absent (irtifa'). Note also the limitation 'مع اتحاد المكان والزمان' (unity of place and time).
The definition explicitly rejects coexistence but allows joint absence.
2
Understand the unity of place and time condition
The condition 'اتحاد المكان والزمان' is crucial. Without it, two meanings might appear to coexist in different places or times. For example, a wall can be white at one end and black at the other—that is not contrariety because place differs.
The passage emphasizes this when discussing a partly white and partly black object.
3
Distinguish contrariety from contradiction
In logical contradiction (al-Tanaqud), the two opposites cannot both be false—one must be true (e.g., existence and non-existence). In contrariety, both can be absent. For instance, a thing can be neither white nor black (e.g., red). This is the key difference.
The passage contrasts with al-Takhaluf? Actually, al-Takhaluf is different; but the point stands: contrariety allows joint absence.
4
Apply to the given examples
Analyze the examples: (a) whiteness and blackness—cannot coexist in same spot, but a spot can be neither (e.g., red). (b) masculinity and femininity—cannot coexist in one individual naturally, but a thing can be neither (e.g., stone). Note the author's distinction between 'rational' and 'natural' contrariety for the second example.
The second example shows that contrariety may be based on empirical observation, not just logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة العاشرة: (التضادّ) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً، كل ذلك مع اتحاد المكان والزمان.
أي: لا يمكن اجتماعهما معاً في شيء واحد في زمان واحد، ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً (أي انتفاؤهما) عن شيء واحد في زمان واحد.
الأمثلة:
أ ـ مثل: (البياض والسواد) فهما معنيان متضادان، والنسبة بينهما هي التضاد، لأنهما لا يجتمعان معاً في مكان واحد في زمان واحد، فلا يكون المكان الواحد بعينه في وقت واحد أبيض وأسود معاً، وحينما نجد شيئاً بعضه أبيض وبعضه أسود، فإنا نقول: قد اختلف المكان، فلم يجتمع البياض والسواد في مكان واحد، ولكن البياض والسواد يمكن ارتفاعهما، أي: انتفاؤهما معاً مع اتحاد المكان والزمان، فقد يكون المكان الواحد بعينه لا أبيض ولا أسود، كأن يكون أحمر أو أصفر أو أزرق مثلاً.
ب ـ ومثل: (الذكورة والأنوثة) فهذان المعنيان متضادان، والنسبة بينهما هي التضاد، لأنهما لا يجتمعان معاً في شيء واحد بحسب العادة والنظام المألوف، فالتضاد بينهما تضادّ غير عقلي، إنما هو تضاد طبيعي، فلا يكون الشيء الواحد ذكراً وأنثى بآن واحد، ولكنهما قد يرتفعان معاً عن شيء واحد في زمان واحد، كارتفاعهما عن مثل الماء، والحجر، وكارتفاعهما عن
The tenth relation: al-Tadad (contrariety). It is a relation between one meaning and another meaning from the aspect of the impossibility of their coexistence but the possibility of their both being removed, all with unity of place and time. That is, they cannot coexist together in one thing at one time, but they can both be removed (i.e., negated) from one thing at one time. Examples: a. Like whiteness and blackness; they are two contrary meanings, and the relation between them is al-Tadad, because they cannot coexist together in one place at one time. So one and the same place at one time cannot be both white and black together. When we find something partly white and partly black, we say: the place has differed, so whiteness and blackness did not coexist in one place. But whiteness and blackness can both be removed, i.e., negated together with unity of place and time; for one and the same place may be neither white nor black, such as being red, yellow, or blue for example. b. Like masculinity and femininity; these two meanings are contrary, and the relation between them is al-Tadad, because they cannot coexist together in one thing according to custom and normal order. So the contrariety between them is not rational contrariety, but rather natural contrariety. Thus one thing cannot be simultaneously male and female, but they can both be removed from one thing at one time, such as their removal from water and stone, and their removal from...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the tenth relation, al-Tadad (contrariety). The author explains that contrary meanings cannot be together in the same subject at the same time and place, but they can both be absent. Two examples are given: (1) white and black—they cannot be in the same spot simultaneously, but a thing can be neither white nor black (e.g., red). (2) Masculinity and femininity—these cannot coexist naturally in one individual, but a thing can be neither male nor female (e.g., water, stone). The author notes that the contrariety between male and female is natural, not logical (i.e., it is based on empirical observation, not a necessary rational impossibility). This contrasts with al-Takhaluf where both can coexist.

Key Takeaway
Contrariety (al-Tadad) is a relation where two meanings cannot coexist in one subject at one time and place, yet both can be absent—unlike contradiction, where one must be present.

Tamāthul: The Relation of Parallelism (التماثل)
Author adds the relation of tamāthul (parallelism) to the list of logical relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of tamāthul and distinguish it from tasāwī in any logical context.

Core Idea
Tamāthul equates results; tasāwī equates denotation. Knowing the difference sharpens your analytical precision.

The author has already enumerated eleven logical relations from the disciplines of logic, uṣūl, and kalām. Now he adds four more, beginning with tamāthul. This relation concerns two meanings that yield the same result, even if their concepts differ. It is crucial to distinguish tamāthul from tasāwī (equality), which requires identity in denotation. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize tamāthul and apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relata
Find the two things being compared. In the example, they are 'five multiplied by five' and 'twenty-five'.
2
Check the result
Do they produce the same outcome? Here both equal twenty-five.
3
Compare with tasāwī
In tasāwī, the two terms must refer to the same set of individuals (e.g., 'man' and 'rational animal'). In tamāthul, they need not; they only need the same result.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a pair, decide if it is tamāthul or tasāwī based on whether they differ in denotation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه هي النسب التي استطعنا أن نحصيها من كلام المناطقة وعلماء الأصول وعلماء الكلام، وباستطاعتنا أن نضيف إليها أربع نسب أخرى، وهي كما يلي: ١ - (التماثل) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مساوٍ له في النتيجة، كالنسبة بين خمسة مضروبة في خمسة وبين العدد خمسة وعشرين، وهي نسبة نسميها التماثل تفريقاً بينه وبين التساوي، لأن التساوي في اصطلاح المناطقة هو الاتحاد في الماصدق مع الاختلاف في المفهوم. ٢ - (الكلية والجزئية) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلياً والآخر جزئياً من جزئياته، كالنسبة بين (إنسان وخالد). وإن كانت هذه النسبة تدخل في العموم والخصوص المطلق، إلا أن فيها ملاحظة معنى الكلية والجزئية هنا. ٣ - (الكلُّ والجزء) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه، كالنسبة بين (خالد ويده أو عينه). فخالد كلٌّ ويده أو عينه جزء من أجزائه. فحيثما وجد الكُلُّ فلا بد أن يكون الجزء موجوداً أيضاً في ضمن الكلّ. ٤ - (الإضافة) هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كل منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر، كإدراك الأبوة والبنوة، فإن أحدهما لا يدرك إلا مع إدراك الآخر، وكإدراك الصغير والكبير، وكإدراك القليل والكثير، وكإدراك الأعلى والأسفل،
These are the relations that we have been able to enumerate from the speech of the logicians, the scholars of uṣūl, and the scholars of kalām. And we can add to them four other relations, as follows: 1 - (Parallelism/Tamāthul): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in result, like the relation between five multiplied by five and the number twenty-five. It is a relation we call tamāthul to distinguish it from equality (tasāwī), because equality in the terminology of the logicians is identity in denotation (māṣdaq) with difference in concept. 2 - (Universality and Particularity / al-Kulliyya wa al-Juz'iyya): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning with respect to one being universal and the other a particular of its particulars, like the relation between (human and Khālid). Although this relation enters into absolute generality and specificity, still here there is an observation of the meaning of universality and particularity. 3 - (The Whole and the Part / al-Kull wa al-Juz'): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning with respect to one being a whole and the other a part of its parts, like the relation between (Khālid and his hand or his eye). So Khālid is a whole, and his hand or eye is a part of his parts. Wherever the whole exists, the part must also exist within the whole. 4 - (Correlation / al-Iḍāfa): It is a relation between two meanings, each one's perception being linked to the perception of the other, like the perception of fatherhood and sonship, for one of them is not perceived except with the perception of the other, and like the perception of small and large, and like the perception of little and much, and like the perception of higher and lower.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents four additional logical relations that the author adds to the previously enumerated eleven. The author first notes that the previous list was derived from logicians, uṣūlīs, and mutakallimūn. Then he lists the four new relations: 1) Tamāthul (similarity or parallelism): This is a relation between two meanings that yield the same result, e.g., 'five times five' and 'twenty-five' both produce the same number. The author stresses that this is distinct from tasāwī (equality) as defined by logicians: equality is identity in denotation (māṣdaq) with difference in concept (e.g., 'man' and 'rational animal' refer to the same set of individuals but have different meanings). Tamāthul is broader and focuses on result equivalence. 2) Kulliyya wa juz'iyya (universality and particularity): This relation holds when one meaning is universal (e.g., 'insān' = human) and the other is a particular instance (e.g., 'Khālid' = a specific person). Although this is a type of absolute generality and specificity, the author highlights the aspect of universality and particularity specifically. 3) Kull wa juz' (whole and part): This relation is between a whole and its part, e.g., 'Khālid' (person) and 'his hand' (part). The whole necessarily includes the part, so wherever the whole exists, the part exists within it. 4) Iḍāfa (correlation): This is a relation where each term is understood only in relation to the other, like father and son, or small and large. The perception of one requires the perception of the other. These four relations supplement the earlier list and are used in logical analysis.

Key Terms
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, tamāthul is distinguished from tasāwī because:
A) Both require identical denotation.
B) Tamāthul requires equal result while tasāwī requires identical denotation.
C) Tamāthul requires identical denotation while tasāwī requires equal result.
D) They are the same relation.

Key Takeaway
Tamāthul is a relation of result-equivalence, distinct from tasāwī (denotation-equivalence).
