Universality and Particularity: الكلية والجزئية
Author adds the relation of universality-particularity (الكلي والجزئي).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of universality-particularity between two terms and distinguish it from whole-part relation.

Core Idea
The universal-particular relation is about conceptual inclusion, not spatial or compositional inclusion.

The second additional relation is that of universality and particularity. This occurs when one meaning is a universal (kullī) and the other is one of its particular instances (juz'ī). For example, 'human' is a universal of which 'Khālid' is a particular. The author notes that this relation is a specific aspect of the broader concept of absolute generality and specificity. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize this relation and differentiate it from the whole-part relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal term
Find the term that can be predicated of many, e.g., 'insān' (human).
2
Identify the particular term
Find the specific instance, e.g., 'Khālid'.
3
Check the subsumption
Is the particular one of the individuals that fall under the universal? Yes, Khālid is a human.
4
Contrast with whole-part
In whole-part, the part is a constituent of the whole; in universal-particular, the particular is an instance of the universal category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه هي النسب التي استطعنا أن نحصيها من كلام المناطقة وعلماء الأصول وعلماء الكلام، وباستطاعتنا أن نضيف إليها أربع نسب أخرى، وهي كما يلي: ١ - (التماثل) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مساوٍ له في النتيجة، كالنسبة بين خمسة مضروبة في خمسة وبين العدد خمسة وعشرين، وهي نسبة نسميها التماثل تفريقاً بينه وبين التساوي، لأن التساوي في اصطلاح المناطقة هو الاتحاد في الماصدق مع الاختلاف في المفهوم. ٢ - (الكلية والجزئية) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلياً والآخر جزئياً من جزئياته، كالنسبة بين (إنسان وخالد). وإن كانت هذه النسبة تدخل في العموم والخصوص المطلق، إلا أن فيها ملاحظة معنى الكلية والجزئية هنا. ٣ - (الكلُّ والجزء) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه، كالنسبة بين (خالد ويده أو عينه). فخالد كلٌّ ويده أو عينه جزء من أجزائه. فحيثما وجد الكُلُّ فلا بد أن يكون الجزء موجوداً أيضاً في ضمن الكلّ. ٤ - (الإضافة) هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كل منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر، كإدراك الأبوة والبنوة، فإن أحدهما لا يدرك إلا مع إدراك الآخر، وكإدراك الصغير والكبير، وكإدراك القليل والكثير، وكإدراك الأعلى والأسفل،
These are the relations that we have been able to enumerate from the speech of the logicians, the scholars of uṣūl, and the scholars of kalām. And we can add to them four other relations, as follows: 1 - (Parallelism/Tamāthul): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in result, like the relation between five multiplied by five and the number twenty-five. It is a relation we call tamāthul to distinguish it from equality (tasāwī), because equality in the terminology of the logicians is identity in denotation (māṣdaq) with difference in concept. 2 - (Universality and Particularity / al-Kulliyya wa al-Juz'iyya): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning with respect to one being universal and the other a particular of its particulars, like the relation between (human and Khālid). Although this relation enters into absolute generality and specificity, still here there is an observation of the meaning of universality and particularity. 3 - (The Whole and the Part / al-Kull wa al-Juz'): It is a relation between a meaning and another meaning with respect to one being a whole and the other a part of its parts, like the relation between (Khālid and his hand or his eye). So Khālid is a whole, and his hand or eye is a part of his parts. Wherever the whole exists, the part must also exist within the whole. 4 - (Correlation / al-Iḍāfa): It is a relation between two meanings, each one's perception being linked to the perception of the other, like the perception of fatherhood and sonship, for one of them is not perceived except with the perception of the other, and like the perception of small and large, and like the perception of little and much, and like the perception of higher and lower.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents four additional logical relations that the author adds to the previously enumerated eleven. The author first notes that the previous list was derived from logicians, uṣūlīs, and mutakallimūn. Then he lists the four new relations: 1) Tamāthul (similarity or parallelism): This is a relation between two meanings that yield the same result, e.g., 'five times five' and 'twenty-five' both produce the same number. The author stresses that this is distinct from tasāwī (equality) as defined by logicians: equality is identity in denotation (māṣdaq) with difference in concept (e.g., 'man' and 'rational animal' refer to the same set of individuals but have different meanings). Tamāthul is broader and focuses on result equivalence. 2) Kulliyya wa juz'iyya (universality and particularity): This relation holds when one meaning is universal (e.g., 'insān' = human) and the other is a particular instance (e.g., 'Khālid' = a specific person). Although this is a type of absolute generality and specificity, the author highlights the aspect of universality and particularity specifically. 3) Kull wa juz' (whole and part): This relation is between a whole and its part, e.g., 'Khālid' (person) and 'his hand' (part). The whole necessarily includes the part, so wherever the whole exists, the part exists within it. 4) Iḍāfa (correlation): This is a relation where each term is understood only in relation to the other, like father and son, or small and large. The perception of one requires the perception of the other. These four relations supplement the earlier list and are used in logical analysis.

Key Terms
الكلية والجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs exemplifies the relation of universality-particularity as described in the passage?
A) Khālid and his hand
B) Human and Khālid
C) Father and son
D) 5x5 and 25

Key Takeaway
Universality-particularity is the relation between a category and its instances, distinct from whole-part composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the universal and particular in the pair: (ṣalāt [prayer] and ẓuhr [noon prayer]). Explain why this is universality-particularity and not whole-part.

Whole and Part: الكل والجزء
Author adds the relation of whole and part (الكل والجزء).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the whole-part relation between two terms and distinguish it from universality-particularity.

Core Idea
The whole-part relation is about composition: the whole contains the part as a constituent.

The third additional relation is that of the whole and the part. This occurs when one meaning is a whole (kull) and the other is a part (juz') of it. For example, 'Khālid' (a person) is a whole, and his hand or eye is a part. The author notes that wherever the whole exists, the part must also exist within the whole. Understanding this relation is crucial for analyzing composite concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole term
Find the term that represents a composite entity that can be divided into parts, e.g., 'Khālid' (the person).
2
Identify the part term
Find the term that is a constituent of that whole, e.g., 'his hand'.
3
Check the inclusion
Is the part a component of the whole? In the example, the hand is a part of Khālid's body.
4
Contrast with universality-particularity
In whole-part, the part is a constituent; in universality-particularity, the particular is an instance, not a constituent.

Source Text

Correlation: الإضافة
Author adds the relation of correlation (الإضافة).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of correlation (iḍāfa) between two terms and recognize its mutual interdependence.

Core Idea
Correlation means each term is understood only in relation to the other; they are conceptually inseparable.

The fourth additional relation is correlation (iḍāfa). This is a relation between two meanings where each one is perceived only in conjunction with the other. Examples include fatherhood and sonship, small and large, little and much, higher and lower. In each case, the concept of one cannot be fully grasped without the other. This lesson teaches you to recognize this relation and its importance in relational logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relata
Find the two terms that are mutually dependent in meaning. For example, 'father' and 'son'.
2
Check mutual perception
Can you fully understand one without the other? In the example, 'father' implies a child, and 'son' implies a parent.
3
List the examples from the text
The author gives: fatherhood-sonship, small-large, little-much, higher-lower.
4
Distinguish from other relations
Correlation is different from tamāthul (result equality), universality-particularity (category membership), and whole-part (composition). It is purely relational.

Source Text

Division and Classification
beginner
7 min
Extension Relations Between Concepts
Author defines four extension-based relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four extension relations (tabāyun, tasāwī, ʻumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq, ʻumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh) by comparing the extensions of any two Islamic terms.

Core Idea
Four relations define how concepts' scopes overlap or differ.

This passage introduces the first four logical relations based on extension—the range of individuals to which concepts apply. Understanding these is key to analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Find the two meanings being compared in the definition. They are always two distinct concepts.
2
Check for complete overlap in extension
If both concepts apply to exactly the same individuals, the relation is tasāwī (equality).
Example: 'human' and 'rational animal' are equal in extension.
3
Check for no overlap
If no individual is common to both, the relation is tabāyun (disparateness).
Example: 'human' and 'stone' have no overlap.
4
Check if one wholly contains the other
If one concept applies to every individual of the other but not vice versa, it is ʻumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq (absolute generality/specificity).
Example: 'animal' applies to all humans, but not all animals are human.
5
Check for partial overlap
If each concept applies to some individuals the other does not, it is ʻumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh (generality/specificity from one aspect).
Example: 'human' and 'white' partially overlap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا نستكمل البحث في النسب، وخلاصتها فيما يلي :
النَّسبُ
في دائرة الألفاظ والمعاني
١ - التباين
٢ - التساوي
٣ - العموم والخصوص المطلق
٤ - العموم والخصوص من وجه
٥ - التواطؤ
٦ - التشكيك
٧ - الترادف
٨ - الاشتراك
٩ - التخالف
١٠ - التضاد
١١ - التناقض
١٢ - التماثل
١٣ - الكلية والجزئية
١٤ - الكل والجزء
١٥ - الإضافة
خلاصة تعريفات النَّسب :
١ - التباين : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، ولا ينطبق أي واحد منهما على أي فردٍ ممّا ينطبق عليه الآخر.
٢ - التساوي : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، إلا أنهما متحدان في الماصدق .
٣ - العموم والخصوص المطلق : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، وأحدهما ينطبق على كلّ ما ينطبق عليه الآخر من أفراد دون العكس .
٤ - العموم والخصوص من وجه : هي النسبة بين معنى كلي ومعنى كلي آخر، وكلّ منهما ينطبق على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الآخر، وينفرد بانطباقه على أفراد لا ينطبق عليها الآخر.
٥ - التواطؤ: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده بالتساوي دون تفاوت.
٦ - التشكيك: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده مع التفاوت قوّةً وضعفاً.
٧ - الترادف: هي نسبة لفظ إلى لفظ من جهة دلالة كلّ منهما على المعنى نفسه الذي يدلّ عليه الآخر.
٨ - الاشتراك: هي نسبة معنى إلى معنى آخر من جهة اشتراكهما في لفظ واحدٍ يدلّ على كلّ منهما، عكس الترادف.
٩ - التخالف: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما، وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان.
١٠ - التضادّ: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً.
١١ - التناقض: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما معاً وعدم إمكان ارتفاعهما معاً، في شيء واحد وزمان واحد.
١٢ - التماثل: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مساوٍ له في النتيجة.
١٣ - الكلية والجزئية: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كليّاً، والآخر جزئياً من جزئياته.
١٤ - الكلّ والجزء: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه.
١٥ - الإضافة: هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كلّ منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر.
And with this we complete the investigation of the relations, and their summary is as follows: Relations in the domain of words and meanings: 1. Disparateness (al-tabāyun), 2. Equality (al-tasāwī), 3. Absolute generality and particularity (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq), 4. Generality and particularity from one aspect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh), 5. Univocality (al-tawāṭu’), 6. Ambiguity (al-tashkīk), 7. Synonymy (al-tarāduf), 8. Homonymy (al-ishtirāk), 9. Compatibility (al-takhāluf), 10. Opposition (al-taḍādd), 11. Contradiction (al-tanāquḍ), 12. Similarity (al-tamāthul), 13. Universality and particularity (al-kulliyya wa al-juz’iyya), 14. Whole and part (al-kull wa al-juz’), 15. Relation (al-iḍāfa). Summary of definitions of the relations: 1. Disparateness: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, and neither applies to any individual to which the other applies. 2. Equality: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, but they are identical in extension. 3. Absolute generality and particularity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, and one of them applies to every individual to which the other applies, but not vice versa. 4. Generality and particularity from one aspect: the relation between a universal meaning and another universal meaning, each of which applies to some individuals to which the other applies, and each is uniquely applicable to individuals to which the other does not apply. 5. Univocality: the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals equally without variation. 6. Ambiguity: the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals with variation in strength and weakness. 7. Synonymy: the relation of a word to a word in terms of each indicating the same meaning as the other. 8. Homonymy: the relation of a meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing a single word that indicates each of them; the opposite of synonymy. 9. Compatibility: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their coexistence and the possibility of their removal together, given unity of place and time. 10. Opposition: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their coexistence given unity of place and time, but it is possible for both to be absent together. 11. Contradiction: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their coexistence together and the impossibility of their removal together, regarding one thing and one time. 12. Similarity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in result. 13. Universality and particularity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being universal and the other being a particular among its particulars. 14. Whole and part: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being a whole and the other being a part among its parts. 15. Relation (al-iḍāfa): the relation between two meanings such that the apprehension of each is linked to the apprehension of the other.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the fifteen types of relations (nusub) between concepts that are fundamental to logic. The author begins by stating that this completes the investigation of relations, then lists them in two groups: first a simple enumeration (1–15), then a summary with full definitions. The definitions are precise and technical.

1. **Disparateness (al-tabāyun)**: Two concepts that have no overlap in extension; e.g., 'human' and 'stone'.
2. **Equality (al-tasāwī)**: Two concepts with different intensions but identical extensions; e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'.
3. **Absolute generality and particularity (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq)**: One concept is broader in extension than the other; e.g., 'animal' (more general) and 'human' (more specific).
4. **Generality and particularity from one aspect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh)**: Two concepts that intersect such that each applies to some individuals the other does not; e.g., 'human' and 'white'—some humans are white, some not; some white things are human, some not.
5. **Univocality (al-tawāṭu’)**: A universal concept applies equally to all its instances; e.g., 'human' applies equally to Zayd and ʻAmr.
6. **Ambiguity (al-tashkīk)**: A universal concept applies with varying intensity; e.g., 'existence' applies to God primarily and to creatures derivatively.
7. **Synonymy (al-tarāduf)**: Two words indicating the exact same meaning; e.g., 'Lion' and 'asad' (though rare in Arabic, 'insān' and 'bashar' are near-synonyms).
8. **Homonymy (al-ishtirāk)**: One word indicating multiple distinct meanings; e.g., 'ʻayn' meaning eye, spring, spy, etc.
9. **Compatibility (al-takhāluf)**: Two concepts that can both be true of the same thing at the same time and place, and also both false; e.g., 'standing' and 'sitting' (one can be standing now, sitting later, or neither if lying).
10. **Opposition (al-taḍādd)**: Two concepts that cannot both be true simultaneously, but can both be false; e.g., 'black' and 'white'—a surface cannot be both black and white at once, but can be neither (e.g., red).
11. **Contradiction (al-tanāquḍ)**: Two concepts that cannot both be true and cannot both be false; e.g., 'existent' and 'non-existent'—everything is either existent or non-existent.
12. **Similarity (al-tamāthul)**: Two concepts equal in result (perhaps equal in logical consequence); this is less common—the text defines it simply.
13. **Universality and particularity (al-kulliyya wa al-juz’iyya)**: The relation between a universal and one of its particulars; e.g., 'animal' and 'human'.
14. **Whole and part (al-kull wa al-juz’)**: The relation between a whole and one of its parts; e.g., 'house' and 'room'.
15. **Relation (al-iḍāfa)**: The relation between two terms where each is defined relative to the other; e.g., 'father' and 'son'.

These definitions are essential for analyzing logical relationships between terms and for constructing valid syllogisms.

Key Terms
التباين
التساوي
العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص من وجه

Key Takeaway
Extension relations determine how two concepts' scopes relate: no overlap, full overlap, one-way inclusion, or partial overlap.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Division and Classification
beginner
7 min
Predication and Word Relations
Author defines predication and word relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify terms as univocal (tawāṭuʼ), analogical (tashkīk), synonymous (tarāduf), or homonymous (ishtirāk) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
How a universal applies to particulars (equal or graded) and how words relate to meanings (same or shared).

After extension, the author turns to predication and word-level relations. These distinctions are crucial for defining terms correctly and avoiding fallacies in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of relation
First three definitions concern how a universal concept relates to its individuals; the fourth concerns words and meanings.
2
Check for equal application
If a universal concept applies to all its instances in the same degree, it is tawāṭuʼ (univocality).
Example: 'human' applies equally to Zayd and ʻAmr.
3
Check for graded application
If the universal applies with variation in intensity (e.g., priority, strength), it is tashkīk (ambiguity).
Example: 'wujūd' (existence) applies to God primarily, to creatures derivatively.
4
Check for synonymy
If two different words indicate the exact same meaning, it is tarāduf (synonymy).
Example: 'asad' and 'layth' both meaning 'lion' are synonyms.
5
Check for homonymy
If two distinct meanings are indicated by a single word, it is ishtirāk (homonymy).
Example: 'ʿayn' can mean 'eye', 'spring', or 'spy'.

خلاصة تعريفات النَّسب :
١ - التباين : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، ولا ينطبق أي واحد منهما على أي فردٍ ممّا ينطبق عليه الآخر.
٢ - التساوي : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، إلا أنهما متحدان في الماصدق .
٣ - العموم والخصوص المطلق : هي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، وأحدهما ينطبق على كلّ ما ينطبق عليه الآخر من أفراد دون العكس .
٤ - العموم والخصوص من وجه : هي النسبة بين معنى كلي ومعنى كلي آخر، وكلّ منهما ينطبق على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الآخر، وينفرد بانطباقه على أفراد لا ينطبق عليها الآخر.
٥ - التواطؤ: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده بالتساوي دون تفاوت.
٦ - التشكيك: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده مع التفاوت قوّةً وضعفاً.
٧ - الترادف: هي نسبة لفظ إلى لفظ من جهة دلالة كلّ منهما على المعنى نفسه الذي يدلّ عليه الآخر.
٨ - الاشتراك: هي نسبة معنى إلى معنى آخر من جهة اشتراكهما في لفظ واحدٍ يدلّ على كلّ منهما، عكس الترادف.
٩ - التخالف: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما، وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان.

Division and Classification
intermediate
10 min
Coexistence and Structural Relations
Author defines coexistence and structural relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the relations of compatibility (takhāluf), opposition (taḍādd), contradiction (tanāquḍ), similarity (tamāthul), universal-particular (kulliyya/juzʼiyya), whole-part (kull/juzʼ), and mutual relation (iḍāfa) using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
These seven relations capture how concepts coexist, oppose, or structurally compose one another.

The final set of relations includes those concerning co-occurrence (can both be true/false?), opposition, and structural dependence. These are widely used in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coexistence condition
Check whether the two concepts can both be true at the same time and place, and whether they can both be false.
2
Check for compatibility
If they can both be true and both be false, it is takhāluf (compatibility).
Example: 'standing' and 'sitting' (one can be neither if lying).
3
Check for opposition
If they cannot both be true but can both be false, it is taḍādd (opposition).
Example: 'black' and 'white' (cannot coexist, but can be absent).
4
Check for contradiction
If they cannot both be true and cannot both be false, it is tanāquḍ (contradiction).
Example: 'existent' and 'non-existent'.
5
Distinguish structural relations
Kulliyya wa juzʼiyya is about class membership (universal vs particular). Kull wa juzʼ is about composition (whole vs part). Iḍāfa is about mutual relational definition.
Example: 'animal' and 'human' = universal-particular; 'house' and 'room' = whole-part; 'father' and 'son' = iḍāfa.

١٢ - التماثل: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مساوٍ له في النتيجة.
١٣ - الكلية والجزئية: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كليّاً، والآخر جزئياً من جزئياته.
١٤ - الكلّ والجزء: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه.
١٥ - الإضافة: هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كلّ منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر.
And with this we complete the investigation of the relations, and their summary is as follows: Relations in the domain of words and meanings: 1. Disparateness (al-tabāyun), 2. Equality (al-tasāwī), 3. Absolute generality and particularity (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq), 4. Generality and particularity from one aspect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh), 5. Univocality (al-tawāṭu’), 6. Ambiguity (al-tashkīk), 7. Synonymy (al-tarāduf), 8. Homonymy (al-ishtirāk), 9. Compatibility (al-takhāluf), 10. Opposition (al-taḍādd), 11. Contradiction (al-tanāquḍ), 12. Similarity (al-tamāthul), 13. Universality and particularity (al-kulliyya wa al-juz’iyya), 14. Whole and part (al-kull wa al-juz’), 15. Relation (al-iḍāfa). Summary of definitions of the relations: 1. Disparateness: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, and neither applies to any individual to which the other applies. 2. Equality: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, but they are identical in extension. 3. Absolute generality and particularity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning different from it in concept, and one of them applies to every individual to which the other applies, but not vice versa. 4. Generality and particularity from one aspect: the relation between a universal meaning and another universal meaning, each of which applies to some individuals to which the other applies, and each is uniquely applicable to individuals to which the other does not apply. 5. Univocality: the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals equally without variation. 6. Ambiguity: the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals with variation in strength and weakness. 7. Synonymy: the relation of a word to a word in terms of each indicating the same meaning as the other. 8. Homonymy: the relation of a meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing a single word that indicates each of them; the opposite of synonymy. 9. Compatibility: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their coexistence and the possibility of their removal together, given unity of place and time. 10. Opposition: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their coexistence given unity of place and time, but it is possible for both to be absent together. 11. Contradiction: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their coexistence together and the impossibility of their removal together, regarding one thing and one time. 12. Similarity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in result. 13. Universality and particularity: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being universal and the other being a particular among its particulars. 14. Whole and part: the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being a whole and the other being a part among its parts. 15. Relation (al-iḍāfa): the relation between two meanings such that the apprehension of each is linked to the apprehension of the other.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the fifteen types of relations (nusub) between concepts that are fundamental to logic. The author begins by stating that this completes the investigation of relations, then lists them in two groups: first a simple enumeration (1–15), then a summary with full definitions. The definitions are precise and technical.

1. **Disparateness (al-tabāyun)**: Two concepts that have no overlap in extension; e.g., 'human' and 'stone'.
2. **Equality (al-tasāwī)**: Two concepts with different intensions but identical extensions; e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'.
3. **Absolute generality and particularity (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq)**: One concept is broader in extension than the other; e.g., 'animal' (more general) and 'human' (more specific).
4. **Generality and particularity from one aspect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh)**: Two concepts that intersect such that each applies to some individuals the other does not; e.g., 'human' and 'white'—some humans are white, some not; some white things are human, some not.
5. **Univocality (al-tawāṭu’)**: A universal concept applies equally to all its instances; e.g., 'human' applies equally to Zayd and ʻAmr.
6. **Ambiguity (al-tashkīk)**: A universal concept applies with varying intensity; e.g., 'existence' applies to God primarily and to creatures derivatively.
7. **Synonymy (al-tarāduf)**: Two words indicating the exact same meaning; e.g., 'Lion' and 'asad' (though rare in Arabic, 'insān' and 'bashar' are near-synonyms).
8. **Homonymy (al-ishtirāk)**: One word indicating multiple distinct meanings; e.g., 'ʻayn' meaning eye, spring, spy, etc.
9. **Compatibility (al-takhāluf)**: Two concepts that can both be true of the same thing at the same time and place, and also both false; e.g., 'standing' and 'sitting' (one can be standing now, sitting later, or neither if lying).
10. **Opposition (al-taḍādd)**: Two concepts that cannot both be true simultaneously, but can both be false; e.g., 'black' and 'white'—a surface cannot be both black and white at once, but can be neither (e.g., red).
11. **Contradiction (al-tanāquḍ)**: Two concepts that cannot both be true and cannot both be false; e.g., 'existent' and 'non-existent'—everything is either existent or non-existent.
12. **Similarity (al-tamāthul)**: Two concepts equal in result (perhaps equal in logical consequence); this is less common—the text defines it simply.
13. **Universality and particularity (al-kulliyya wa al-juz’iyya)**: The relation between a universal and one of its particulars; e.g., 'animal' and 'human'.
14. **Whole and part (al-kull wa al-juz’)**: The relation between a whole and one of its parts; e.g., 'house' and 'room'.
15. **Relation (al-iḍāfa)**: The relation between two terms where each is defined relative to the other; e.g., 'father' and 'son'.

These definitions are essential for analyzing logical relationships between terms and for constructing valid syllogisms.

Key Terms
التخالف
التضاد
التناقض
التماثل
الكلية والجزئية
الكل والجزء
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Taḍādd
C) Tanāquḍ
D) Tamāthul

Key Takeaway
Coexistence relations (compatibility, opposition, contradiction) and structural relations (universal-particular, whole-part, iḍāfa) complete the logical taxonomy of concept relationships.

Distinguishing Necessary and Accidental Conditional Conjunctive Propositions
Classification of conditional conjunctive into necessary and accidental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional conjunctive proposition as necessary or accidental based on the presence or absence of a real relationship between its parts.

Core Idea
Not all 'if...then' statements reflect a real connection; some are mere coincidences.

In logic, conditionals are not all alike. Some express a genuine entailment, like 'if it rains, the ground gets wet'; others are just coincidental, like 'if it rains, I wear a red shirt.' The author introduces this classification to sharpen our ability to distinguish real logical implications from accidental associations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conditional conjunctive proposition, locate the part after 'if' (المقدم) and the part after 'then' (التالي). This is the first step to analyze the connection.
2
Assess the relationship
Determine if there is a real, objective connection (علاقة في الواقع) between the antecedent and consequent that necessitates the consequent. If yes, the connection is necessary; if not, it is coincidental.
3
Check for exceptions
Consider whether the consequent could be false while the antecedent is true. For necessary conditionals, this cannot happen. For accidental, it can.
4
Label the type
If the connection is based on a real relationship, call it necessary (لزومية). If the connection is merely coincidental, call it accidental (اتفاقية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الشرطية المتصلة :
إضافة إلى أقسام القضية العامة التي تشترك فيها الحملية والمتصلة والمنفصلة، لا بد أن نلاحظ في الشرطية المتصلة أن ربط التالي بالمقدم فيها له حالتان :
١ - فإما أن يكون لوجود علاقة في الواقع بينهما .
٢ - وإما أن لا يكون لوجود علاقة في الواقع بينهما، وإنما اتفق في الواقع أن يحدث هذا التصاحب .
فإذا كان الربط لوجود علاقة في الواقع بينهما توجب ذلك فالمناطقة يسمونها (لزومية).
وإذا كان الربط لمجرد أنه حصل التصاحب بينهما اتفاقاً فالمناطقة يسمونها (اتفاقية).
وبهذا يصير لدينا في القضية الشرطية المتصلة قسمان :
الأول : اللزومية .
Divisions of the conditional conjunctive proposition: In addition to the general divisions of propositions shared by categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive, we must observe in the conditional conjunctive that the connection of the consequent to the antecedent has two states: 1 - Either it is due to a real relationship between them. 2 - Or it is not due to a real relationship between them, but rather this accompaniment coincidentally occurs in reality. So if the connection is due to a real relationship between them that necessitates it, logicians call it (لزومية - necessary). And if the connection is merely because the accompaniment between them occurs coincidentally, logicians call it (اتفاقية - accidental). And thus we have in the conditional conjunctive proposition two divisions: The first: the necessary.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the division of conditional conjunctive propositions into two types based on the nature of the connection between antecedent and consequent. The author reminds us that beyond the general divisions (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) shared by all propositions, conditional conjunctives have a specific classification concerning the relationship between the two parts. The first case is when the connection is due to a real, objective relationship (علاقة في الواقع) between antecedent and consequent, such as a causal or logical entailment; logicians call this 'لزومية' (necessary). The second case is when there is no real relationship, but the two parts happen to co-occur in reality; this is called 'اتفاقية' (accidental). For example, 'If Zaid is a tailor, then he knows how to stitch' is necessary because the profession entails the skill. An accidental example would be 'If tomorrow is Friday, then it is cloudy' — there is no necessary connection between Friday and cloudiness. This classification helps distinguish genuine logical implications from mere coincidences in reasoning.

Key Terms
لزومية
اتفاقية
ربط التالي بالمقدم

Key Takeaway

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Classifies the three main types of disjunctive conditionals in a diagram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions based on their truth conditions and identify them in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions have three distinct logical forms, each with unique truth conditions.

In Islamic logic, a disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) presents alternatives. However, not all 'either-or' statements are the same; they differ in whether they allow both true, both false, or exactly one. This lesson introduces the three main divisions classified by classical logicians, which are foundational for understanding conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic structure of a disjunctive
A disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) consists of two alternatives. It always claims incompatibility but the strength varies.
2
Learn the three divisions from the diagram
The diagram lists three types: 'حقيقية' (exclusive or true disjunction), 'مانعة جمع فقط' (exclusive of conjunction only), and 'مانعة خلو فقط' (exclusive of disjunction only).
Memorize the Arabic terms and their literal translations.
3
Memorize the truth conditions for each type
For exclusive (حقيقية): exactly one alternative is true. For exclusive of conjunction only: both cannot be true, but both can be false. For exclusive of disjunction only: both cannot be false, but both can be true.
Use the mnemonic: 'حقيقية' = strictly one; 'مانعة جمع فقط' = prevents both true; 'مانعة خلو فقط' = prevents both false.
4
Test with a simple Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'Either this act is obligatory (wajib) or it is prohibited (haram).' This is exclusive of conjunction only because an act cannot be both wajib and haram, but it could be neither (e.g., mubah).
Apply the truth conditions to classify the example.
5
Check your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple choice question to verify you can identify each type based on truth conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأقسام الرئيسية للمنفصلة
__|__
| | |
حقيقية مانعة جمع فقط مانعة خلو فقط
Main divisions of the disjunctive:
     |
     |
تاريخية    مانعة جمع فقط    مانعة خلو فقط
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a diagram summarizing the three main divisions of disjunctive conditional propositions. The first is 'حقيقية' (exclusive), which is the true disjunction that prevents both conjunction (both true) and disjunction (both false). The second is 'مانعة جمع فقط' (exclusive of conjunction only), which prevents both being true but allows both false. The third is 'مانعة خلو فقط' (exclusive of disjunction only), which prevents both being false but allows both true. This classification is foundational for understanding conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
حقيقية
مانعة جمع فقط
مانعة خلو فقط

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunctives differ in which combinations of truth values they exclude, and mastering this classification is essential for correct conditional reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Mutual Partial Generality (Mutakhālifān)
Defines mutual partial generality and gives examples with predication rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify terms that have mutual partial generality and correctly describe their predication rules.

Core Idea
Two terms overlap partially; neither is fully included in the other.

This lesson introduces the second type of term relation: mutual partial generality (عموم وخصوص من وجه). Unlike complete inclusion or exclusion, here two terms are such that each applies to some but not all individuals of the other. The passage gives examples and the rules for predicating one term of the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Look for a pair of terms that the author lists as examples, such as 'animal' and 'white', or 'tall' and 'human'. These are called mutakhālifāt (different).
The terms are from conceptual simple terms (مفردات تصورية).
2
Check for overlap
Determine whether some individuals of the first term are also individuals of the second, and vice versa. For 'animal' and 'white', there is overlap: some animals are white, and some white things are animals.
If there is no overlap, it's mutual exclusion (تباين). If one fully includes the other, it's absolute generality/specificity (عموم مطلق).
3
Test universality
Ask: Does the first term apply to all individuals of the second? And does the second apply to all of the first? For mutual partial generality, neither holds. E.g., not all animals are white, and not all white things are animals.
The passage says: 'neither is predicated on all individuals of the other, neither affirmative nor negative.'
4
Conclude the relation
If the terms overlap but neither contains the other, they have mutual partial generality. This means each term is predicated on some individuals of the other affirmatively and on some negatively.
The rule: each term can be said of some of the other's individuals affirmatively ('some A is B') and negatively ('some A is not B').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : ما بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه من المفردات التصورية (وينطبقان على المتخالفين) يحمل كل واحد منهما على بعض أفراد الآخر فقط حمل إيجاب وحمل سلب، ولا يحمل أي منهما على كل أفراد الآخر لا حمل إيجاب ولا حمل سلب . الأمثلة : (حيوان . أبيض . طويل . متكلم . جميل . إنسان) هذه متخالفات، بينها
Secondly: That between which there is mutual general-special relation (general and specific from one respect) among conceptual terms (and they apply to different terms); each one is predicated on some individuals of the other only, both affirmative and negative predication, and neither is predicated on all individuals of the other, neither affirmative nor negative predication. Examples: (animal, white, tall, speaking, beautiful, human); these are different (mutakhālifāt), between them...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage introduces a second type of relation between conceptual terms: mutual partial generality (عموم وخصوص من وجه). This applies to terms that are different (متخالفات), meaning that neither is fully included in the other. The rule is that each term can be predicated on some individuals of the other both affirmatively and negatively, but not on all individuals either way. The examples listed are: animal, white, tall, speaking, beautiful, human. These are said to be mutakhālifāt (different from each other). The implication is that, for instance, 'animal' and 'white' are different: some animals are white, some are not; some white things are animals, some are not. So neither can be predicated universally of the other. The passage likely continues with further explanation, but the provided text ends here.

Equality of Extension (Tasāwī) and Its Predication Rules
Author defines equality of extension and its permissible copulative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify terms with equality of extension and determine the correct predication forms (universal/particular positive) while rejecting invalid negative predications.

Core Idea
Equal terms differ in meaning but share referents; only positive predication is valid.

In Islamic logic, conceptual terms relate in various ways. One key relation is equality of extension (tasāwī), where two terms have different meanings yet refer to exactly the same things. This passage defines that relation and explains which types of predication are permissible, using examples from Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Find the terms said to be equal in extension. Notice that they have different meanings but refer to the same individuals.
E.g., 'human' and 'potentially laughing' are different concepts but apply to the same set of people.
2
Check the extension (maṣdaq) overlap
Verify that every individual of term A is an individual of term B and vice versa. This establishes equality.
In the example, every human can laugh (potentially), and every laughing being is human.
3
Apply the predication rule
Only universal positive predication (Every A is B, Every B is A) is always valid. Particular positive (Some A is B) is also valid but used only when a speaker has a specific purpose.
Do not use negative predication of any kind.
4
Reject negative predication
Understand why negative predication (universal or particular) is impossible: it would deny the identity of referents, effectively negating the thing from itself.
Saying 'Every human is not potentially laughing' is like saying 'I am not myself'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً: ما بينهما تساوٍ من المفردات التصورية (وهما المختلفان في المعنى المتحدان في الماصدق) يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب كليّ، وقد يُقتصر على الحمل الجزئي إذا تعلق به غرض للمتكلم، ولا يحمل أيّ منهما على الآخر حمل سلب لا كلي ولا جزئي.
الأمثلة: فيقال مثلاً:
في الحمل الكلي الموجب
كل إنسان ضاحك بالقوة
كل ضاحك بالقوة إنسان
كل مسلم عند الله هو مؤمن
كل مؤمن عند الله هو مسلم
في الحمل الجزئي الموجب
بعض الإنسان ضاحك بالقوة
بعض من هو ضاحك بالقوة إنسان
بعض المسلمين مؤمنون
بعض المؤمنين مسلمون
ولا يقال:
في الحمل الكلي السالب
كل إنسان ليس بضاحك بالقوة
كل ضاحك بالقوة ليس بإنسان
أو في الحمل الجزئي السالب
بعض الإنسان ليس بضاحك بالقوة
بعض من هو ضاحك بالقوة ليس بإنسان
وهكذا، فقضايا من هذا النوع كاذبة، نظراً إلى أنها قد سلبت الاتفاق في الماصدق عن متساويين في الماصدق، إذ أفراد المحمول هي أفراد الموضوع نفسها، والسلب أدى إلى سلب الشيء عن نفسه، فهو نظير ما لو قال قائل: أنا لست نفسي.
Fourth: What between them is equality among simple conceptual terms (they are different in meaning but identical in referent). Each one of them can be predicated on the other with a universal positive predication, and one may restrict to particular predication if a purpose of the speaker is attached to it. Neither of them is predicated on the other with a negative predication, neither universal nor particular. Examples: For example, it is said: In universal positive predication: Every human is potentially laughing, Every potentially laughing is human, Every Muslim in God's sight is a believer, Every believer in God's sight is a Muslim. In particular positive predication: Some human is potentially laughing, Some who is potentially laughing is human, Some Muslims are believers, Some believers are Muslims. And it is not said: In universal negative predication: Every human is not potentially laughing, Every potentially laughing is not human. Or in particular negative predication: Some human is not potentially laughing, Some who is potentially laughing is not human. And so on, for propositions of this type are false, considering that they have negated the agreement in referent about two equal terms in referent, since the individuals of the predicate are the same individuals of the subject, and the negation led to negating the thing from itself, so it is analogous to if someone said: I am not myself.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the relation of equality (tasāwī) between two simple conceptual terms. Equality means the two terms differ in meaning but have identical referents (e.g., 'human' and 'potentially laughing' — different concepts but same individuals). The author states that such terms can only be predicated of each other positively: either universally or particularly. Universal positive predication (Every A is B and every B is A) is valid because all individuals of one are all individuals of the other. Particular positive predication (Some A is B) is also valid but is only used if the speaker has a specific purpose. Negative predication of any kind is invalid because it would negate the agreement in referent, effectively negating the thing from itself, like saying 'I am not myself'. The examples use Islamic concepts: 'human' and 'potentially laughing' are philosophically equal; 'Muslim' and 'believer' in God's sight are theologically equal.

Key Terms
تساوٍ
الماصدق
حمل إيجاب كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two simple conceptual terms have equality of extension (tasāwī), which of the following is a valid predication?
A) Every A is B and every B is A
B) Some A is not B
C) No A is B
D) Every A is B but not every B is A

Key Takeaway
Two terms with equality of extension can only be predicated positively, never negatively, because negative predication would deny the identity of their referents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Explain why 'Every Muslim is not a believer' is a false proposition based on the rule of equality of extension.

Synonymy (Tarāduf): Predication Rules for Identical Meanings
Author defines synonymy and its predication rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two terms are synonymous based on the predication rule and apply positive/negative predication correctly in Islamic definition contexts.

Core Idea
Synonyms share the same meaning, so only positive predication is valid; negative predication negates the thing from itself.

This passage introduces the concept of synonymy (tarāduf) and its logical implications for predication. When two expressions have identical meanings, they can only be predicated positively of each other. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences, where synonymous terms must be handled carefully to avoid logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining criteria of tarāduf
Find in the Arabic the two conditions: difference in expression (lafẓ) and unity in meaning (ma'nā). Underline them.
2
Parse the predication rule
The rule states that each synonym can be predicated positively on the other, but not negatively. Note that the author does not specify universal vs particular; the rule applies to the meaning itself.
3
Examine the example: 'insān' and 'bashar'
Verify that they meet the criteria: different words, same meaning. Then apply the rule: positive predication is correct, negative would be self-negation.
4
Distinguish from other relations
Contrast tarāduf with tasāwī (equal extension but different meaning). This helps prevent confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامساً: ما بينهما ترادف من الألفاظ (وهما المختلفان في اللفظ المتحدان في المعنى) يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب، ولا يحمل أي منهما على الآخر حمل سلب، لاتحادهما في المعنى.
فيقال مثلاً: الإنسان هو بشر، والبشر هو إنسان.
ولا يقال مثلاً: الإنسان ليس بشراً، والبشر ليس إنساناً.
إذا أريد بذلك معنى كل منهما، لأنه يؤدي إلى سلب الشيء عن نفسه.
Fifth: What between them is synonymy among expressions (they are different in expression but identical in meaning). Each one of them is predicated on the other with positive predication, and neither of them is predicated on the other with negative predication, due to their unity in meaning. For example, it is said: The human is the mortal, and the mortal is the human. And it is not said, for example: The human is not a mortal, and the mortal is not a human, if the meaning of each is intended, because it leads to negating the thing from itself.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines synonymy (tarāduf): two terms that differ in expression (lafẓ) but have identical meaning (ma'nā). For such terms, only positive predication is valid, whether universal or particular (the author does not specify; he says 'positive predication' without qualification, likely meaning either universal or particular as appropriate). Negative predication is invalid because it would negate the thing from itself. The example 'human' and 'mortal' (insān and bashar) are synonyms in Arabic (both meaning 'human being'). Unlike equality (tasāwī) where terms differ in meaning but share referent, synonyms share the same meaning.

Similarity in Result: Tamāthul
Author defines similarity and its predication rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of tamāthul (similarity) in Islamic logical contexts and distinguish them from synonymy and equality based on the criterion of leading to the same result.

Core Idea
Similarity in logic means two terms converge in result despite differing in meaning.

This lesson introduces a special type of relation between terms: tamāthul, or similarity in result. Unlike synonymy (where terms share the same meaning) or equality (where terms refer to the same thing), similarity requires only that the terms lead to the same conclusion. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct predicative reasoning in Islamic logic, especially in jadal and munazara where arguments may be structurally different yet share the same outcome.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Definition
Identify the author's definition of tamāthul in the Arabic text: 'وهما المؤديان إلى معنى واحد في النتيجة' (they are the two that lead to one meaning in the result). This is the core criterion.
The definition focuses on the result, not the terms themselves.
2
Compare with Synonymy and Equality
The author likens tamāthul to synonyms and equals but emphasizes they are not identical. Note the phrase 'كالمترادفين وكالمتساويين' (like synonyms and like equals). This shows similarity is analogous but distinct.
Synonyms share meaning; equals share reference; tamāthul shares only the conclusion.
3
Identify the Predication Condition
Understand the rule: each term can be predicated positively of the other: 'يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب لا حمل سلب'. Negative predication would be false because it denies the similarity.
This condition tests whether tamāthul holds between two terms.
4
Examine the Examples
List the examples given: (1) 'five multiplied by five' and 'five added to ten and ten' both yield 25; (2) 'even' and 'odd' are both number; (3) 'necessity, possibility, and impossibility' are all judgments of the intellect. Verify that each pair leads to the same result in a proposition.
The examples span arithmetic, number theory, and modal logic.
5
Apply the Reversal Test
The author states that the examples can be reversed (subject and predicate swapped) and still be truthful: 'وتعكس هذه الأمثلة فيجعل الموضوع محمولاً والمحمول موضوعاً ويكون الحمل صادقاً'. This shows symmetry, a key property of tamāthul.
Not all relations are symmetric; tamāthul is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً: ما بينهما تماثل، وهما المؤديان إلى معنى واحد في النتيجة،
وهما كالمترادفين وكالمتساويين، يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل
إيجاب لا حمل سلب، لتماثلهما في النتيجة، فيقال مثلاً خمسة مضروبة
بخمسة هي خمسة وعشرون، أو هي خمسة مضاف إليها عشرة وعشرة.
ويقال: الزوج والفرد هما العدد. ويقال: الوجوب والإمكان والاستحالة هي
أحكام العقل المتعلقة بالوجود والعدم. وتعكس هذه الأمثلة فيجعل الموضوع
محمولاً والمحمول موضوعاً ويكون الحمل صادقاً.
ولا يصح أن يكون شيء منها سالباً لأن القضايا حينئذٍ تكون كاذبة، إذ
yؤدي السلب إلى نفي التماثل عن المتماثلين.
Sixth: What between them is similarity, and they are the two that lead to one meaning in the result. They are like synonyms and like equals: each one of them is predicated on the other with positive predication, not negative predication, due to their similarity in result. For example, it is said: five multiplied by five is twenty-five, or it is five added to ten and ten. And it is said: even and odd are number. And it is said: necessity, possibility, and impossibility are the judgments of the intellect relating to existence and non-existence. And these examples are reversed, making the subject predicate and the predicate subject, and the predication is truthful. And it is not correct for any of them to be negative, because then the propositions would be false, since negation leads to denying similarity from two similar things.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines similarity (tamāthul) between two terms that, while different in expression and meaning, lead to the same result in a conclusion. For example, 'five multiplied by five' and 'five added to ten and ten' are different expressions but both result in 25. Similarly, 'even' and 'odd' are both number (they are not identical but both are numbers). The author considers them like synonyms and equals in that they can be predicated positively of each other (e.g., 'Five multiplied by five is twenty-five' and vice versa). Negative predication is invalid because it would deny the similarity that exists between them. The examples include mathematical and logical concepts (even, odd, modality). This is a more abstract relation than synonymy or equality.

نتيجة

Key Takeaway
Tamāthul is a relation where two terms, though different, lead to the same truth in a conclusion, allowing mutual positive predication.

Predication Rules between Whole and Part
Author defines predication rules between a whole and its part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid predications when one term is a whole and the other is its part.

Core Idea
A whole and its part are distinct; only partial predication is logically valid.

In this lesson, we examine the logical rules governing how a whole and its part can be predicated of each other. The source defines three cases and explains why universal predication fails. Understanding this rule prevents errors in conceptual analysis and in interpreting metaphorical statements in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole and part in the example
Notice that 'face' (whole) and 'two eyes' (part) are simple conceptual terms (mufrad tasawwuri). The author uses them to illustrate the rules.
The terms are from everyday language but are treated as logical simples.
2
Distinguish the three types of predication
Rule (a) allows partial predication of the whole on the part: 'some of the whole is the part'. Rule (b) allows partial predication of the part on the whole: 'some of the part is the whole'. Rule (c) prohibits universal or absolute predication in either direction.
The key is that 'some of' (ba‘d) is used for valid cases.
3
Understand why universal predication fails
The whole is distinct (mubāyin) from its part; they are not coextensive. Therefore, 'The eyes are the face' and 'The face is the eyes' are false logically.
Distinctness means the whole is not identical to the part, nor does the part encompass the whole.
4
Analyse the hadith example
The Prophet said 'The Hajj is Arafah.' Logically, this would be invalid because Hajj (whole) and Arafah (part) are not identical. The author explains it is metaphorical (majāz) for rhetorical emphasis.
Metaphorical usage does not violate logical rules; it operates on a different level of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعاً: إذا كان أحد المفردين التصوريين كلاً وكان الآخر جزءاً له
فالحمل بينهما يكون على الوجه التالي:
أ ـ يحمل بعض الكل على الجزء فيقال: العينان بعض الوجه أو من
الوجه.
ب ـ ويحمل الجزء على بعض الكل، فيقال: بعض الوجه عينان.
جـ ـ ولا يحمل أي واحد منهما على الآخر حملاً كلياً ولا حملاً مطلقاً،
فلا يقال: العينان وجه، ولا: الوجه عينان. لأن الكل في هذه الحالة مباين
لجزئه، ولا ينطبق أي واحد منهما على ما صدق الآخر.
وما يستعمل من ذلك عند الناس فهو من قبيل المجاز لغرض بلاغي،
ومنه قول الرسول ﷺ: «الحج عرفة».
Seventh: If one of the two conceptual simple terms is a whole and the other is a part of it, then the predication between them is as follows: (a) Some of the whole is predicated on the part, so it is said: The two eyes are some of the face or from the face. (b) And the part is predicated on some of the whole, so it is said: Some of the face is two eyes. (c) And neither of them is predicated on the other with universal predication nor absolute predication, so it is not said: The two eyes are a face, nor: The face is two eyes. Because the whole in this case is distinct from its part, and neither applies to the referent of the other. And what is used of that among people is metaphorical for a rhetorical purpose, and among it is the saying of the Messenger (ﷺ): 'The Hajj is Arafah.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines the part-whole relation between two simple conceptual terms. When one term is a whole (e.g., 'face') and the other is a part (e.g., 'two eyes'), predication is restricted to particular forms: one can say 'The two eyes are some of the face' (predicating part of the whole using 'some of') or 'Some of the face is two eyes' (predicating the whole of the part using 'some of'). Universal predication (e.g., 'The two eyes are the face' or 'The face is the two eyes') is invalid because the whole is distinct (mubāyin) from its part; they do not have the same extension. The author notes that when people use such universal predications, like the hadith 'The Hajj is Arafah', it is metaphorical (majāz) for a rhetorical purpose, not literal logical predication.

Key Takeaway
In logical predication, a whole and its part are not interchangeable; only partial predication is valid, and universal statements are either false or metaphorical.

The Operation of Division (Taqsīm)
Author defines division with examples from human classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three levels of particularization (species, varieties, individuals) in the logical operation of division (taqsīm) and apply this to Islamic legal classifications.

Core Idea
Division is the logical scalpel that dissects universals into their constituent particulars.

In this passage, the author introduces the first research operation: division. He explains how a universal is broken down into species, varieties, and individuals, providing examples from human classification. Understanding this operation is foundational for defining terms and establishing categories in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation description
The author states 'the universal is divided into particulars'. Note that this is a mental operation, not physical division.
2
Extract the three levels
The particulars are of three types: species (anwā‘, e.g., human under animal), varieties (aṣnāf, e.g., white/black human), and individuals (afrād, e.g., Zayd). Recognize the hierarchy from genus to species to variety to individual.
3
Analyze the examples of human division
The author divides human by color, continent, intelligence, health, belief, etc. Note that these are all accidental (variety-level) divisions. Division into individuals uses proper names.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider dividing the universal 'acts of worship' into species: prayer, fasting, etc. Then further divide 'prayer' into varieties: obligatory, sunna, etc. Identify which level each represents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسائل البحث عمليات التقسيم والجمع ، والتحليل والتركيب الكلي ينقسم - كما عرفنا - إلى جزئيات، وهذه الجزئيات تكون أنواعاً، كانقسام الجنس إلى أنواعه، وتكون أصنافاً أو أفراداً كانقسام النوع إلى أصنافه أو أفراده، ومثال انقسام النوع إلى أصنافه : انقسام الإنسان إلى أبيض وأسود وأحمر وأصفر، وانقسامه إلى إفريقي وآسيوي وأوروبي، وانقسامه إلى ذكي وبليد وما بينهما، وانقسامه إلى صحيح وعليل، وانقسامه إلى مؤمن وكافر، وإلى تقي وفاجر، وهكذا لا تكاد تنحصر تقسيمات الأصناف باختلاف الاعتبارات . ومثال انقسام النوع إلى أفراده : انقسام الإنسان إلى سعدٍ وباسمٍ وجميل، وسعديّة وباسمة وجميلة، وهكذا إلى سائر أفراد نوع الإنسان . والجزئيات المشتركة في معنى يطلق هذا المعنى على كل منها يمكن أن تجمع ذهناً في كلي واحد، فحيث نجد أشياء مشتركة في معنى جامع لها - ولو بوصف من الأوصاف - يمكن أن نجمعها تحت اسم كلي جامع مشتق من هذا الوصف المشترك، فوحيد القرن كلي يمكن أن يطلق على كل حيوان ذي قرن واحد فقط . وذوات القوائم الأربع كلي نجمع تحته كل حيوان يمشي على أربع قوائم . والثديي كلي نجمع تحته كل حيوان يُرضع بثدي له، أو هو من نوع ما يرضع بثدي له . والأبيض كلي نجمع تحته كل ذي لون أبيض من أي نوع ومن أي جنس، والطويل كذلك . وهكذا إلى ما لا حصر له . والكل يتألف - كما عرفنا سابقاً - من أجزاء، والأجزاء البسيطة أو المركبة قابلة للتركيب بوجه ما، سواء أكان هذا التركيب على وجه له نظير في
Means of Investigation: Operations of Division and Composition, Analysis and Synthesis. The universal is divided - as we have known - into particulars, and these particulars constitute species, like the division of a genus into its species, and they constitute varieties or individuals, like the division of a species into its varieties or individuals. An example of the division of a species into its varieties: the division of human into white, black, red, and yellow; and its division into African, Asian, and European; and its division into intelligent, stupid, and what is between them; and its division into healthy and ill; and its division into believer and disbeliever, and into pious and immoral; and thus the divisions of varieties hardly end due to different considerations. And an example of the division of a species into its individuals: the division of human into Sa'd, Basim, and Jamil, and Sa'diyya, Basima, and Jamila, and thus to the rest of the individuals of the human species. The particulars that share in a meaning such that this meaning applies to each of them can be mentally gathered into one universal. So whenever we find things sharing a comprehensive meaning - even by one attribute - we can gather them under a universal comprehensive name derived from that common attribute. For example, 'unicorn' is a universal that can apply to every animal having only one horn. And 'quadrupeds' is a universal under which we gather every animal that walks on four legs. And 'mammal' is a universal under which we gather every animal that suckles with a breast, or is of the type that suckles. And 'white' is a universal under which we gather everything having a white color from any species and any genus, and 'tall' likewise. And so on ad infinitum. And the whole is composed - as we have learned previously - of parts, and the simple or compound parts are capable of composition in some manner, whether this composition is in a way that has a counterpart in...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental research operations: division (taqsīm) and composition (jam‘), which are also referred to as analysis (taḥlīl) and synthesis (tarkīb). The author first explains division: the universal is divided into its particulars. These particulars can be species (anwā‘) from a genus (jins), varieties (aṣnāf) from a species, or individuals (afrād) from a species. Numerous examples are given: the human species can be divided by skin color, continental origin, intelligence, health, religion, piety, etc. Division into individuals is illustrated with proper names like Sa'd and Basim. The author emphasizes that such divisions are practically inexhaustible due to differing considerations (i‘tibārāt). Then the passage turns to composition: particulars that share a common meaning (ma‘nā jāmi‘) can be mentally gathered (tujma‘ dhihnan) into a single universal. This universal is named from that common attribute. Examples include 'unicorn' (for animals with one horn), 'quadruped' (four-legged walkers), 'mammal' (suckling animals), and 'white' (anything white). The author concludes by noting that the whole (al-kull) is composed of parts, and that parts—whether simple or compound—are capable of composition in some manner, although the page cuts off before completing this point.

Division is the logical operation of breaking a universal into its subordinate species, varieties, or individuals, each based on a distinct criterion.

The Operation of Composition (Jam‘)
Author defines composition with examples from animal kinds and colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how particulars sharing a common attribute can be mentally gathered into a universal (composition), and apply this to form concepts in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Composition is the mental glue that binds scattered particulars into a unified concept.

The author now moves to the second research operation: composition. He explains that whenever we find multiple things sharing a common meaning, we can gather them mentally under a single universal name derived from that common attribute. This operation is essential for forming abstract concepts in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation description
The author says 'particulars that share a meaning can be mentally gathered into one universal'. This is composition (jam‘) or synthesis.
2
Understand the condition for composition
The shared meaning can be any attribute, even a single description (بوصف من الأوصاف). The universal is named from that common attribute (مشتق).
3
Analyze the examples
Unicorn: all animals with one horn. Quadruped: animals that walk on four legs. Mammal: animals that suckle. White: anything white. Notice that the universal can span multiple species and genera.
4
Apply to a kalam example
Compose the universal 'mu‘jiza' (miracle) by gathering specific miraculous events (e.g., splitting the moon, Quran's inimitability) under the common attribute of being an extraordinary act proving prophethood.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسائل البحث عمليات التقسيم والجمع ، والتحليل والتركيب الكلي ينقسم - كما عرفنا - إلى جزئيات، وهذه الجزئيات تكون أنواعاً، كانقسام الجنس إلى أنواعه، وتكون أصنافاً أو أفراداً كانقسام النوع إلى أصنافه أو أفراده، ومثال انقسام النوع إلى أصنافه : انقسام الإنسان إلى أبيض وأسود وأحمر وأصفر، وانقسامه إلى إفريقي وآسيوي وأوروبي، وانقسامه إلى ذكي وبليد وما بينهما، وانقسامه إلى صحيح وعليل، وانقسامه إلى مؤمن وكافر، وإلى تقي وفاجر، وهكذا لا تكاد تنحصر تقسيمات الأصناف باختلاف الاعتبارات . ومثال انقسام النوع إلى أفراده : انقسام الإنسان إلى سعدٍ وباسمٍ وجميل، وسعديّة وباسمة وجميلة، وهكذا إلى سائر أفراد نوع الإنسان . والجزئيات المشتركة في معنى يطلق هذا المعنى على كل منها يمكن أن تجمع ذهناً في كلي واحد، فحيث نجد أشياء مشتركة في معنى جامع لها - ولو بوصف من الأوصاف - يمكن أن نجمعها تحت اسم كلي جامع مشتق من هذا الوصف المشترك، فوحيد القرن كلي يمكن أن يطلق على كل حيوان ذي قرن واحد فقط . وذوات القوائم الأربع كلي نجمع تحته كل حيوان يمشي على أربع قوائم . والثديي كلي نجمع تحته كل حيوان يُرضع بثدي له، أو هو من نوع ما يرضع بثدي له . والأبيض كلي نجمع تحته كل ذي لون أبيض من أي نوع ومن أي جنس، والطويل كذلك . وهكذا إلى ما لا حصر له . والكل يتألف - كما عرفنا سابقاً - من أجزاء، والأجزاء البسيطة أو المركبة قابلة للتركيب بوجه ما، سواء أكان هذا التركيب على وجه له نظير في
Means of Investigation: Operations of Division and Composition, Analysis and Synthesis. The universal is divided - as we have known - into particulars, and these particulars constitute species, like the division of a genus into its species, and they constitute varieties or individuals, like the division of a species into its varieties or individuals. An example of the division of a species into its varieties: the division of human into white, black, red, and yellow; and its division into African, Asian, and European; and its division into intelligent, stupid, and what is between them; and its division into healthy and ill; and its division into believer and disbeliever, and into pious and immoral; and thus the divisions of varieties hardly end due to different considerations. And an example of the division of a species into its individuals: the division of human into Sa'd, Basim, and Jamil, and Sa'diyya, Basima, and Jamila, and thus to the rest of the individuals of the human species. The particulars that share in a meaning such that this meaning applies to each of them can be mentally gathered into one universal. So whenever we find things sharing a comprehensive meaning - even by one attribute - we can gather them under a universal comprehensive name derived from that common attribute. For example, 'unicorn' is a universal that can apply to every animal having only one horn. And 'quadrupeds' is a universal under which we gather every animal that walks on four legs. And 'mammal' is a universal under which we gather every animal that suckles with a breast, or is of the type that suckles. And 'white' is a universal under which we gather everything having a white color from any species and any genus, and 'tall' likewise. And so on ad infinitum. And the whole is composed - as we have learned previously - of parts, and the simple or compound parts are capable of composition in some manner, whether this composition is in a way that has a counterpart in...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental research operations: division (taqsīm) and composition (jam‘), which are also referred to as analysis (taḥlīl) and synthesis (tarkīb). The author first explains division: the universal is divided into its particulars. These particulars can be species (anwā‘) from a genus (jins), varieties (aṣnāf) from a species, or individuals (afrād) from a species. Numerous examples are given: the human species can be divided by skin color, continental origin, intelligence, health, religion, piety, etc. Division into individuals is illustrated with proper names like Sa'd and Basim. The author emphasizes that such divisions are practically inexhaustible due to differing considerations (i‘tibārāt). Then the passage turns to composition: particulars that share a common meaning (ma‘nā jāmi‘) can be mentally gathered (tujma‘ dhihnan) into a single universal. This universal is named from that common attribute. Examples include 'unicorn' (for animals with one horn), 'quadruped' (four-legged walkers), 'mammal' (suckling animals), and 'white' (anything white). The author concludes by noting that the whole (al-kull) is composed of parts, and that parts—whether simple or compound—are capable of composition in some manner, although the page cuts off before completing this point.

Division and Collection: The Two Fundamental Epistemological Operations
Author describes methods of dividing and collecting knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the operations of division (taqsīm) and collection (jam‘) in a given Islamic science passage and explain how they produce universal rules.

Core Idea
To know the universal, divide the particular; to formulate rules, collect the scattered.

This passage introduces two foundational operations in Islamic epistemology: division and collection. These are not merely organizational tools; they are how the mind moves from particulars to universal rules. Understanding them will help you see how Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam build their comprehensive systems.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two operations
In the Arabic passage, find the words 'التقسيم' (division) and 'الجمع' (collection). Notice how they are introduced as paired operations.
2
Identify the purpose of division
The author says division facilitates study by breaking a universal into its parts. What specific benefit does he mention? Look for 'تيسير لدراسة كل قسم'.
3
Identify the purpose of collection
After studying the parts, we collect similar rulings. What does the author say collection produces? Look for 'إعطائها صفة القاعدة الكلية' and 'معرفة جديدة'.
4
Recognize the natural inclination
The author explains why the mind naturally seeks universals. Find the phrase 'من طبيعة الفكر ميله دائماً إلى إدراك كليات الأحكام' and understand its implication.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Think of a fiqh concept like 'الصلاة' (prayer). It can be divided into its types (obligatory, voluntary) and then collected under the universal ruling that all prayers require purity. Confirm this matches the passage.

فمن الوسائل النافعة جداً أو الضرورية في كثير من الأحيان في مجال اكتساب المعارف الحسية أو الاستنباطية؛ النظرية أو العملية، عمليات التقسيم والجمع، أي تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته وإلى أصنافه، وجمع الجزئيات المتفرقة في كلياتها . ففي التقسيم لكليات الأشياء تيسير لدراسة كل قسم دراسة مستوعبة، أو أكثر إحاطة بالصفات والخصائص، وبعد الدراسة الجزئية التفصيلية، واستنباط الأحكام منها، تعود العملية إلى جمع الأحكام الجزئية المتماثلة، أو جمع الأشباه والنظائر، وإعطائها صفة القاعدة الكلية الشاملة لأفراد الصنف، أو لأفراد النوع، أو لأفراد الجنس القريب أو البعيد، ففي هذا الجمع اختصار للمعرفة، وإيجاز فيها دون الإخلال بالمقصود منها، ودون الإخلال بتطبيقاتها، بل هي أيسر وأحكم لضبط الجزئيات دون إخلال، وفي هذا الجمع أيضاً معرفة جديدة، تكتسب فيها كليات الأحكام . ومن طبيعة الفكر ميله دائماً إلى إدراك كليات الأحكام، وكليات القضايا، ليوفر على نفسه حفظ كل جزئي وصفاته وخصائصه، وليستوعب في أقصر مدة أوسع ما يمكن من معرفة، وليتيسر له تطبيق ما استوعب من أحكام كلية على كل جزئي يتعرض له من الجزئيات الداخلة في عموم ما ينطبق عليه الحكم الكلي، ولو لم يكن له بخصوصه سابق معرفة به . لذلك يلجأ الباحثون بعد عمليات البحث إلى استخلاص الأحكام الكلية العامة، وصوغ القواعد الكلية، والقوانين العلمية، ثم يضعون هذه الأحكام والقواعد والقوانين أسسا يبنى عليها وينتفع بها في كل مجالات المعرفة النظرية والتطبيقية .
Among the very useful or often necessary means in the field of acquiring sensory or deductive knowledge, theoretical or practical, are the operations of division and collection: dividing the universal into its particulars and into its kinds, and collecting scattered particulars under their universals. In the division of universals of things there is facilitation for studying each section comprehensively, or with more comprehensive coverage of attributes and characteristics. After detailed partial study and deducing rulings from them, the process returns to collecting similar partial rulings, or collecting similars and parallels, and giving them the character of a universal rule comprehensive of the individuals of the class, or the individuals of the species, or the individuals of the proximate or remote genus. In this collection there is a shortening of knowledge, and conciseness without compromising its purpose, and without compromising its applications; rather it is easier and more precise for controlling particulars without deficiency. In this collection also there is new knowledge, in which universal rulings are acquired. And it is in the nature of the mind to always incline towards perceiving universal rulings and universal propositions, to spare itself the memorization of every particular and its attributes and characteristics, and to comprehend in the shortest time the widest possible knowledge, and to facilitate for itself applying what it has comprehended of universal rulings to every particular it encounters among the particulars falling under the generality of what the universal ruling applies to, even if it had no prior knowledge of it specifically. Therefore, after research operations, researchers resort to extracting general universal rulings, formulating universal rules and scientific laws, then they place these rulings, rules, and laws as foundations upon which to build and benefit in all fields of theoretical and applied knowledge.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage describes the epistemological method of division and collection. The author explains that these operations are very useful or necessary in acquiring both sensory and deductive knowledge, whether theoretical or practical. Division means breaking down a universal into its particulars and kinds. Collection means gathering scattered particulars under their universal categories. The benefit of division is that it allows each part to be studied comprehensively. After studying the parts and deriving rulings, the process then collects similar rulings or similar things and formulates them into universal rules that cover individuals of a class, species, or genus. This collection provides a concise and precise summary of knowledge without losing the intended meaning or applicability. Moreover, the mind naturally seeks universal rules to avoid memorizing every particular. This enables the mind to apply general knowledge to new particulars it encounters. Ultimately, researchers extract universal laws and build upon them in all fields. The passage uses Islamic sciences illustrations implicitly, such as the terms 'rulings' (أحكام) which are typical of fiqh, and the notion of genus and species which is logical. The method described is foundational for sciences like fiqh (collecting rulings into universal principles) and kalam (formulating general theological rules).

Key Takeaway
Division and collection are complementary processes that transform scattered particulars into universal rules, streamlining knowledge and enabling its application to new cases.
