Analysis and Synthesis: Foundations of Systematic Inquiry
Author explains the processes of breaking down wholes and rebuilding them for knowledge and application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the operations of analysis and synthesis to a complex entity in an Islamic science, such as the human body or a legal ruling.

Core Idea
Analysis and synthesis are the twin engines of systematic inquiry in Islamic sciences.

This passage introduces you to two fundamental intellectual operations: analysis (al-taḥlīl) and synthesis (al-tarkīb). These are not abstract concepts but practical tools that scholars in Islamic medicine, jurisprudence, and theology use every day. By learning to break a whole into its parts and then reassemble them meaningfully, you gain a powerful method for understanding complex entities and creating new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Whole Entity
Begin by determining the subject you want to understand. In the passage, the example is the human being (insān). In other contexts, it could be a text like the Quran, a legal ruling, or a theological argument.
The whole must be a coherent entity with parts that can be distinguished.
2
Decompose into Parts (Analysis)
Divide the whole into its constituent parts. The author mentions both simple (basiṭa) and compound (murakkaba) parts. For the human, they are bodily organs and psychological elements. Study each part's attributes, characteristics, and functions, and how they interconnect.
Analysis is not merely listing; it is a deep study of each part in relation to the whole.
3
Examine Interconnections and Functions
After analyzing parts individually, look at how they relate to each other. Each part performs a specific role within the whole, and the whole's major function arises from their cooperation. For example, the heart and lungs work together in circulation.
This step ensures you understand the system, not just isolated components.
4
Synthesize: Reassemble or Create New Combinations
After analysis, you may either reassemble the original whole with deeper understanding or invent a new synthesis that serves a human purpose. The author emphasizes that synthesis considers suitability with human goals (ghāyah).
Synthesis can lead to practical applications, inventions, or new theories.
5
Apply General Rulings and Observe Special Cases
Finally, use the general findings from analysis and synthesis to make judgments and apply them in real situations. The medical researcher issues general rulings and then applies them to individual patients, adjusting for specific cases.
This connects theoretical knowledge to practical action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الوسائل النافعة جداً، أو الضرورية في كثير من الأحيان، في مجال اكتساب المعارف الحسية أو الاستنباطية النظرية أو العملية، عمليات التحليل والتركيب . ويقصد بالتحليل تجزئة الكل إلى أجزائه التي يتألف منها، بسيطة كانت أو مركبة، ودراسة كل جزء منها دراسة خاصة به، لمعرفة صفاته، وخصائصه، ووظائفه، ثم النظر في وجه ترابط الأجزاء بعضها ببعض، وأداء كل جزء منها وظيفته الخاصة به، بحسب موضعه من الكل، حتى اجتمع منها الكل فأدى وظيفته الكبرى القائمة على تعاون الأجزاء . وبعد عمليات التحليل للشيء الواحد، أو لعدد من الأشياء، وبعد النظر في صفات الأجزاء وخصائصها، وما يمكن أن تقوم به من وظائف، تأتي عملية إعادة التركيب، أو ابتكار تركيب جديد يفترضه التخيل، ملاحظاً الملاءمة بين التركيب الذي يتخيله وبين غاية من غايات الإنسان في الحياة . إن الباحث في طب الإنسان لا بد له من أن يُحلّل الإنسان إلى أجزائه التي يتألف منها، جسدية كانت أو نفسية، ثم يدرس كل عضو من أعضائه وكلّ جزء من أجزاء نفسه دراسة تشريحية تفصيلية؛ ويدرس اللحم والدم والعظم والجملة العصبية والخلايا ووظائفها وآثارها وعللها، ويعرف العناصر التي تتركب منها، وما يؤثر عليها، وما ينتج عنها، وما تقوم به من وظائف ضمن الجهاز الكلي . وفي دراسته لنفس الإنسان يدرس عناصرها الأساسية، وما يؤثر فيها، وعلاقاتها بالجسد وتأثيرها فيه، وتأثرها منه، وخدمات كلّ منها للآخر، وتعاونة معه . وبعد عمليات التحليل يصدر أحكامه العامة التي توصل إليها، ثم يجري تطبيقاته العملية وفق ما توصل إليه هو أو غيره من الباحثين من أحكام عامة، ووفق ما يلاحظ من حالات خاصة في واقع الشخص الذي يعالجه .
And among the very useful or often necessary means in the field of acquiring sensory or deductive knowledge, theoretical or practical, are the operations of analysis and synthesis. By analysis is meant dividing the whole into its constituent parts, whether simple or compound, and studying each part with its own study, to know its attributes, characteristics, and functions, then examining the manner of interconnection of the parts with each other, and each part performing its own function according to its position in the whole, until the whole combines and performs its major function based on the cooperation of the parts. After analysis of a single thing or a number of things, and after examining the attributes and characteristics of the parts, and what functions they can perform, comes the process of re-synthesis, or the invention of a new synthesis that imagination assumes, observing the suitability between the synthesis it imagines and one of the human goals in life. Indeed, the researcher in human medicine must analyze the human into its constituent parts, whether bodily or psychological, then study each of its organs and each part of its own being with a detailed anatomical study; and study flesh, blood, bone, the nervous system, cells, their functions, effects, and diseases, and know the elements they are composed of, what affects them, what results from them, and what functions they perform within the whole system. And in his study of the human soul, he studies its basic elements, what affects it, its relationship with the body, its influence on it, its being influenced by it, and the services of each to the other, and its cooperation with it. After analysis, he issues his general rulings that he has arrived at, then he carries out his practical applications according to what he or other researchers have arrived at of general rulings, and according to what he observes of special cases in the reality of the person he is treating.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains the operations of analysis and synthesis. The author states these are very useful or necessary for acquiring knowledge, both sensory and deductive. Analysis is defined as dividing the whole into its parts—simple or compound—studying each part's attributes, characteristics, and functions, and then examining how parts interconnect and each contributes to the whole's major function. After analysis, synthesis occurs: either reassembling the same parts or creating a new combination aligned with human goals. The author illustrates with human medicine: a researcher must analyze the human body and soul into parts, study them anatomically and functionally, then after analysis issue general rulings and apply them practically based on those rulings and observed cases. This method is foundational in Islamic sciences such as medicine (Tibb) and even in analyzing texts (like Quranic exegesis dividing verses into parts). The passage emphasizes that analysis is a precursor to synthesis and application.

Key Takeaway
Analysis and synthesis work together: analysis provides deep understanding of parts and their relations, while synthesis enables practical application and innovation for human benefit.

Reasoning: Immediate and Non-Immediate
Author divides reasoning into direct and indirect, listing types of each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given inference as either immediate or non-immediate and identify the subcategories within each.

Core Idea
Reasoning is either immediate (one step) or non-immediate (multiple steps).

The author opens with a concrete argument from Tawhid to show how premises lead to a conclusion, then formally divides all reasoning into two kinds. Immediate reasoning works with a single proposition, while non-immediate reasoning requires two or more premises. Understanding this division is your first step toward mastering the tools of inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Analyse the Tawhid example
Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Note that it uses a conditional and the denial of its consequent.
This argument is a conditional modus tollens.
2
Recognise it as non-immediate
Because it requires two premises, it falls under non-immediate reasoning, specifically the qiyas (syllogism) category.
3
Define immediate reasoning
Immediate reasoning needs only one proposition. From a single proposition, you can derive another through opposition, conversion, or implication of conditionals.
The passage lists: taqabul, 'aks, talazum al-shartiyyat.
4
List non-immediate types
Non-immediate reasoning includes qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Each will be studied separately.
5
Classify sample inferences
Given an inference, decide if it is immediate or non-immediate by counting the premises needed.
Practise with examples from Islamic sciences.

جـ ـ وكالتوصل إلى معرفة أن الله واحدٌ لا شريك له بملاحظة القضيتين التاليتين :
لو كان في السماء والأرض آلهة بحقٍّ غير الله لفسدتا (ببرهان العقل) لكنهما لم تفسدا (ببرهان المشاهدة)، فينتج أنه لا توجد آلهة متعدّدة معبودة بحقّ .

والاستدلال ينقسم إلى قسمين :

وهذا يكون فيما يسمى بأحكام القضايا (التقابل ـ العكس ـ تلازم الشرطيات) .

١ - التقابل .
٢ - العكس .
And arriving at knowledge that God is one, no partner with Him, by considering the following two propositions: If there were in the heavens and the earth gods besides God, they would be corrupted (by intellectual proof), but they have not been corrupted (by observational proof), so it results that there are no multiple gods worshipped rightly. And the existence of the Creator is originally accepted, but the proof here is for negating multiplicity. Reasoning is divided into two sections: The first section: immediate reasoning, which is that in which the researcher needs no more than one proposition. And this occurs in what is called judgments of propositions (al-taqabul, al-'aks, and the implication of conditionals). And by this section, reasoning is done from the truth of a proposition to the truth of another or its falsity, or from the falsity of a proposition to the truth of another or its falsity. And that is by virtue of rational opposition, or by virtue of the rules of conversion of the proposition which necessitates this conversion, or by virtue of rational implication which occurs in conditional propositions. And its explanation will come in its proper places. The second section: non-immediate reasoning, which is that in which the researcher needs more than one proposition until he reaches the desired conclusion. And this occurs in the following proofs: (al-qiyas, al-istiqra', al-tamthil). And for each of these proofs there is a specific investigation. Chapter One: Immediate reasoning. 1. Opposition. 2. Conversion.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage classifies reasoning into two broad categories and provides an illustrative example. The author begins with a concrete argument from Tawhid: the hypothetical syllogism that if multiple gods existed, the universe would be corrupted; since it is not corrupted, there are not multiple gods. This example shows how reasoning moves from premises to a conclusion. Then the author formally divides 'istidlal' (reasoning) into two sections. The first, direct or immediate reasoning, requires only a single proposition to infer something about another proposition. It operates through properties of propositions: opposition (taqabul), conversion ('aks), and implication among conditionals (talazum al-shartiyyat). The author notes that immediate reasoning can proceed from truth to truth, truth to falsity, falsity to truth, or falsity to falsity, depending on the logical relationship. The second section, indirect or non-immediate reasoning, requires more than one proposition to reach a conclusion. This includes syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). The passage ends by announcing that each of these proofs will be studied separately, and then introduces the chapter on immediate reasoning, which will cover opposition and conversion.

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is fundamentally of two types: immediate (single‑proposition) and non‑immediate (multi‑proposition); mastering this division is essential for understanding all later logical topics.

Three Types of Indirect Inference
Author lists three types of indirect inference: induction, syllogism, and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between induction, syllogism, and analogy as three types of indirect inference in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Three types of indirect inference: induction, syllogism, and analogy – each with a distinct logical structure.

This lesson introduces the three fundamental types of indirect inference (al-istidlal ghayr al-mubashir) as classified in classical Islamic logic. Understanding these categories will help you recognise different reasoning patterns in Islamic sciences such as Kalam, Fiqh, and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the Arabic text, note the three numbered items: الاستقراء (induction), القياس (syllogism), التمثيل (analogy). These are the only types of indirect inference listed.
Indirect inference requires a mediating step; direct inference is immediate.
2
Characterise each type by logical direction
Induction moves from particular to universal. Syllogism moves from universal to particular (or universal to universal). Analogy moves from particular to particular. This helps classify any argument you encounter.
In syllogism, the middle term connects premises; in analogy, a shared attribute ('illa) connects cases.
3
Apply Islamic examples
For induction: 'Every known intoxicant is forbidden' (from particular instances). For syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; so nabidh is forbidden.' For analogy: 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication; nabidh shares that cause; so nabidh is forbidden.'
These examples come from the commentary and are typical in Islamic logic.
4
Assess certainty level
Syllogism yields certain (yaqini) conclusions if premises are true. Induction is usually incomplete (naqis) and yields probability. Analogy is also probable unless the 'illa is certain. This distinction is crucial in Kalam and Usul.
Complete induction (istiqra' tamm) is rare; most induction is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
الاستدلال غير المباشر
١ - الاستقراء .
٢ - القياس .
٣ - التمثيل .
Chapter Two
Indirect inference
1 - Induction.
2 - Syllogism.
3 - Analogy.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a chapter on indirect inference (al-istidlal ghayr al-mubashir). In classical logic, indirect inference is reasoning that goes from known premises to a conclusion that is not immediately evident without a mediating step. The author enumerates three types: 

1. Induction (istiqra'): Reasoning from particular instances to a universal generalization. In Islamic logic, induction is often incomplete (istiqra' naqis) and does not yield certainty, but it is used in empirical sciences. For example, observing that every known intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden leads to the general rule that all intoxicants are forbidden, though this is not a rigorous proof. 

2. Syllogism (qiyas): Here, qiyas refers specifically to the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), which consists of two premises sharing a middle term that leads to a necessary conclusion. For instance: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. All khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, all khamr is forbidden.' This is the most certain form of indirect inference. 

3. Analogy (tamthil): Reasoning from one particular to another based on a shared attribute or cause ('illa). This is the basis of qiyas in Usul al-Fiqh but is distinguished here from syllogism. An example: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates. Nabidh (date wine) also intoxicates, so nabidh is forbidden.' The passage thus lays out the fundamental division of indirect inference into these three categories, which are foundational for the study of logic.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
القياس
التمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) التمثيل (Analogy)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Indirect inference has three forms: induction (istiqra'), syllogism (qiyas), and analogy (tamthil); only the categorical syllogism provides certain demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) Every created thing requires a creator, and the world is created, so the world requires a creator. (c) Khamr is forbidden because of intoxication, and nabidh also intoxicates, so nabidh is forbidden.
?

Three Elements of Experiment and Observation
Enumerates the three elements of experimental observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three elements of experimental observation as defined in the passage.

Core Idea
Experimental observation requires focus, interpretation, and inference.

Observation is not passive; it is a structured process. This passage breaks down experimental observation into three essential elements. Understanding them will help you see how Islamic scholars like Ibn al-Haytham systematically studied phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first element: directing attention
The passage states the observer must direct full attention to a specific aspect or property of the subject. This means focusing on one variable at a time, like Ibn al-Haytham isolating light reflection in his experiments.
2
Identify the second element: perceiving the meaning of the effect
After focused observation, the observer perceives the significance of the observed effect based on prior scientific experience. For example, noticing that a lens focuses light requires knowledge of optics.
3
Identify the third element: deducing judgments
Finally, the observer infers judgments after understanding relationships between the observed thing and other things. This is the step where conclusions are drawn, like concluding that light travels in straight lines.
The three elements are interdependent, not strictly sequential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعناصر التجربة والملاحظة تتلخص : ١ - بتوجيه الملاحظ انتباهه كله إلى ناحية أو صفة معينة من الشيء الموضوع للبحث، مع طرح الأسئلة المخبرية عليه . ٢ - ثم بإدراك الملاحظ المجرّب معنى الأثر الذي يشاهده بالملاحظة المركزة، وذلك في ضوء خبراته العلمية السابقة . ٣ - ثم باستنتاج ما يمكن استنتاجه من أحكام، بعد إدراك العلاقات المختلفة بين الشيء الملاحظ وغيره من الأشياء .
The elements of experiment and observation are summarized: 1- Directing the observer's full attention to a specific aspect or property of the subject under investigation, while posing laboratory questions to it. 2- Then the experimental observer perceives the meaning of the effect he observes through focused observation, in light of his previous scientific experiences. 3- Then deducing whatever judgments can be deduced, after perceiving the various relationships between the observed thing and other things.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage lists the three elements of the experimental observation process. First, the observer must focus all attention on a particular aspect of the subject and ask empirical questions. Second, he perceives the significance of the observed effect using his prior knowledge. Third, he infers judgments after understanding relationships. This breakdown shows that experimental observation is not haphazard but a disciplined procedure.

Key Takeaway
Experimental observation is a three-element process: focus, interpret, and infer – a framework used by Islamic scientists like Ibn al-Haytham.

Division of Speech: Mufrad and Jumlah
Author divides speech into mufrad and jumlah with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic expression as mufrad or jumlah based on the criteria given in the text.

Core Idea
Speech is either a simple expression or a complete sentence; only complete sentences convey a full meaning.

Before we can understand how debate works, we must classify the types of speech. The author divides speech that conveys meaning into two fundamental categories: mufrad (simple) and jumlah (sentence). In this lesson, you will learn to distinguish between them, including compound phrases that are not complete sentences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for division
Note that the division is based on whether the speech conveys a complete meaning (tam) on which silence is valid. Mufrad does not; jumlah does.
The definition of jumlah explicitly mentions 'conveys a complete meaning'.
2
Recognize types of mufrad
Mufrad includes single words (noun, verb, particle) and compound phrases that are not complete sentences, such as 'idafi' (genitive construct) and 'taqyidi' (restrictive compound).
Examples: 'كتاب الله' and 'الرسول الصادق' are not complete sentences.
3
Distinguish performative and informative jumlah
Jumlah is further divided into performative (insha'iyya) and informative (khabariyya). Informative sentences contain a tasdiq (assent), i.e., a logical proposition.
This distinction is crucial for debate, as we will see.
4
Apply classification to examples
Given an Arabic phrase, determine whether it is mufrad or jumlah based on whether it is a complete sentence. If it is a complete sentence, classify it as insha' or khabar.
Practice with phrases like 'الصلاة فرض' (informative) and 'صلوا' (performative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما تجري فيه المناظرة وما لا تجري فيه
ينقسم الكلام الدالُّ على معنىً إلى مفردٍ وجملة.
فالمفرد: هو كل ما كان من قبيل التصورات، فيدخل فيه الاسم والفعل والحرف، ويدخل فيه المركب الإضافي مثل: «كتابُ الله»، والمركب التقييدي مثل: «الرسول الصادق»، وكلُّ ما ليس بجملة تامة من المركبات الناقصة.
والجملة: كل كلام أفاد معنىً تاماً يصح السكوت عليه، وهي:
١ - إما أن تكون جملة إنشائية.
٢ - وإما أن تكون جملة خبرية، وهي الجملة التي تتضمن (تصديقاً) أي: القضية المنطقية التي حُكم فيها بإثبات أو نفي نسبة بين موضوع ومحمول (مسندٍ إليه ومسند).
والأصل في غير التصديق أن لا تجري فيه المناظرة، لأنه لا يشتمل على دعوى تكون محلاً للنزاع، أو يسوغ من الناحية الشكلية توجيه الاعتراض عليها، ولكن قد يستفسر عن معناه، أو عن المراد منه، وعند بيان المعنى أو بيان المراد يمكن توجيه الاعتراض، باعتبار أن ذلك تصديق قابل للاعتراض عليه.
فحين يستفسر (السائل) عن معنى كلمة، فيوضح (المعلّل) معناها، فإنه يكون قد قدّم بهذا التعريف اللفظي ادّعاء بأن معناها هو ما ذكره، عندئذٍ
يتسنَّى (للسائل) أن يناظر (المعلل) حول هذا الادعاء، فيطالبه بتصحيح النقل إن كان ناقلاً، أو بالدليل إن كان مدَّعياً.
What debate is conducted in and what it is not conducted in. Speech that indicates a meaning is divided into simple and sentence. The simple: it is everything that is of the kind of concepts, so it includes the noun, verb, and particle, and includes the genitive construct like 'book of Allah', and the restrictive compound like 'the truthful messenger', and everything that is not a complete sentence from the incomplete compounds. The sentence: every speech that conveys a complete meaning upon which silence is valid, and it: 1- either it is a performative sentence. 2- or it is an informative sentence, which is the sentence that contains (assent) i.e., the logical proposition in which there is a judgment of affirmation or negation of a relation between subject and predicate. And the principle regarding non-assent is that debate is not conducted in it, because it does not include a claim that is a locus for dispute, or it is not formally permissible to direct an objection against it, but its meaning or what is intended by it may be asked about, and upon clarification of the meaning or what is intended, it becomes possible to direct an objection, considering that that is an assent open to objection. For when the questioner asks about the meaning of a word, and the explainer clarifies its meaning, then he has by this verbal definition presented a claim that its meaning is what he mentioned; at that point it becomes possible for the questioner to debate the explainer about this claim, demanding correction of transmission if he is a transmitter, or evidence if he is a claimant.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage provides a foundational classification of speech for the art of dialectic (munazara). The author first divides speech that conveys meaning into two types: mufrad (simple) and jumlah (sentence). The mufrad includes not only single words (noun, verb, particle) but also composite phrases that are not complete sentences, such as 'book of Allah' (idafi) and 'the truthful messenger' (taqyidi). The jumlah is defined as a complete unit that can stand alone in communication. It is further divided into performative (insha'iyya) and informative (khabariyya). The informative sentence is equated with tasdiq (assent) – a logical proposition that affirms or denies a relation between subject and predicate. The core principle is then stated: by default, non-assent (i.e., concepts and performative sentences) is not subject to munazara because it does not contain a claim (da'wa) that can be disputed. However, if someone asks for clarification of a meaning and the explainer provides it, that explanation itself becomes a claim (e.g., 'this word means X'), which can then be debated. Thus, the passage establishes that debate always requires an explicit or implicit claim that can be affirmed or denied. The example of a questioner asking about a word's meaning illustrates how even a definition can become the subject of dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
مفرد
جملة
إنشائي
خبري
تصديق

D) بسم الله

Key Takeaway
Speech that conveys meaning is either mufrad (incomplete) or jumlah (complete); only jumlah can be a logical proposition (tasdiq).

Principle of Debate: Tasdiq and Claims
Author states debate applies primarily to tasdiq, and definitions can become claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only tasdiq (or claims arising from definitions) is the proper subject of munazara, and identify when a definition becomes a debatable claim.

Core Idea
Debate requires a claim; non-tasdiq lacks a claim, so it is not debatable until a definition is given.

Now that we know the types of speech, we need to know which type can be debated. The author establishes a key principle: by default, only informative sentences (tasdiq) are subject to munazara. However, even a simple definition can become a claim if it is presented as an answer to a question. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of tasdiq
Tasdiq is the informative sentence that affirms or denies a relation. It inherently contains a claim (da'wa) that can be true or false.
This is why it is the natural subject of debate.
2
Understand why non-tasdiq is not debatable
Non-tasdiq (mufrad, insha') does not contain a claim that can be accepted or rejected. There is no truth-value to argue over.
One cannot debate whether 'book of Allah' is true or false; it is just a phrase.
3
Analyze how definitions become claims
When a questioner asks for the meaning of a word, the explainer's definition is an assertion about what the word means. This is a claim (da'wa) because it can be correct or incorrect.
At that point, the definition becomes a tasdiq (proposition) open to debate.
4
Apply the principle to a debate scenario
Consider a debate about a legal ruling. The claim is a tasdiq. If someone asks 'What is qiyas?', the explainer's definition becomes a claim that can be challenged.
The questioner can then demand evidence or correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms and Two Types of Division in Disputation
Chapter on divisions in disputation: terminology and types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between division of a whole into its parts and division of a universal into its particulars, and identify the key terms al-maqsim and qasim.

Core Idea
Division is either of a whole into its parts or of a universal into its particulars.

This passage opens the chapter on divisions (taqseem) and how disputation proceeds concerning them. The author defines two key terms—the divisible thing (al-maqsim) and its coordinate parts (qasīm)—and then introduces the two fundamental types of division: dividing a concrete whole into its parts, and dividing a universal concept into its species. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper argumentation in Islamic disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of division
Locate in the passage the two types: division of the whole (al-kull) into its parts, and division of the universal (al-kullī) into its particulars. Note the Arabic phrases for each.
2
Define 'al-maqsim'
Find the definition: al-maqsim is the thing upon which division falls, from which parts are extracted. It is also called 'mawrid al-qisma' (locus of division).
3
Define 'qasīm'
Understand that each part relative to another part sharing the same maqsim is called a qasīm. This highlights the relational nature of coordinate divisions.
4
Analyse the example given
The passage gives a partial example: 'Water is oxygen...' (incomplete). Recognise this as the first type: dividing a whole (water) into its parts (oxygen, hydrogen).
The example is incomplete but illustrates the concept.

(٤) التَّقسِيماتُ والمُنَاظَرَةُ فِيهَا مصطلحات : المَقْسِم : هو الشيء الذي يقع عليه التقسيم فيستخرج منه أقسام، ويطلق عليه اسم (مورد القسمة)، وكل قسم بالنسبة إلى القسم الآخر المشارك له في المقسم يسمى (قسيماً) له . مقدمة : سبق معنا في البحوث المنطقية بيان التقسيم والجمع، والتحليل والتركيب باعتبارها طرائق من طرائق البحث العلمي، وعرفنا هنالك أن التقسيم يكون (للكلّ) إذ يقسم إلى أجزائه، و (للكليّ) إذ يقسم إلى جزئياته، وسبق أن عرفنا الفرق بين (الكلّ) و (الكلي) . ويعقد علماء آداب البحث والمناظرة باباً خاصاً للتقسيم ولضوابط المناظرة فيه، ويقسمونه إلى نوعين : النوع الأول : تقسيم (الكلّ) إلى أجزائه . النوع الثاني : تقسيم (الكليّ) إلى جزئياته . تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه : وأما تقسيم الكلّ إلى أجزائه : فيعرفونه بأنه تحصيل الحقيقة المركبة بذكر جميع أجزائها التي تتركب منها. كقول الكيميائي مثلاً : الماء أوكسجين
(4) Divisions and Disputation Regarding Them. Terminology: The divisible thing (al-maqsim) is the thing upon which division falls, so that parts are extracted from it, and it is called 'the locus of division'. And every part relative to the other part sharing in the divisible thing is called its 'coordinate division' (qasim). Introduction: We have previously covered in logical investigations the explanation of division and combination, analysis and synthesis as methods of scientific research. And there we knew that division is of the whole (al-kull) when it is divided into its parts, and of the universal (al-kulli) when it is divided into its particulars. And we previously knew the difference between the whole and the universal. The scholars of the etiquette of research and disputation devote a special chapter to division and the rules of disputation in it, and they divide it into two types: The first type: division of the whole into its parts. The second type: division of the universal into its particulars. Division of the whole into its parts: As for division of the whole into its parts, they define it as obtaining the composite reality by mentioning all its constituent parts. For example, the chemist says: Water is oxygen...

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the topic of divisions (taqseem) and how disputation proceeds regarding them. The author first defines key terms: 'al-maqsim' is the thing that gets divided, from which parts are extracted; it is also called 'mawrid al-qisma' (locus of division). Each part, in relation to another part that shares the same maqsim, is called a 'qasim' (coordinate division). Then the author provides a brief introduction, noting that division was previously discussed as a method of scientific research, and it can be of two kinds: division of a whole (al-kull) into its parts, and division of a universal (al-kulli) into its particulars. The difference between 'kull' (whole) and 'kulli' (universal) was previously established. Now, the scholars of adab al-bahth (etiquette of research) treat this topic separately for the rules of disputation. They divide division into two types: type one is division of a whole into its parts, and type two is division of a universal into its particulars. The passage then begins to explain the first type: division of a whole into its parts is defined as obtaining the composite reality by enumerating all its constituent parts. An example from chemistry is given: water is oxygen... (the text cuts off).

Division: Al-Maqsim and Its Two Types
Author defines al-maqsim and qasim, then introduces the two types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-maqsim and qasim, and classify a division as either division of the whole into parts or division of the universal into particulars.

Core Idea
Dividing a whole differs fundamentally from dividing a universal.

This passage introduces the core terminology of logical division: al-maqsim (the thing divided) and qasim (each relative part). It also reminds us that division applies to two distinct objects: the whole (al-kull) and the universal (al-kulli). Mastering this distinction is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where division is used to organize rulings and concepts.

Guided Path
1
Find the maqsim
Whenever a division is presented, first identify what is being divided (the maqsim). This is the object that yields the parts.
Also referred to as mawrid al-qisma.
2
Identify the qasim
Each resulting part, when contrasted with another part of the same maqsim, is called a qasim. Note that qasim is a relational term.
A part is only a qasim relative to another part.
3
Determine if the maqsim is a whole or a universal
If the maqsim is a whole (kull), its parts are integral components. If it is a universal (kulli), its parts are particular instances under it. This decides the type of division.
The author states this distinction was previously taught.
4
Classify the division
Using the above, classify the division as either type 1 (whole → parts) or type 2 (universal → particulars). This classification affects the logical rules that apply.
The rules of disputation (adab al-bahth) treat these two types differently.

And we previously learned the difference between al-kull and al-kulli. The author first defines 'al-maqsim' (the divided) as the thing upon which division falls, from which parts are extracted; it is also called 'mawrid al-qisma' (the object of division). Division of the Whole: Definition and Conditions
Author defines division of the whole and its two necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given division of a whole satisfies the two conditions of comprehensive coverage and mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
A valid division of a whole must cover all parts and keep them distinct from each other and the whole.

This passage defines the first type of division—division of the whole into its parts. You will learn its precise conditions and how to spot invalid divisions. This tool is used in Islamic sciences to classify rulings, definitions, and legal categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maqsim and its parts
Find the whole being divided (e.g., water, chair) and list its stated parts (e.g., hydrogen, oxygen; wood, nails, form).
2
Check comprehensiveness (hasir)
Does the division list all constituent parts? Any missing part makes the division incomplete. For water, only hydrogen and oxygen; for a chair, form is also needed.
3
Check distinctness (mubayin)
Ensure each part is separate from the others and from the whole. No part alone should be called by the whole name (e.g., oxygen alone is not water).
4
Evaluate validity
If both conditions are satisfied, the division is valid; otherwise, it is invalid and open to objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تقسيم الكلّ إلى أجزائه : فيعرفونه بأنه تحصيل الحقيقة المركبة بذكر جميع أجزائها التي تتركب منها. كقول الكيميائي مثلاً : الماء أوكسجين وهيدروجين متحدان، فكل جزء من هذين الجزءين عنصر يدخل في المركب الذي هو الماء، وليس هو وحده ماءً، وهذان الجزءان هما جميع أجزاء الماء الصافي. وكقول النجار: الكرسي خشب ومسامير وهيئة خاصة. ويشترط لصحة تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه شرطان: الشرط الأول: أن يكون التقسيم حاصراً، أي: أن يكون جامعاً لجميع أجزاء المَقْسِم، مانعاً من دخول أي جزء ليس هو من أجزائه. الشرط الثاني: أن يكون كل قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام، ومبايناً للمَقْسِم، باعتبار أن (الكل) مباين لأي جزء من أجزائه، فلا يقال على الأوكسجين وحده أو على الهيدروجين وحده: ماء، ولا يقال على الخشب وحده أو على المسامير: كرسي، للتباين بين الكل وأجزائه. فإذا استوفى تقسيم الكلّ لأجزائه هذين الشرطين، كان تقسيماً صحيحاً، لا يتوجه له الاعتراض بالفساد.
As for the division of the whole into its parts: they define it as the attainment of the composite reality by mentioning all of its constituent parts. For example, a chemist says: water is oxygen and hydrogen combined. Each of these two parts is an element that enters into the compound that is water, and it alone is not water. These two parts are all the parts of pure water. And as a carpenter says: a chair is wood, nails, and a specific form. Two conditions are required for the validity of the division of the whole into its parts: The first condition: that the division be comprehensive (hasir), i.e., encompassing all parts of the maqsim, preventing the entry of any part that is not among its parts. The second condition: that each part be distinct (mubayin) from the other parts, and distinct from the maqsim, considering that the whole is distinct from any of its parts. So one does not say of oxygen alone or hydrogen alone: water, nor does one say of wood alone or nails alone: chair, due to the distinctness between the whole and its parts. If the division of the whole into its parts fulfills these two conditions, it is a valid division, not subject to objection of invalidity.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of division: division of the whole into its parts. The definition: it is the attainment of the composite reality by mentioning all its constituent parts. Two examples are given: a chemist divides water into oxygen and hydrogen (both combined form water, neither alone is water), and a carpenter divides a chair into wood, nails, and a specific form. Two conditions for validity are then stated. The first condition: the division must be comprehensive (hasir), meaning it encompasses all parts of the maqsim and excludes any extraneous parts. The second condition: each part must be distinct (mubayin) from the other parts and from the whole itself, since the whole is distinct from any part. The examples illustrate: oxygen alone is not water, wood alone is not a chair. When both conditions are met, the division is valid and not open to objections.

Key Terms
تقسيم الكل
حاصر
مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A valid division of the whole must be comprehensive (covering all parts) and mutually exclusive (each part distinct from others and the whole).

Dividing a Universal into Its Particulars
Defines division of the universal with a grammatical example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of dividing a universal into its particulars by adding restrictions, using an example from an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Division of a universal creates mutually exclusive subcategories by adding distinct restrictions.

When a universal concept encompasses many instances, we often need to break it into its constituent types. This division is not about physical parts but about logical subclasses. The passage explains this process and gives a concrete example from Arabic grammar: the division of 'kalima' (word) into noun, verb, and particle by adding restrictions such as 'indicates meaning' and 'time is part of meaning'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal (al-maqsim)
Find the single concept that will be divided. In the example, it is 'kalima' (word). In Islamic sciences, this could be 'hukm' (ruling), 'salah' (prayer), etc.
2
Determine the restrictions (quyud)
Look for the conditions or attributes that will separate the universal into different parts. The restrictions must be distinct and mutually exclusive. For kalima, the first restriction is 'indicates a meaning in itself'.
3
Apply the restrictions sequentially
Start with the broadest restriction to create a binary split, then apply further restrictions within each branch. In the passage, first split: indicates meaning (noun/verb) vs. no meaning (particle). Then within indicates meaning, split by time as part of meaning (verb) or not (noun).
4
Verify mutual exclusivity and completeness
Ensure that each resulting part is distinct and that together they cover all instances of the universal. In the kalima example, every word is either a particle, verb, or noun, and no word belongs to two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته: فيعرفونه بأنه ضم قيود متباينة أو متخالفة إلى المَقْسِم لتحصيل أقسام متباينة أو متخالفة بعدد تلك القيود، كقول النحوي في تقسيم الكلمة: (الكلمة) إن لم تدل على معنىً في نفسها فهي (الحرف). وإن دلت على معنى في نفسها: فإن كان الزمن جزءاً من معناها، فهي (الفعل). وإن لم يكن الزمن جزءاً من معناها، فهي (الاسم).
As for the division of the universal into its particulars: they define it as the addition of distinct or differing restrictions to the maqsim to obtain distinct or differing parts according to the number of those restrictions. For example, the grammarian's division of the word (kalima): If the word does not indicate a meaning in itself, it is a particle (harf). If it indicates a meaning in itself: then if time is part of its meaning, it is a verb (fi'l). If time is not part of its meaning, it is a noun (ism).
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of division: division of the universal into its particulars. Definition: it is the addition of distinct or differing restrictions (quyud) to the maqsim to obtain distinct or differing parts equal in number to those restrictions. A clear example from Arabic grammar is the division of kalima (word) into its three types: harf (particle), fi'l (verb), and ism (noun). The division proceeds by applying restrictions: first, whether the word indicates a meaning in itself or not. If not, it is harf. If it does, then check if time (zaman) is part of its meaning. If yes, it is fi'l; if no, it is ism. Each restriction distinguishes one type from another, and the types are mutually exclusive. This illustrates how a universal concept (kalima) is divided into its particular sub-categories by adding differentiating attributes.

Key Terms
تقسيم الكلي
قيد
الكلمة

Key Takeaway
Division of a universal into its particulars is achieved by adding distinct restrictions, yielding mutually exclusive and exhaustive subcategories.

Two Conditions for Valid Division of a Whole into Its Parts
Author defines division of whole into parts and states two necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given division of a whole into its parts satisfies the conditions of exhaustiveness and distinctness, and apply them to Islamic classification examples.

Core Idea
Valid division requires covering every part and keeping each part separate from the whole and other parts.

This passage introduces a fundamental logical operation: breaking down a whole into its constituent parts. The author gives two everyday examples—water and chairs—to show that a whole is not merely the sum of its parts. He then lays down two essential conditions for any valid division: it must be exhaustive (ḥāṣir) and each part must be distinct (mubāyin) from the other parts and from the whole itself. These conditions are used across Islamic sciences to ensure precise classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole (al-maqsim) being divided.
Find the thing that is being broken down into parts. In the passage, the wholes are water and a chair. Each whole is a composite of multiple components.
2
Check exhaustiveness (ḥāṣir).
Ask: Does the division include all necessary parts? Is anything missing? Does it include anything that is not a true part? The condition requires that the division covers all parts and excludes non-parts.
For water, oxygen and hydrogen are the only parts; for the chair, wood, nails, and form are the parts.
3
Check distinctness (mubāyin).
Ask: Is each part separate from every other part? Is each part separate from the whole? The whole is not identical to any part.
Oxygen alone is not water; wood alone is not a chair. The parts are mutually exclusive.
4
Apply the two conditions to an Islamic science example.
Take a real classification from fiqh, usul, or kalam. For instance, the pillars of prayer (arkān al-ṣalāh) include takbīr, qirāʼah, rukūʻ, sujūd, etc. Check if the list is exhaustive (no pillar omitted) and if each part is distinct (e.g., qirāʼah and rukūʻ are separate).
If the division fails either condition, it is invalid and open to objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهيدروجين متحدان، فكل جزء من هذين الجزءين عنصر يدخل في المركب الذي هو الماء، وليس هو وحده ماءً، وهذان الجزءان هما جميع أجزاء الماء الصافي. وكقول النجار: الكرسي خشب ومسامير وهيئة خاصة. ويشترط لصحة تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه شرطان: الشرط الأول: أن يكون التقسيم حاصراً، أي: أن يكون جامعاً لجميع أجزاء المَقْسِم، مانعاً من دخول أي جزء ليس هو من أجزائه. الشرط الثاني: أن يكون كل قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام، ومبايناً للمَقْسِم، باعتبار أن (الكل) مباين لأي جزء من أجزائه، فلا يقال على الأوكسجين وحده أو على الهيدروجين وحده: ماء، ولا يقال على الخشب وحده أو على المسامير: كرسي، للتباين بين الكل وأجزائه. فإذا استوفى تقسيم الكلّ لأجزائه هذين الشرطين، كان تقسيماً صحيحاً، لا يتوجه له الاعتراض بالفساد.
Oxygen and hydrogen are united, so each part of these two parts is an element that enters into the compound which is water, but it alone is not water, and these two parts are all the parts of pure water. And like the carpenter's saying: a chair is wood, nails, and a special form. Two conditions are required for the validity of dividing a whole into its parts: The first condition: that the division be exhaustive, i.e., that it includes all parts of the divided whole and prevents the entry of any part that is not among its parts. The second condition: that each part be distinct from the other parts and distinct from the whole, considering that the whole is distinct from any of its parts, so one does not say of oxygen alone or of hydrogen alone: water, nor does one say of wood alone or of nails: a chair, due to the distinctness between the whole and its parts. If the division of the whole into its parts fulfills these two conditions, it is a valid division and is not open to objection of corruption.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical operation of dividing a whole into its constituent parts. The author begins by giving two examples: water (composed of oxygen and hydrogen) and a chair (composed of wood, nails, and a form). These examples illustrate that a whole is not simply the sum of its parts; each part is a component but not identical to the whole. The passage then states two necessary conditions for a valid division. The first condition is that the division must be exhaustive, covering all parts of the whole and excluding anything extraneous. The second condition requires that each part be mutually exclusive from the other parts and also distinct from the whole itself. The author clarifies this by noting that the whole is different from any of its parts taken individually—for instance, oxygen alone is not water, and wood alone is not a chair. When both conditions are satisfied, the division is considered correct and immune to objections of invalidity. This is a foundational concept in logic used in various Islamic sciences, such as dividing the act of prayer (ṣalāh) into its obligatory parts (arkān) which must be exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Division and Classification

The Three Conditions for a Valid Division of a Universal
Defining division of a universal and its three necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given division of a universal into its particulars satisfies the three conditions of exhaustiveness, absolute specificity, and mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
A valid logical division must be exhaustive, its parts fully contained, and no two parts overlap.

In Islamic jurisprudence, dividing legal rulings into categories like wājib, mandūb, mubāḥ, etc., requires precise logical division. If the division is flawed, rulings may be misapplied. This lesson teaches you the three conditions that guarantee a sound division, using examples from grammar and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal and its divisions
Look at the examples: 'word' is divided into particle, verb, noun; 'body' is divided into mineral, plant, animal. Find the universal (al-maqsim) and the listed divisions (aqsām).
2
Check exhaustiveness
The division must cover all possible subcategories (condition 1). In the body example, are mineral, plant, and animal the only types of body? In classical logic, yes. Verify that no type is omitted.
3
Check absolute specificity
Each division must be absolutely more specific (akhaṣṣ muṭlaq) than the universal. For 'animal' and 'body': every animal is a body, but not every body is an animal. This condition prevents equality, broader categories, or overlap.
4
Check mutual exclusivity
No two divisions may share a member (condition 3). In the word example, can a word be both a noun and a verb? No, by definition they are distinct. Ensure no overlap.
5
Apply to a new example
Test the division of legal rulings (aḥkām) into wājib, mandūb, mubāḥ, makrūh, ḥarām. Check each condition: are they exhaustive? Is each absolutely more specific than 'ruling'? Are they mutually exclusive?
Look for potential violations: e.g., some classifications consider makrūh as a subset of mubāḥ, which would break condition 2 or 3.

وأما تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته: فيعرفونه بأنه ضم قيود متباينة أو متخالفة إلى المَقْسِم لتحصيل أقسام متباينة أو متخالفة بعدد تلك القيود، كقول النحوي في تقسيم الكلمة: (الكلمة) إن لم تدل على معنىً في نفسها فهي (الحرف). وإن دلت على معنى في نفسها: فإن كان الزمن جزءاً من معناها، فهي (الفعل). وإن لم يكن الزمن جزءاً من معناها، فهي (الاسم). فالكلمة : حرف وفعل واسم . وكقول المنطقي في تقسيم الجسم : (الجسم) إن كان غير نام فهو (الجماد) . وإن كان نامياً : فإن كان غير متحرك بالإرادة فهو (النبات) . وإن كان متحركاً بالإرادة فهو (الحيوان) . فالجسم : جماد ونبات وحيوان . ويشترط لصحة تقسيم الكليّ إلى جزئياته ثلاثة شروط : الشرط الأول : أن يكون حاصراً ، أي : أن يكون جامعاً لكل الأقسام التي ينقسم إليها الكليّ ، وأن يكون مانعاً من دخول ما ليس من أقسامه . الشرط الثاني : أن يكون كل قسم أخص مطلقاً من المقسم ، أي : لا مساوياً له ، ولا أعم مطلقاً منه ، ولا مبايناً له ، ولا أعم من وجه وأخص من وجه . الشرط الثالث : أن يكون كلُّ قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام ، أي : ليس بينه وبين بعض الأقسام الأخرى مساواة ، ولا عموم أو خصوص مطلق ، ولا عموم وخصوص من وجه . فإذا استوفى تقسيم الكلي لجزئياته هذه الشروط ، كان تقسيماً صحيحاً ، لا يتوجه له الاعتراض بالفساد . أنواع تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته : باعتبارات مختلفات نلاحظ أن تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته يتنوع إلى أنواع ، فبالنظر إلى تباين الأقسام أو تخالفها له نوعان :
As for the division of a universal into its particulars: they define it as adding distinct or different restrictions to the divided thing to obtain distinct or different parts according to the number of those restrictions. Like the grammarian's statement in dividing the word: (The word) if it does not indicate a meaning by itself, it is a (particle). And if it indicates a meaning by itself: if time is part of its meaning, it is a (verb). And if time is not part of its meaning, it is a (noun). So the word: particle, verb, and noun. And like the logician's statement in dividing body: (Body) if it is not growing, it is (mineral). And if it is growing: if it is not moving by will, it is (plant). And if it is moving by will, it is (animal). So body: mineral, plant, and animal. Three conditions are required for the validity of dividing a universal into its particulars: The first condition: that it be exhaustive, i.e., it includes all the divisions into which the universal is divided, and prevents the entry of what is not among its divisions. The second condition: that each division be absolutely more specific than the divided thing, i.e., not equal to it, nor absolutely more general than it, nor distinct from it, nor more general in one respect and more specific in another. The third condition: that each division be distinct from the other divisions, i.e., there is not between it and any other division equality, nor absolute generality or specificity, nor generality and specificity in one respect. If the division of the universal into its particulars fulfills these conditions, it is a valid division and is not open to objection of corruption. Types of division of a universal into its particulars: Considering various aspects, we note that the division of a universal into its particulars is diversified into types. For with respect to the distinctness or difference of the divisions, it has two types:
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage explains the logical operation of dividing a universal concept into its subordinate particulars. The author first defines it as the addition of distinct or different restrictions (quyūd) to the universal (maqsim) to generate mutually exclusive parts. Two classic examples are given: the grammarian's division of 'word' into particle, verb, and noun, and the logician's division of 'body' into mineral, plant, and animal. These illustrate how different restrictions (e.g., 'indicates meaning' and 'time') produce distinct subdivisions. Three conditions are necessary for a valid division. The first is exhaustiveness, as before, covering all possible divisions. The second condition requires that each particular be absolutely more specific than the universal: it cannot be equal, more general, completely distinct, or overlapping in generality. For instance, 'animal' is absolutely more specific than 'body'. The third condition demands that the particulars themselves be mutually exclusive without equality, subsumption, or overlap. When these conditions are met, the division is sound. Finally, the author notes that there are two types based on whether the divisions are truly distinct (mutabāyina) or merely different (mukhtalifa). This classification is crucial in fields like fiqh, where one might divide legal rulings into obligatory, recommended, permissible, etc., ensuring no overlap.

Division and Classification

Three Conditions for a Correct Division of a Universal
Author lists three conditions for a correct division of a universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the three conditions (hasir, akhas mutlaq, mubayin) for a valid logical division of a universal in the context of Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A valid division must be exhaustive, each part more specific than the whole, and parts mutually exclusive.

In this lesson, we learn the three essential conditions for logically dividing a universal term into its particulars. These conditions ensure that the division is complete and each part is distinct and properly related to the whole. The passage provides the criteria and an example from the division of 'body' into mineral, plant, and animal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal being divided
Find the term (kulli) that is being partitioned. In the passage, it is 'body' (al-jism) divided into mineral, plant, animal.
The universal must be a general concept that applies to each part.
2
Check if the division is exhaustive (hasir)
Verify that the list of parts includes all possible subcategories of the universal and excludes anything that is not a genuine part. The body example includes three categories covering all bodies.
If a part is missing, the division is incomplete and invalid.
3
Verify each part is absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaq) than the divided term
Each part must be a proper subset of the universal; it cannot be equal, more general, opposite, or overlapping in a partial way. For body: mineral, plant, and animal are all more specific than 'body'.
For example, 'body' cannot be a part because it would equal the whole.
4
Ensure mutual exclusivity (mubayin) between parts
Confirm that no two parts share definitional overlap. In the body example, something cannot be both mineral and plant, etc.
Overlap would cause confusion and logical error.

فالكلمة : حرف وفعل واسم . وكقول المنطقي في تقسيم الجسم : (الجسم) إن كان غير نام فهو (الجماد) . وإن كان نامياً : فإن كان غير متحرك بالإرادة فهو (النبات) . وإن كان متحركاً بالإرادة فهو (الحيوان) . فالجسم : جماد ونبات وحيوان . ويشترط لصحة تقسيم الكليّ إلى جزئياته ثلاثة شروط : الشرط الأول : أن يكون حاصراً ، أي : أن يكون جامعاً لكل الأقسام التي ينقسم إليها الكليّ ، وأن يكون مانعاً من دخول ما ليس من أقسامه . الشرط الثاني : أن يكون كل قسم أخص مطلقاً من المقسم ، أي : لا مساوياً له ، ولا أعم مطلقاً منه ، ولا مبايناً له ، ولا أعم من وجه وأخص من وجه . الشرط الثالث : أن يكون كلُّ قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام ، أي : ليس بينه وبين بعض الأقسام الأخرى مساواة ، ولا عموم أو خصوص مطلق ، ولا عموم وخصوص من وجه . فإذا استوفى تقسيم الكلي لجزئياته هذه الشروط ، كان تقسيماً صحيحاً ، لا يتوجه له الاعتراض بالفساد . أنواع تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته : باعتبارات مختلفات نلاحظ أن تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته يتنوع إلى أنواع ، فبالنظر إلى تباين الأقسام أو تخالفها له نوعان : ١ - التقسيم الحقيقي . ٢ - التقسيم الاعتباري . وبالنظر إلى انحصار المَقْسِم في الأقسام المذكورة له نوعان : ١ - التقسيم العقلي . ٢ - التقسيم الاستقرائي . وفيما يلي شرح هذه الأنواع: التقسيم الحقيقي : هو ما كانت الأقسام فيه متباينة في العقل، ومتباينة في الخارج، وذلك بأن يحدَّ العقل لكل قسم منها حقيقةً تباين حقيقة ما عداه من الأقسام، وبها يتميز عن كل واحد منها، ثم لا يكون في الواقع خارج الذهن قسمٌ واحد منها يمكن أن تتحقق فيه حقيقة من حقائق الأقسام الأخرى المباينة له ولو باعتبارات مختلفات . الأمثلة : ١ - العدد ينقسم إلى زوج وفرد. فالعقلُ يقضي بهذه القسمة، ويضع لكل قسم حقيقة تميزه عن القسم الآخر، وبهذه الحقيقة تظهر مباينته لقسيمه، وليس من الممكن أن يُوجد في الواقع عدد هو زوج وهو فرد بآن واحد، مهما اختلفت الاعتبارات . ٢ - الزاوية في الهندسة تنقسم إلى حادة وقائمة ومنفرجة . فالعقل يقضي بهذه القسمة، ويضع لكل قسم من الأقسام الثلاثة حقيقة تميزه عن القسمين الآخرين، وبهذه الحقيقة تظهر مباينته لكلٍّ منهما، وليس من الممكن أن توجد في الواقع زاوية هي حادة وقائمة معاً، أو حادة ومنفرجة معاً، أو قائمة ومنفرجة معاً، مهما اختلفت الاعتبارات .
The word: letter, verb, and noun. And like the logician's saying in the division of body: (Body) if it is not growing then it is (mineral). And if it is growing: if it is not moving voluntarily then it is (plant). And if it is moving voluntarily then it is (animal). So body: mineral, plant, and animal. And three conditions are required for the correctness of the division of a universal into its particulars: The first condition: that it be encompassing, i.e., that it gather all the divisions into which the universal is divided, and that it prevent the entry of what is not among its divisions. The second condition: that each division be absolutely more specific than the divided term, i.e., not equal to it, nor absolutely more general than it, nor opposite to it, nor more general in one respect and more specific in another. The third condition: that each division be opposite to the others, i.e., there is no equality between it and some other divisions, nor absolute generality or specificity, nor generality and specificity in one respect. When the division of a universal into its particulars fulfills these conditions, it is a correct division, and no objection of corruption can be directed at it. Types of division of a universal into its particulars: Considering different considerations, we see that the division of a universal into its particulars diversifies into types, so with respect to the mutual exclusivity of the divisions or their difference, it has two types: 1- Real division. 2- Conceptual division. And with respect to the confinement of the divided term to the mentioned divisions, it has two types: 1- Rational division. 2- Inductive division. Following is the explanation of these types: Real division: it is that in which the divisions are mutually exclusive in the mind and mutually exclusive in the external world, such that the mind defines for each division a reality that is opposite to the reality of the other divisions, and by which it is distinguished from each of them, and then in reality outside the mind there is no single division that can have the reality of one of the other opposite divisions realized in it even with different considerations. Examples: 1- Number is divided into even and odd. The mind judges this division, and posits for each division a reality that distinguishes it from the other division, and by this reality its opposition to its counterpart appears, and it is not possible that in reality a number exists that is even and odd at the same time, no matter how different the considerations. 2- The angle in geometry is divided into acute, right, and obtuse. The mind judges this division, and posits for each of the three divisions a reality that distinguishes it from the other two, and by this reality its opposition to each of them appears, and it is not possible that in reality an angle exists that is acute and right together, or acute and obtuse together, or right and obtuse together, no matter how different the considerations.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and types of dividing a universal (kulli) into its particulars (juz'iyyat). It begins with an example from logic: the division of 'body' into mineral, plant, and animal, preceded by a brief division of the word into letter, verb, and noun. These examples illustrate how a universal term can be divided into mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive subcategories. The author then lists three necessary conditions for a sound division: (1) The division must be encompassing (hasir), meaning it includes all possible subcategories and excludes anything that does not belong. (2) Each part must be absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaq) than the divided term — i.e., it cannot be equal to, more general, opposite, or overlapping in a partial way. (3) Each part must be mutually exclusive (mubayin) with every other part, so that no two parts overlap in definition or reality. When these conditions are met, the division is correct (sahih) and immune to the objection of being corrupted (fasad). Next, the author categorizes division from two perspectives. The first perspective considers whether the parts are distinct in both mind and reality (real division, taqsim haqiqi) or only by mental or conceptual consideration (conceptual division, taqsim i'tibari). The second perspective considers whether the division is exhaustive based on rational analysis alone (rational division, taqsim 'aqli) or based on empirical investigation (inductive division, taqsim istiqra'i). The passage then elaborates on real division, providing two clear examples: the division of number into even and odd, and the division of angles into acute, right, and obtuse. In both cases, the parts are mutually exclusive in both thought and actual existence — no number can be both even and odd, and no angle can possess two different measures simultaneously. This real division is contrasted with conceptual division, which is not explained here but is implied to be a division based on mental distinction without external reality. The passage serves as a foundational discussion for logical classification and is critical for understanding how to correctly partition universals in the sciences.

Types of Division: Real, Conceptual, Rational, Inductive
Author defines and distinguishes types of division: real, conceptual, rational, inductive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given logical division as real or conceptual, and as rational or inductive, based on the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
Real division requires mutual exclusivity in both mind and reality; rational division is exhaustive by reason alone.

This lesson explores the four types of division: real (haqiqi) vs. conceptual (i'tibari) based on whether parts are distinct in reality, and rational ('aqli) vs. inductive (istiqra'i) based on how exhaustiveness is achieved. The passage gives clear examples: number into even and odd is real and rational; angle into acute, right, and obtuse is also real. These distinctions are crucial for precise logical classification in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the division is real or conceptual
Check whether the parts are mutually exclusive both in the mind (conceptually) and in external reality. If yes, it is real (haqiqi). If the distinction is merely mental without external separation, it is conceptual (i'tibari).
The passage says real division requires exclusivity in both 'aql and kharij.
2
Check if the division is rational or inductive
Determine if the exhaustive list of parts is derived from rational analysis alone (rational) or from empirical enumeration (inductive). The passage does not elaborate on inductive, but it contrasts rational division, which is a priori.
Rational division is based on the nature of the universal; inductive relies on observation.
3
Apply to the examples from the passage
The division of number into even and odd is real because no number can be both, and rational because it follows from the concept of number. The division of angle into acute, right, and obtuse is similarly real and rational.
The passage uses geometry as an example, but in Islamic logic, these principles apply broadly.

فالكلمة : حرف وفعل واسم . وكقول المنطقي في تقسيم الجسم : (الجسم) إن كان غير نام فهو (الجماد) . وإن كان نامياً : فإن كان غير متحرك بالإرادة فهو (النبات) . وإن كان متحركاً بالإرادة فهو (الحيوان) . فالجسم : جماد ونبات وحيوان . ويشترط لصحة تقسيم الكليّ إلى جزئياته ثلاثة شروط : الشرط الأول : أن يكون حاصراً ، أي : أن يكون جامعاً لكل الأقسام التي ينقسم إليها الكليّ ، وأن يكون مانعاً من دخول ما ليس من أقسامه . الشرط الثاني : أن يكون كل قسم أخص مطلقاً من المقسم ، أي : لا مساوياً له ، ولا أعم مطلقاً منه ، ولا مبايناً له ، ولا أعم من وجه وأخص من وجه . الشرط الثالث : أن يكون كلُّ قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام ، أي : ليس بينه وبين بعض الأقسام الأخرى مساواة ، ولا عموم أو خصوص مطلق ، ولا عموم وخصوص من وجه . فإذا استوفى تقسيم الكلي لجزئياته هذه الشروط ، كان تقسيماً صحيحاً ، لا يتوجه له الاعتراض بالفساد . أنواع تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته : باعتبارات مختلفات نلاحظ أن تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته يتنوع إلى أنواع ، فبالنظر إلى تباين الأقسام أو تخالفها له نوعان : ١ - التقسيم الحقيقي . ٢ - التقسيم الاعتباري . وبالنظر إلى انحصار المَقْسِم في الأقسام المذكورة له نوعان : ١ - التقسيم العقلي . ٢ - التقسيم الاستقرائي . وفيما يلي شرح هذه الأنواع: التقسيم الحقيقي : هو ما كانت الأقسام فيه متباينة في العقل، ومتباينة في الخارج، وذلك بأن يحدَّ العقل لكل قسم منها حقيقةً تباين حقيقة ما عداه من الأقسام، وبها يتميز عن كل واحد منها، ثم لا يكون في الواقع خارج الذهن قسمٌ واحد منها يمكن أن تتحقق فيه حقيقة من حقائق الأقسام الأخرى المباينة له ولو باعتبارات مختلفات . الأمثلة : ١ - العدد ينقسم إلى زوج وفرد. فالعقلُ يقضي بهذه القسمة، ويضع لكل قسم حقيقة تميزه عن القسم الآخر، وبهذه الحقيقة تظهر مباينته لقسيمه، وليس من الممكن أن يُوجد في الواقع عدد هو زوج وهو فرد بآن واحد، مهما اختلفت الاعتبارات . ٢ - الزاوية في الهندسة تنقسم إلى حادة وقائمة ومنفرجة . فالعقل يقضي بهذه القسمة، ويضع لكل قسم من الأقسام الثلاثة حقيقة تميزه عن القسمين الآخرين، وبهذه الحقيقة تظهر مباينته لكلٍّ منهما، وليس من الممكن أن توجد في الواقع زاوية هي حادة وقائمة معاً، أو حادة ومنفرجة معاً، أو قائمة ومنفرجة معاً، مهما اختلفت الاعتبارات .
The word: letter, verb, and noun. And like the logician's saying in the division of body: (Body) if it is not growing then it is (mineral). And if it is growing: if it is not moving voluntarily then it is (plant). And if it is moving voluntarily then it is (animal). So body: mineral, plant, and animal. And three conditions are required for the correctness of the division of a universal into its particulars: The first condition: that it be encompassing, i.e., that it gather all the divisions into which the universal is divided, and that it prevent the entry of what is not among its divisions. The second condition: that each division be absolutely more specific than the divided term, i.e., not equal to it, nor absolutely more general than it, nor opposite to it, nor more general in one respect and more specific in another. The third condition: that each division be opposite to the others, i.e., there is no equality between it and some other divisions, nor absolute generality or specificity, nor generality and specificity in one respect. When the division of a universal into its particulars fulfills these conditions, it is a correct division, and no objection of corruption can be directed at it. Types of division of a universal into its particulars: Considering different considerations, we see that the division of a universal into its particulars diversifies into types, so with respect to the mutual exclusivity of the divisions or their difference, it has two types: 1- Real division. 2- Conceptual division. And with respect to the confinement of the divided term to the mentioned divisions, it has two types: 1- Rational division. 2- Inductive division. Following is the explanation of these types: Real division: it is that in which the divisions are mutually exclusive in the mind and mutually exclusive in the external world, such that the mind defines for each division a reality that is opposite to the reality of the other divisions, and by which it is distinguished from each of them, and then in reality outside the mind there is no single division that can have the reality of one of the other opposite divisions realized in it even with different considerations. Examples: 1- Number is divided into even and odd. The mind judges this division, and posits for each division a reality that distinguishes it from the other division, and by this reality its opposition to its counterpart appears, and it is not possible that in reality a number exists that is even and odd at the same time, no matter how different the considerations. 2- The angle in geometry is divided into acute, right, and obtuse. The mind judges this division, and posits for each of the three divisions a reality that distinguishes it from the other two, and by this reality its opposition to each of them appears, and it is not possible that in reality an angle exists that is acute and right together, or acute and obtuse together, or right and obtuse together, no matter how different the considerations.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and types of dividing a universal (kulli) into its particulars (juz'iyyat). It begins with an example from logic: the division of 'body' into mineral, plant, and animal, preceded by a brief division of the word into letter, verb, and noun. These examples illustrate how a universal term can be divided into mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive subcategories. The author then lists three necessary conditions for a sound division: (1) The division must be encompassing (hasir), meaning it includes all possible subcategories and excludes anything that does not belong. (2) Each part must be absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaq) than the divided term — i.e., it cannot be equal to, more general, opposite, or overlapping in a partial way. (3) Each part must be mutually exclusive (mubayin) with every other part, so that no two parts overlap in definition or reality. When these conditions are met, the division is correct (sahih) and immune to the objection of being corrupted (fasad). Next, the author categorizes division from two perspectives. The first perspective considers whether the parts are distinct in both mind and reality (real division, taqsim haqiqi) or only by mental or conceptual consideration (conceptual division, taqsim i'tibari). The second perspective considers whether the division is exhaustive based on rational analysis alone (rational division, taqsim 'aqli) or based on empirical investigation (inductive division, taqsim istiqra'i). The passage then elaborates on real division, providing two clear examples: the division of number into even and odd, and the division of angles into acute, right, and obtuse. In both cases, the parts are mutually exclusive in both thought and actual existence — no number can be both even and odd, and no angle can possess two different measures simultaneously. This real division is contrasted with conceptual division, which is not explained here but is implied to be a division based on mental distinction without external reality. The passage serves as a foundational discussion for logical classification and is critical for understanding how to correctly partition universals in the sciences.

Key Terms

التقسيم الاعتباري
التقسيم العقلي
التقسيم الاستقرائي

Division and Classification
beginner
5 min
Real and Notional Division: Key Logical Distinction
Defines two types of division with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real division from notional division and correctly classify any given division as one of the two types.

Core Idea
Real division separates things in both mind and reality; notional division separates them in mind only.

In logic, classification (taqseem) is a fundamental operation. Understanding whether a division is real or notional shapes how we interpret categories and definitions. This lesson unpacks the two types using concrete examples, including the five universals central to Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of division
The author lists real division (taqseem haqiqi) and notional division (taqseem i'tibari) as the primary categories. Note that a secondary classification (rational and inductive) is mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Extract the defining criteria of real division
Real division requires mutual exclusivity in both the mind (conceptual distinctness) and external reality (ontological exclusivity). The author emphasizes that no single external object can belong to more than one division under any consideration.
The mind assigns a unique reality (haqiqa) to each part.
3
Extract the defining criteria of notional division
Notional division requires distinctness only in the mind. In external reality, a single thing can embody multiple divisions under different considerations. The mind still assigns distinct realities, but they can coincide in one object.
4
Analyze the examples for each type
For real division: number (even/odd), angles (acute/right/obtuse), known (existent/non-existent). For notional division: the five universals (genus, species, etc.) with the example of 'animal' being both genus and species under different considerations.
The five universals example is key to understanding notional division in Islamic logic.
5
Test your understanding with a new example
Consider the division of 'Islamic legal rulings' into wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram. Is this real or notional? A single act can be wajib under one aspect (e.g., prayer) and mandub under another (e.g., supererogatory prayer)? Actually, each act has a specific ruling; but one act cannot be both wajib and haram simultaneously. This division is real because the categories are mutually exclusive in reality.
This step reinforces the criteria via a fiqh example.

٣ - المعلوم إما موجود وإما معدوم .
والأقسام في التقسيم الحقيقي تسمى أقساماً متباينة، لافتراقها في العقل والخارج معاً .
التقسيم الاعتباري :
هو ما كانت الأقسام فيه مختلفة في العقل فقط، مع إمكان وجود شيء واحد خارج الذهن تجتمع فيه حقائق الأقسام باعتبارات مختلفة، فالعقل يحدُّ لكل قسم من الأقسام حقيقة تخالف حقيقة كل ما عداه من الأقسام، إلا أن شيئاً واحداً قد يكون أحد هذه الأقسام باعتبار، وقد يكون هو القسم الآخر باعتبار آخر، وقد يكون هو القسم الثالث باعتبار ثالث، وهكذا مهما زادت الأقسام .
