1 - Real division. 2 - Notional division. And with regard to the confinement of the divided thing in the mentioned divisions, it has two types: 1 - Rational division. 2 - Inductive division. Below is an explanation of these types: Real division: It is that in which the divisions are distinct in the mind and distinct in external reality, such that the mind determines for each division a reality that contrasts with the reality of the other divisions, and by it it is distinguished from each one. Then there is not in external reality any single division in which can be realized a reality from the realities of the other divisions that are distinct from it, even under different considerations. Examples: 1 - Number is divided into even and odd. The mind judges this division and posits for each division a reality that distinguishes it from the other division, and by this reality its distinctness from its counterpart appears. It is not possible for there to exist in reality a number that is even and odd simultaneously, however much the considerations differ. 2 - The angle in geometry is divided into acute, right, and obtuse. The mind judges this division and posits for each of the three divisions a reality that distinguishes it from the other two divisions, and by this reality its distinctness from each of them appears. It is not possible for there to exist in reality an angle that is acute and right together, or acute and obtuse together, or right and obtuse together, however much the considerations differ. 3 - The known is either existent or non-existent. And the divisions in real division are called mutually exclusive divisions, because of their separation in both mind and external reality. Notional division: It is that in which the divisions differ only in the mind, with the possibility that a single thing outside the mind can combine the realities of the divisions under different considerations. The mind determines for each division a reality that differs from the reality of every other division, except that a single thing may be one of these divisions under one consideration, and may be the other division under another consideration, and may be the third division under a third consideration, and so on however many divisions there are. Examples: 1 - The universal according to logicians is divided into genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident. The mind determines for each of these divisions a reality that differs from the reality of every other division, but we observe in reality that some things that are described as species under one consideration are described as genus under another consideration, and we may find a thing that is described as genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident under different considerations. For (animal) is a universal; it is genus with respect to what is beneath it of species, such as human, horse, gazelle, cow, and snake. And it is species with respect to its inclusion under a genus above it, which is (growing) that encompasses plants and animals. Thus animal is genus under one consideration and species under another consideration. And (colored) is a universal; it is genus with respect to what is beneath it of species, such as white, red, green, and yellow, and it is species with respect to its inclusion under a genus above it, which is
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental types of division (taqseem) in logic: real division (taqseem haqiqi) and notional division (taqseem i'tibari). The author begins by listing them, then adds a secondary classification into rational (aqli) and inductive (istiqrai) division—though the explanation that follows focuses on the primary dichotomy.

Real division is characterized by mutual exclusivity in both the mind and external reality. The mind assigns to each division a distinct reality (haqiqa) that cannot be realized in the same external object under any consideration. Three examples illustrate this: numbers are either even or odd, never both; angles are acute, right, or obtuse, never a combination; and the known is either existent or non-existent. These divisions are called 'mutually exclusive divisions' (aqsam mutabayina) because they are separate in thought and in actuality.

Notional division, in contrast, has divisions that are distinct only in the mind; in external reality, a single thing can embody multiple divisions under different considerations. The mind still assigns distinct realities to each division, but they can coincide in one object. The classic example is the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams): genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident. For instance, 'animal' is a genus relative to its species (human, horse, etc.) but a species relative to the higher genus 'growing' (al-nami). Similarly, 'colored' is a genus for specific colors but a species under a higher genus (though the text cuts off before completing that example). This shows that a single universal can be both genus and species under different aspects—impossible in a real division.

The key distinction aids in classifying logical terms and avoiding confusion in definitions and arguments.

Key Terms
التقسيم الحقيقي
التقسيم الاعتباري
أقسام متباينة

Key Takeaway
Real division yields mutually exclusive parts in both mind and reality; notional division yields parts distinct only in mind, allowing a single thing to belong to multiple parts under different considerations.

Division and Classification

Real Division: Mutual Exclusivity in Mind and Reality
Author defines real division and its distinct sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given division is a real division based on the mutual exclusivity of its sections in both conceptual and real domains.

Core Idea
Real division demands that its categories be separate in both thought and existence.

In logic, not all divisions are equal. The author here defines real division (al-taqsīm al-ḥaqīqī), the strongest type, where sections are not merely conceptually distinct but also actually non-overlapping in the external world. This principle is crucial for disciplines like fiqh, where dividing legal rulings must be exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
Locate the term 'taqsīm' and its associated 'aqsām' in the passage. The passage discusses 'real division' specifically.
2
Note the condition
The text states that sections are called 'mutabāyina' because they diverge in both al-ʿaql (intellect) and al-khārij (external reality). This is the key property.
3
Apply the condition
Check if a potential division has categories that are mutually exclusive in both mind and reality. If a thing could belong to two categories simultaneously, the division is not real.
Example: 'Acts are either wajib or haram' is real because no act can be both.
4
Distinguish from conventional division
Real division contrasts with conventional (ʿurfī) or mental (ʿaqlī) division where overlap or conceptual separation alone is acceptable.
In fiqh, divisions based on customary usage may allow ambiguity, unlike real divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقسام في التقسيم الحقيقي تسمى أقساماً متباينة، لافتراقها في العقل والخارج معاً .
And the sections in the real division are called distinct sections, because they diverge in both intellect and external reality.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines real division (al-taqsīm al-ḥaqīqī). The author states that the resulting sections (aqsām) are termed 'mutabāyina' (distinct) due to their divergence (iftirāq) in two domains: the intellect (al-ʿaql) and external reality (al-khārij). This means the categories are not only conceptually distinct but also exist as separate, non-overlapping entities in the real world. For instance, in Islamic jurisprudence, dividing acts into 'wajib' and 'haram' is a real division because an act cannot be both obligatory and forbidden simultaneously, both in the mind and in divine law. The passage serves as a foundational principle for understanding logical classifications.

أقسام متباينة

Key Takeaway
Real division requires that its categories be mutually exclusive in both conceptual thought and external reality, ensuring no overlap.

Understanding Conventional Division
Definition of conventional division with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define conventional division and distinguish it from real division using the criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
One object, many mental realities: the essence of conventional division.

Not all divisions cut reality at its joints. The logician's conventional division (al-taqsīm al-iʿtibārī) splits a concept only in the mind, while the same external thing can instantiate multiple sections under different aspects. This foundational idea clarifies how logical categories relate to real objects.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence beginning with 'التقسيم الاعتباري : هو ما كانت الأقسام فيه مختلفة في العقل فقط'. This is the core definition.
2
Identify the two conditions
The definition has two parts: (a) sections differ only in the mind (العقل فقط), and (b) it is possible for one external thing to combine the realities of those sections under different considerations (باعتبارات مختلفة).
3
Understand the role of the mind
The mind assigns distinct definitions to each section, yet those definitions can all apply to a single entity when viewed from different angles.
This does not make the sections unreal; they are conceptually real.
4
Recognize the term for these sections
The passage calls such sections 'aqsām mutakhālifa' (different sections) because they diverge intellectually but can converge in external reference.

التقسيم الاعتباري : هو ما كانت الأقسام فيه مختلفة في العقل فقط، مع إمكان وجود شيء واحد خارج الذهن تجتمع فيه حقائق الأقسام باعتبارات مختلفة، فالعقل يحدُّ لكل قسم من الأقسام حقيقة تخالف حقيقة كل ما عداه من الأقسام، إلا أن شيئاً واحداً قد يكون أحد هذه الأقسام باعتبار، وقد يكون هو القسم الآخر باعتبار آخر، وقد يكون هو القسم الثالث باعتبار ثالث، وهكذا مهما زادت الأقسام . الأمثلة : ١ - الكلي عند المناطقة ينقسم إلى جنس، ونوع، وفصل، وخاصة، وعرض عام . فالعقل يحدُّ لكل قسم من هذه الأقسام حقيقةً تخالف حقيقة كل قسم من الأقسام الأخرى، ولكننا نلاحظ في الواقع أن بعض ما ينطبق عليه أنه نوع باعتبار، ينطبق عليه أنه جنس باعتبار آخر، وقد نجد شيئاً ينطبق عليه أنه جنس ونوع وفصل وخاصة وعرض عام باعتبارات مختلفات . فـ (الحيوان) كلي هو جنس بالنظر إلى ما تحته من أنواع منها: الإنسان والفرس والغزال والبقرة والثعبان . وهو نوع بالنظر إلى اندراجه في جنس فوقه هو (النامي) الذي يعمُّ النبات والحيوان، إذن فالحيوان جنس باعتبار ونوع باعتبار آخر . و (المُلَوَّن) كليّ هو جنس بالنظر إلى ما تحته من أنواع منها: الأبيض والأحمر والأخضر والأصفر، وهو نوع بالنظر إلى اندراجه في جنس فوقه هو (المكيّف)، الذي يعمُّ الملون والمشموم والملموس وغير ذلك. وهو فصل بالنظر إلى كونه يفصل نوع الكثيف عن نوع اللطيف اللذين يشملهما جنس هو (الجسم)، فنقول في تعريف الكثيف هو جسم ملون، وهكذا فقد يكون خاصة باعتبار رابع، وعرضاً عاماً باعتبار خامس. ٢ - الجهات تنقسم إلى ستة أقسام: فوق وتحت وأمام وخلف وذات اليمين وذات الشمال. فالعقل يحدُّ لكل قسم من هذه الأقسام حقيقة تخالف حقيقة كل قسم من الأقسام الأخرى، ولكننا نلاحظ في الواقع أن بعض ما ينطبق عليه أنه فوق باعتبار هو تحت باعتبار آخر، وأمام باعتبار ثالث، وخلف باعتبار رابع، وعن يمين باعتبار خامس، وعن الشمال باعتبار سادس. فالكعبة مثلاً هي فوق الأرض وتحت السماء وأمام المتجه إليها، وخلف المستدبر لها، وعن يمين الواقف إلى جهة الميزاب متجهاً إلى الشرق، وعن يسار الواقف بين الركنين اليمانيين متجهاً إلى الشرق أيضاً، فاجتمعت هذه الأقسام الستة كلُّها في شيء واحد باعتبارات مختلفات. والأقسام في التقسيم الاعتباري تسمى أقساماً متخالفة، لافتراقها في العقل دون الخارج، إذ قد تجتمع في الماصدق.
Conventional division: It is that in which the sections are different only in the mind, with the possibility of a single thing outside the mind in which the realities of the sections come together under different considerations. So the mind defines for each section a reality that differs from the reality of every other section, except that a single thing may be one of these sections under one consideration, and may be that other section under another consideration, and may be the third section under a third consideration, and so on, no matter how many sections increase. Examples: 1 - The universal according to the logicians is divided into genus, species, difference, property, and common accident. The mind defines for each of these sections a reality that differs from the reality of every other section, but we observe in actuality that some of what applies to it as a species under one consideration applies to it as a genus under another consideration, and we may find something that applies to it as genus, species, difference, property, and common accident under different considerations. For (animal) is a universal that is a genus with respect to what is below it of species, including human, horse, gazelle, cow, and snake. And it is a species with respect to its inclusion under a genus above it, which is (growing being) that encompasses plants and animals. So animal is a genus under one consideration and a species under another consideration. And (colored) is a universal that is a genus with respect to its species such as white, red, green, and yellow; and it is a species with respect to its inclusion under a genus above it, which is (qualified being) that encompasses colored, smelled, touched, and others. And it is a difference with respect to its distinguishing the dense type from the subtle type, both of which are included under a genus that is (body). So we say in the definition of the dense: it is a colored body. And thus it may be a property under a fourth consideration, and a common accident under a fifth. 2 - The directions are divided into six sections: above, below, front, back, right, and left. The mind defines for each of these sections a reality that differs from the reality of every other section, but we observe in actuality that some of what applies to it as above under one consideration is below under another consideration, front under a third, back under a fourth, right under a fifth, and left under a sixth. For the Kaaba, for example, is above the ground and below the sky, front for the one facing it, back for the one turning away from it, to the right of the one standing towards the waterspout facing east, and to the left of the one standing between the two Yemeni corners also facing east. So all these six sections come together in one thing under different considerations. And the sections in conventional division are called different (mutakhālifa) sections, because they diverge only in the mind, not in external reality, as they may come together in extension.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition and illustration of conventional division (al-taqsīm al-iʿtibārī). The author begins by defining it: it is division where the parts differ only intellectually (al-ʿaql), not necessarily in external existence. A single real entity can encompass the realities of multiple parts under different aspects (iʿtibārāt). The mind assigns distinct definitions to each part, but actual things can be classified in multiple ways. The first example involves the five universals (kulliyāt) — genus, species, difference, property, common accident. Each has a distinct definition, yet a single thing can be all five depending on perspective. For instance, 'animal' is a genus relative to its species (human, horse, etc.) and a species relative to the higher genus 'growing being' (nāmī). Similarly, 'colored' (mulawwan) is a genus for colors, a species for 'qualified being' (mukayyaf), and a difference when distinguishing dense and subtle bodies. So the same concept can take multiple roles. The second example uses the six directions (jihāt). While each direction is conceptually distinct, a single object like the Kaaba can be above, below, front, back, right, and left simultaneously depending on the observer's relation. The author sums up by calling these sections 'mutakhālifa' (different), diverging only in the mind, not in reality. This classification is essential for understanding that multiple logical categories can be applied to one subject without contradiction.

Key Terms
التقسيم الاعتباري
أقسام متخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes conventional division from real division?
A) In conventional division, the sections cannot exist in the same external object.
B) In conventional division, the sections differ only in the mind with possible external convergence.
C) Conventional division only applies to universals.
D) Conventional division is arbitrary and has no logical basis.

Key Takeaway
Conventional division allows multiple mental categories to converge on one real thing, a crucial tool for nuanced logical and legal analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a real entity (e.g., a person) and list at least three different logical or legal categories it can fall under, showing how it can be all of them under different considerations.
?
Construct your own example of conventional division from fiqh (e.g., the categories of water: tahir, mutahhir, najis) and explain how one water sample can be all three under different aspects.

Division and Classification

Conventional Division Applied: The Five Universals
Example of five universals illustrating conventional division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how a single universal can be classified as genus, species, difference, property, or common accident under different considerations, using the examples from the passage.

Core Idea
One universal, five logical hats: perspective determines category.

The passage illustrates conventional division with the five universals—genus, species, difference, property, and common accident. Although each universal has a distinct definition, a single concept like 'animal' or 'colored' can play all five roles when considered relative to different hierarchies. This lesson unpacks that analysis.

Guided Path
1
List the five universals
From the passage: جنس, نوع, فصل, خاصة, عرض عام. Recall their definitions: genus (above species), species (below genus), difference (distinguishing within genus), property (exclusive accident), common accident (shared accident).
2
Analyze the first example: 'animal'
The passage says 'animal' is a genus relative to its species (human, horse, etc.) and a species relative to the genus 'growing being' (النامي). So it fulfills two roles.
Consider what makes it genus vs species: the perspective of above or below.
3
Analyze the second example: 'colored'
The passage shows 'colored' (الملون) as a genus for colors, a species under 'qualified being' (المكيف), and a difference when distinguishing dense bodies from subtle ones. It can further be a property and common accident in other contexts.
Notice how the same universal takes three roles simultaneously in the example.
4
Generalize the pattern
Any universal can be all five universals when viewed from different angles. This is a perfect example of conventional division: the sections (the five types) are mentally distinct, yet a single universal instantiates all of them.

Division and Classification

Understanding Rational Division
Author defines rational division and gives three examples related to numbers and being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a rational division and distinguish it from empirical or conventional divisions using the criterion of intellectual necessity.

Core Idea
Rational division yields exhaustive parts by intellectual necessity alone.

In logic and Islamic sciences, divisions are essential for classification. Rational division is a special type where the mind sees the parts as exhaustive without needing external evidence. This lesson unpacks the definition and examples from the text, equipping you to recognize such divisions in Kalam, Fiqh, and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the definition: 'it is that in which the intellect judges the exhaustive inclusion of the divided whole in its sections merely by considering the division.' Note the key phrases: 'intellect judges' and 'merely by considering the division' – no empirical check is needed.
2
Identify the condition of exhaustiveness
The term 'inḥiṣār' (exhaustive inclusion) means the division covers all possible parts without remainder. In rational division, this is known a priori.
3
Analyze the first example
Numbers are either even or odd. Reason sees that any number belongs to one of these two, and no third option exists. This is self-evident.
4
Analyze the second example
The known (al-maʿlūm) is either existent or non‑existent. The intellect immediately knows there is no third state for what is known.
This example is used in Kalam to discuss the categories of being.
5
Analyze the third example
Two numbers are either equal, or one is greater, or one is smaller. Reason sees these three relations as exhaustive for any two numbers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم العقلي:
هو ما يجزم العقل فيه بانحصار المَقْسِم في أقسامه بمجرد النظر في القسمة.
الأمثلة:
١ - العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، فالعقل يجزم بأن العدد لا يحتمل قسماً ثالثاً غيرهما.
٢ - المعلوم إما موجود وإما معدوم، فالعقل يجزم بأن المعلوم لا يحتمل قسماً ثالثاً غير الموجود والمعدوم.
٣ - العددان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكبر أو أصغر من الآخر.
Rational division: It is that in which the intellect judges the exhaustive inclusion of the divided whole in its sections merely by considering the division. Examples: 1 - The number is either even or odd; the intellect judges that the number admits no third part besides them. 2 - The known is either existent or non-existent; the intellect judges that the known admits no third part besides the existent and the non-existent. 3 - Two numbers are either equal or one is greater or smaller than the other.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines rational division (al-taqsīm al-ʿaqlī). The author states that it is a division where the intellect (al-ʿaql) decisively judges that the whole (al-maqsim) is exhausted by its sections simply by reflecting on the division itself, without requiring empirical verification. Three examples are provided. First, numbers are either even (zawj) or odd (fard); the intellect immediately knows there is no third category. Second, the known (al-maʿlūm) is either existent (mawjūd) or non-existent (maʿdūm); again no third possibility. Third, two numbers are either equal (mutasāwiyān) or one is greater (akbar) or smaller (aṣghar). These cases are self-evident to reason and do not depend on observation. This type of division contrasts with real and conventional divisions, as it relies solely on rational necessity. It is akin to a priori classifications in logic, often used in demonstrative sciences.

Key Terms
التقسيم العقلي
مَقْسِم
انحصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of rational division?
A) The division of animal into rational and irrational.
B) The division of numbers into prime and composite.
C) The division of the known into existent and non-existent.
D) The division of acts into obligatory and forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Rational division is recognized by the intellect's immediate judgment of exhaustiveness without empirical support, as seen in the examples of numbers and being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the division of obligatory acts (wajib) in fiqh into 'aynī and kifā'ī. Is this a rational division? Why or why not? (Hint: Does the intellect see exhaustiveness without empirical evidence?)

Division and Classification

Conceptual Division and Its Aspectual Parts
Author notes the naming of parts in conceptual division and explains their mental basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the parts of conceptual division are called 'mutakhaalifa' and distinguish conceptual division from real division using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
Conceptual parts diverge in the mind but can converge in one referent.

In this lesson, we explore a key distinction in Islamic logic: conceptual division (al-taqsim al-i'tibari). Unlike real divisions that separate objects, conceptual division separates mental aspects. The author teaches us that its parts are termed 'different in aspect' (mutakhaalifa) precisely because they are distinct only in thought, not in external reality. This subtle point is crucial for correctly analyzing definitions and classifications in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Find what the parts of conceptual division are called and the reason given. The text says they are called 'aqsam mutakhaalifa' (different in aspect) because they are separate in the mind, not in external reality.
2
Understand 'different in mind' vs. 'different in reality'
Contrast mental separation (laftiraq fi al-'aql) with external separation (dun al-kharij). The parts are not mutually exclusive in the real world; they are aspects of a single thing.
For example, the six directions are conceptually separate but all apply to one body.
3
Note the possibility of combination
Read the final clause: 'since they may come together in the referent (masdaq).' This means multiple conceptual aspects can coexist in the same external object.
Think of a pen: it is both 'long' and 'blue' – these are conceptually different but true of the same pen.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the attributes of Allah (sifat) in kalam: they are conceptually distinct (knowledge, power, will) but all refer to one divine essence. This illustrates conceptual division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقسام في التقسيم الاعتباري تسمى أقساماً متخالفة، لافتراقها في العقل دون الخارج، إذ قد تجتمع في الماصدق.
And the parts in conceptual division are called 'different/aspectual divisions' (aqsaam mutakhaalifa), because they are separate in the mind not in external reality, since they may come together in the referent.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines conceptual division (taqsim i'tibari). It explains that the division is mental, based on considerations. The resulting parts are termed 'mutakhaalifa' (differing in aspect) because they are distinct only conceptually; in external reality, they can combine in a single object. For example, the six directions are conceptually separate but can all apply to one thing. This contrasts with real divisions where parts exclude each other in external existence.

Key Terms
تقسيم اعتباري
متخالفة
ماصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They are based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The parts of conceptual division are mentally distinct but may share a single referent in external reality.

Division and Classification

Understanding Rational Division: Definition and Examples
Author defines rational division and provides three examples, noting its standard formula uses disjunction between affirmation and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a division is rational or empirical, and distinguish between using the true contradictory (naqid) and its equivalent (musawi al-naqid).

Core Idea
Rational division yields certainty because reason itself confirms no third possibility.

In this passage, the author defines rational division (al-taqsim al-'aqli) as a division whose exhaustiveness is known by reason alone, without empirical investigation. He provides three clear examples and then explains the standard formula: the division is usually expressed as a disjunction between a predicate and its negation. However, sometimes a term equivalent to the negation is used instead. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing valid divisions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition
Locate the definition of rational division in the Arabic. Identify the key phrase 'بمجرد النظر في القسمة' which indicates the mind's immediate certainty.
2
Examine the examples
Read the three examples. For each, confirm that the mind can see there is no third possibility without looking outward. This is the hallmark of rational division.
Example 1: number even/odd; Example 2: known existent/non-existent; Example 3: two numbers equal/greater/smaller.
3
Identify the disjunction formula
Note that the standard formula uses the contradictory (naqid): e.g., 'إما زوج وإما لا'. Understand why negation is the true contradictory because it exactly denies the predicate.
The logical contradictory always covers all possibilities.
4
Distinguish the equivalent term
See how the author shows that sometimes an equivalent term (مساوي النقيض) like 'فرد' is used instead of 'لا'. Recognize that 'فرد' is not the logical contradictory but it coextends with it (every number is either even or odd, and if not even, it is odd).
'Odd' is musawi al-naqid because it is materially equivalent to 'not even'.
5
Verify exhaustiveness
For any division, check whether reason alone can guarantee no other part. If empirical observation is needed, it is not rational division.
E.g., dividing animals into species requires empirical observation, so it is not rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم العقلي: هو ما يجزم العقل فيه بانحصار المَقْسِم في أقسامه بمجرد النظر في القسمة. الأمثلة: ١ - العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، فالعقل يجزم بأن العدد لا يحتمل قسماً ثالثاً غيرهما. ٢ - المعلوم إما موجود وإما معدوم، فالعقل يجزم بأن المعلوم لا يحتمل قسماً ثالثاً غير الموجود والمعدوم. ٣ - العددان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكبر أو أصغر من الآخر. والأصل في التقسيم العقلي أن يؤتى به على صيغة الترديد بين الإثبات والنفي، مثل: العدد إما زوج وإما لا، باعتبار أن النفي هو نقيض الإثبات، ولكن قد يؤتى بما يساوي النقيض فيقال مثلاً : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، فالفرد في الحقيقة ليس هو النقيض، ولكنه مساوي النقيض .
Rational division: It is that in which the mind certifies the exhaustive enumeration of the divided term into its parts merely by considering the division. Examples: 1. Number is either even or odd; the mind certifies that number does not admit a third division other than them. 2. The known is either existent or non-existent; the mind certifies that the known does not admit a third division other than existent and non-existent. 3. Two numbers are either equal or one is greater or smaller than the other. The principle in rational division is that it is presented in the form of disjunction between affirmation and negation, like: number is either even or not, considering that negation is the contradictory of affirmation. But it may be presented with what is equivalent to the contradictory, thus one says e.g.: number is either even or odd; odd in reality is not the contradictory, but it is equivalent to the contradictory.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines rational division (taqsim 'aqli) which is based on pure reason, without need for empirical investigation. The mind, simply by considering the concept, knows that the division is exhaustive and that no other part exists. Three examples are given: (1) numbers are either even or odd; (2) the known is either existent or non-existent; (3) two numbers are either equal, or one is greater or smaller. The text then notes the standard principle: the division is usually expressed as a disjunction between the affirmation of a predicate and its negation (e.g., 'either even or not even'), because negation is the true contradictory. However, sometimes an equivalent term is used instead of pure negation, like using 'odd' instead of 'not even'. 'Odd' is not the logical contradictory of 'even' but is materially equivalent to it. This shows that rational divisions are certain because they are derived from the nature of the concept itself.

Key Terms
تقسيم عقلي
نقيض
مساوي النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The word is noun, verb, or particle.

C) Planets of the solar system are nine in number.
D) Body is either mineral, plant, or animal.

Key Takeaway
Rational division is a powerful tool that gives certain, exhaustive knowledge through reason alone, provided its form uses either the true contradictory or a coextensive equivalent.

Division and Classification
intermediate

Inductive Division: Definition and How It Works
Author defines inductive division and gives examples from Arabic grammar and astronomy, distinguishing it from rational division by its reliance on empirical survey.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish inductive division from rational division and identify the role of empirical observation in establishing exhaustive enumeration.

Core Idea
Inductive division rests on observation, not pure reason; its exhaustiveness is provisional.

Not every exhaustive division is based on a priori logical necessity. Some divisions depend entirely on empirical investigation. This lesson introduces you to inductive division (al-taqsim al-istiqra'i), a classification that relies on sound induction from external reality rather than the mind's abstract judgment. Understanding this distinction is critical for evaluating classifications in fields like linguistics, jurisprudence, and the natural sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of inductive division
Read the definition in the Arabic passage: 'Adductive division is that in which the mind, by itself, does not prevent the existence of another division...' The key is that reason alone cannot rule out other categories; only empirical observation can establish exhaustiveness.
Contrast this with rational division, where the mind alone can rule out extra categories.
2
Identify the source of exhaustiveness
Notice that the exhaustiveness of an inductive division is 'dependent on what the induction of reality in external existence establishes.' The mind's judgment is secondary to observation.
The author uses the phrase 'istiqra' sahih' (sound induction) to emphasize completeness of survey.
3
Examine the first example (Arabic grammar)
The division of word (kalima) into noun, verb, and particle is inductive because the abstract mind does not forbid a fourth part; only through surveying Arabic speech do grammarians confirm these three parts.
This example is foundational in Arabic morphology (sarf) and syntax (nahw).
4
Examine the second example (astronomy)
The division of planets (as known at the time) into nine is also inductive. The mind alone does not preclude additional planets; observation confirms the nine. The text notes a possible tenth that disintegrated.
This illustrates that inductive divisions can be revised with new evidence.
5
Apply the principle about presentation form
The author states that inductive division should not be expressed in the 'disjunctive form' (either X or not X) because that form implies rational certainty. Instead, inductive divisions simply list the observed parts.
This structural rule helps distinguish inductive from rational divisions in scholarly writings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الاستقرائي : هو ما لا يمنع العقل فيه بمجرده وجود قسم آخر غير ما ورد في التقسيم، لكنه إذا نظر إلى الأقسام الموجودة فعلاً في الخارج، بناء على ما أثبته الاستقراء الصحيح، فإنه يحكم بانتفاء وجود أي قسم آخر، فحكم العقل بانحصار المقسم في أقسامه هنا، تابع لما يثبته استقراء الواقع في الوجود الخارجي، وليس مستنداً إلى نظرته المجردة عن ملاحظة الوجود الخارجي . الأمثلة : ١ - الكلمة تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : اسم وفعل وحرف . فالعقل المجرد لا يمنع من وجود قسم رابع، إلا أن استقراء واقع الكلام العربي في الوجود الخارجي، أثبت انحصار الكلمة في هذه الأقسام الثلاثة . ٢ - الكواكب السيارة التابعة للشمس تسعة وهي : عطارد، فالزهرة، فالأرض، فالمريخ، فالمشتري، فزحل، فأورانس، فنبتون، فبلوتو. والعقل لا يمنع من وجود كوكب أو كواكب أخرى، إلا أن استقراء واقع التوابع للشمس في الوجود الخارجي أثبت انحصارها في هذه الكواكب التسع، ويضاف إليها كوكب عاشر بين المريخ والمشتري، إلا أنه انفجر وغدا كتلاً صغيرة متناثرة. والأصل في التقسيم الاستقرائي أن لا يؤتى به على صيغة الترديد بين الإثبات والنفي، تخصيصاً لهذه الصيغة بالتقسيم العقلي .
Inductive division: It is that in which the mind, by itself, does not prevent the existence of another division other than what is mentioned in the division. However, when it looks at the divisions actually existing in external reality, based on what sound induction has established, then it judges the non-existence of any other division. So the mind's judgment of the exhaustive enumeration of the divided term into its parts here is dependent on what the induction of reality in external existence establishes, and is not based on the mind's abstract consideration without observing external existence. Examples: 1. The word is divided into three parts: noun, verb, and particle. The abstract mind does not prevent the existence of a fourth part, but the induction of the reality of Arabic speech in external existence established the exhaustive enumeration of the word into these three parts. 2. The planets orbiting the sun are nine: Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, Neptune, and Pluto. The mind does not prevent the existence of another planet or planets, but the induction of the reality of the sun's satellites in external existence established their exhaustive enumeration into these nine planets, and a tenth planet is added between Mars and Jupiter, but it exploded and became scattered small masses. The principle in inductive division is that it is not presented in the form of disjunction between affirmation and negation, to reserve that form for rational division.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines inductive division (taqsim istiqra'i), which relies on empirical investigation. Unlike rational division, the mind alone cannot rule out other possibilities; only after thorough observation does it accept exhaustiveness. Two examples are given: (1) the parts of speech in Arabic are limited to noun, verb, and particle based on linguistic induction; (2) the nine planets of the solar system (as known at the time) are established by astronomical observation, with a note about a possible tenth that disintegrated. The text states the principle: inductive division should not be expressed in the disjunctive form (either X or not X) because that would imply rational certainty; instead, it should simply list the observed parts. This preserves the distinction between rational and inductive divisions.

Key Terms
تقسيم استقرائي
استقراء صحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Inductive division is an empirical classification whose exhaustiveness is based on sound induction from external reality, not on a priori logical necessity.
